This material was produced from a microfilm copy of the original document. While 
the most advanced technological means to photograph and reproduce this document 
have been used, the quality is heavily dependent upon the quality of the original 
submitted.
The following explanation of techniques is provided to help you understand 
markings or patterns which may appear on this reproduction.
1.The sign or "target" for pages apparently lacking from the document 
photographed is "Missing Page(s)". If it was possible to obtain the missing 
page(s) or section, they are spliced into the film along with adjacent pages. 
This may have necessitated cutting thru an image and duplicating adjacent 
pages to insure you complete continuity.
2. When an image on the film is obliterated with a large round black mark, it 
is an indication that the photographer suspected that the copy may have 
moved during exposure and thus cause a blurred image. You will find a 
good image of the page in the adjacent frame.
3. When a map, drawing or chart, etc., was part of the material being 
photographed

a

method 
in

left hand corner of a large sheet and to continue photoing from left to 
right in equal sections with a small overlap. If necessary, sectioning is 
continued again — beginning beiow the first row and continuing on untii 
complete.
4. The majority of users indicate that the textual content is of greatest value, 
however, a somewhat higher quality reproduction could be made from 
"photographs" if essential to the understanding of the dissertation. Silver 
prints of "photographs" may be ordered at additional charge by writing 
the Order Department, giving the catalog number, title, author and 
specific pages you wish reproduced.

ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS
The research on Easter Island that forms the "basis of this 
discussion was made possible "by the assistance and guidance of William 
Mulloy, director of an expedition financed "by the International Fund 
for Monuments of New York and the Republic of Chile. Gonzalo Figueroa, 
whose indispensible aid will not oe soon forgotten, was kind enough to 
allow me to use field notes from his preliminary excavations into one 
of the Easter Island ahu.
The hard work and friendship of the Easter Islanders who 
worked on the excavations and restoration, especially three foremen, 
Jerman Hotu, Juan Haoa, and Martin Rapu, as well as many other 
islanders who contributed extra time and effort towards the completion 
of our project, are appreciated not only by myself, but will be by 
everyone who is able to visit the restored ceremonial complex of Tahai.
Others, Herb Pownall, who provided a wide range of expert 
photographic knowledge; Pat McCoy, who aided with some of the mapping; 
Mario Arevalo P., who accomplished most of the field mapping and 
located sites in the survey; and Rafael Rapu, a photographic assistant, 
completed essential technical activities well beyond what was necessary.
Finally I should like to thank Arden R. King, teacher and 
advisor, for continual encouragement, and especially my wife Gabriella 
for years of support; and encouragement.

CHAPTER I
THE STUDY OF POLYNESIAN RELIGIOUS CENTERS
The Polynesian Marae Complex 
The religious structures called marae which were built by the 
inhabitants of the East Polynesian islands occupy a position of con­
siderable interest in all the descriptions and analyses of Polynesian 
cultures since the time of the earliest European explorers in the 
Pacific. The impressive size and the permanent character of these 
stone monuments contribute to their ethnographic and archaeological 
importance. 
Serious comparative study of such remains began with 
Kenneth P. Emory's surveys and discussions in the 1930's and 19^0's 
(19335 193^> 19^3} 19^7) and continues with his more recent publica­
tion (1970). Archaeological researchers of Polynesia are now faced 
with the need to synthesize recently accumulated field data in order 
to clarify what is known of the marae type religious centers.
The position of Easter Island, the easternmost extension of 
Polynesia, is of great importance to this synthesis because the most 
anthropological research has been focused on religious ceremonial cen­
ters here. Easter Island studies are significant because of the early 
settlement of the island during the migration of Polynesians into East 
Polynesia. Because of its isolation, the island was probably not con­
tacted again after settlement until European exploration. That the
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 11 ===

r
2n
culture of Easter was basically Polynesian at the time of European con­
tact is indicated by Captain James Cook in 177^-
In Colour, Features, and Language they [the Easter Islanders ] 
bear such affinity to the people of the more Western isles that 
no one will doubt but that they have had the same origin, it is 
extraordinary that the Nation should have spread themselves over 
all the isles of this Vast Ocean from New Zealand to this Island 
which is almost a fourth part of the circumference of the Globe 
. . . (Beaglehole 1961:35^-) •
The earliest travelers recognized that the religious monuments called 
ahu on Easter Island were similar to marae on other East Polynesian 
islands. I obtained new information about the nature and antiquity of 
Easter Island ahu through archaeological research in 1968, which poses 
some problems in relating these data to what is known of marae develop­
ment in other parts of Polynesia. The discovery of a large ahu with 
cut stone architecture, dated at A.D. 690, is especially important for 
understanding Easter Island prehistory and its cult ural relationships. 
The basic problem that arises is one of explaining the early date, for 
it is hard to account for the development of such complex religious 
architecture this early. The proper explanation and interpretation of 
this date would help to determine to what extent the cultural develop­
ments on Easter Island, which approach in some features the developmen­
tal stage generally referred to as a civilization (Mulloy 1968), are 
the result of independent invention. I am interested in determining 
what hypothetical pattern of Polynesian settlement and diffusion best 
explains the emerging picture of Easter Island developments and of sim­
ilar transformations in religious architecture elsewhere in Polynesia.

J

ii
Theoretical and Methodol-gical Orientation
The theoretical and methodological orientation brought to bear
on the ahu and marae problem and on related questions is primarily a 
comparative one which attempts to utilize all pertinent data on 
Polynesian cultural systems. This comparative approach, as seen below, 
is supplemented by projection of observed behavior in historic times 
into the prehistoric context. The synthesis of ahu and. marae architec­
ture presented here concentrates initially on outlining the developmen­
tal aspects of ahu on Easter Island. The problem is approached from 
two viewpoints: l) from the updated archaeological record, and 2) from
projections of ethnographic description back into protohistoric. and 
prehistoric time periods on Easter Island.
The interpretation of archaeological data yields some informa­
tion concerning ceremonies and participation at ahu that is needed to 
understand the developmental sequence. I am seeking not only an out­
line of this developmental sequence, but also a definition of the cul­
tural context. The definition of the major features of ahu change and 
context on Easter Island is enhanced by comparative study of ethno­
graphic and archaeological data from other East Poljnaesian islands such 
as the Marquesas, the Society Islands, and Hawaii. This synthesis also 
aims at a reconstruction, based on the kinds of evidence mentioned 
above, of the proto-East Polynesian marae complex and at a delineation 
of the developmental process of structural and functional changes in 
East Polynesian marae. The parallel development observed in East 
Polynesian islands is recognized as a result of: l) a common cultural
Easter Island Prehistory and Comparative Studies
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 13 ===

r 
n
base, 2) similar macro-ecological conditions, 3) similar growth and 
expansion of population, and H) in some cases, continued contact after 
initial settlement.
Significantly more data on the prehistory of Easter Island are 
available now than there were at the time of the conclusions drawn 
after the research of the Norwegian Expedition of 1955-56 (Heyerdahl 
and Ferdon, eds. 1961, 1965), and considerably more knowledge about 
Easter Island ahu than there was at the time Metraux discussed them 
(19^0). These two sources provide the major body of data and inter­
pretations regarding Easter Island ahu. Further excavations conducted 
by Mulloy between i960 and 1972 on ahu structures considerably 
increase understanding of architectural changes. The author's work, 
like the earlier work of Mulloy, was primarily concerned with ahu.
We have had very few new historical or ethnographic data since 
the earlier writings, but recent archaeological materials offer, as an 
initial perspective, a much clearer picture of the prehistory of those 
islands which were possibly the homeland of the original settlers of 
Easter Island. The Marquesas, Mangareva, the Tuamotus, Tahiti and the 
entire Society Islands group, and the early occupation of Samoa and 
Tonga in West Polynesia, have been the subject of considerable archae­
ological research since 1961. For example, Metraux had only one major 
source for Marquesan archaeology, Linton's archaeological survey and 
description of material culture which dealt only with surface remains 
(Linton 1925)• The first subsurface archaeology in the Marquesas was 
undertaken by Suggs in 1956 (Suggs 1961). The Marquesas have been 
stated by Metraux (19^0:^17), Emory (19^-3) , and more recently Sinoto 
(1968) and Emory (1972) to be the probable homeland of the Easter
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 14 ===

r 
fi
Island settlers. Research conducted by a number of investigators in 
the other islands provides additional data for this synthesis.
A more complete knowledge of the Easter Island assemblages of 
adzes, fishhooks, other fishing equipment, and especially of the struc­
tural components of the ahu, will lead to more insightful and realistic 
comparisons in the examination of cultural contacts and influences on 
Easter Island culture. Understanding the probable influences on and 
contacts with Easter Island is a prerequisite for understanding the 
function and operation of prehistoric ahu and for providing more valid 
use of the comparative data. This synthesis includes an analysis of 
what the archaeological research to date has added to our knowledge of 
the operation and use of the ahu.
Ethnographic Projection
The second perspective developed here for viewing the cultural 
context of Easter Island ahu is derived from a reconstructed ethno­
graphic description of Easter Island culture and its projection back in 
time. Thus this paper draws together data from the archaeological rec­
ord and from ethnographic resources. The limitations of the ethno­
graphic data from Easter Island have made this two-directional approach 
the most fruitful. The further restrictions on the use of ethnographic 
data for this sort of projection are recognized and the problems noted 
by Suggs (i960) in the use of tradtional histories and genealogies 
have been considered. Suggs (1960:771) states, however:
Traditions are apparently most valuable when they are thought 
of as providing a body of general data which can be used in a 
positive fashion, as a kind of 'paleo-ethnology1 for the culture 
in question, to aid in the interpretation of the cold facts and 
sequences of archaeology and to facilitate the ultimate recon­
struction of the subject culture's prehistory.
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 15 ===

The description of the cultural context of ahu and their use in pre­
historic and protohistoric times does, of course, depend directly upon 
ethnographic analogy, as does most archaeological interpretation.
Since one of my major goals is to provide a view of the cultural con­
text of the ceremonial centers of the ahu or marae type throughout 
East Polynesia during the prehistoric and protohistoric periods, the 
somewhat fuller ethnographic records from other islands such as the 
Marquesas, Tahiti and Hawaii, in regard to marae construction and use, 
will be utilized to elucidate early ceremonial complexes. Green 
(1967:111) emphasized the need for a reconstructed protohistoric base­
line to facilitate prehistoric interpretation.
According to Ascher, the establishment of conditions of rele­
vance for archaeological arguments from analogy should take the fol­
lowing fom: l) relevance can be established by demonstration or
accepting as demonstrated that there is a historical continuity 
between the archaeologically observed unit and the ethnographically 
cited society or social unit, and 2) in the absence of justification 
on the basis of demonstrated historical continuity, relevance could be 
justified by seeking analogies in cultures which manipulate similar 
environments in similar ways. The latter is the "new analogy" 
described by Ascher (1961:319-32^-).
Historical continuity between the archaeological society and 
the ethnographic society in the case of Easter Island is clear partic­
ularly for the Middle and Late periods distinguished by Smith (1961a) 
although Heyerdhal (1952, 1958) considered the large statues associ­
ated with the Middle Period ahu to be the work of white-skinned, red- 
haired people ("long ears") from Peru. The archaeological research of
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 16 ===

r 
71
Mulloy, Smith, and the writer provides evidence for ti : continuity of 
culture from the Early to the Middle Period, and thus indicates con­
tinuous occupation hy Polynesians. The probability of replacement of 
an earlier population by a later one is also greatly reduced by the 
isolation of this island (see Emory 1970:91). The historical relation­
ships among the inhabitants of the several East Polynesian islands can 
be demonstrated culturally, linguistically, biologically, and, in gen­
eral, archaeologically. All of the East Polynesian cultures under con­
sideration here are currently thought to have stemmed from a common 
cultural base no older than 2500 years, with the differentation of 
East Polynesian societies occurring considerably later.
The fundamental unity within the Polynesian family of languages, 
marked by an even greater unity within the islands of West 
Polynesia and within those of Eastern Polynesia, means a common 
origin for the whole family and secondary common origins for the 
West and East divisions (Emory 1963:84).
We are of course also dealing with cultures manipulating simi­
lar environments in similar ways. What remains to be explained by the 
projection of a complex of behavior associated with ahu use back through 
time are the changes in the physical structure and context of the cere­
monial structure itself seen in the archaeological record of approxi­
mately 1500 years for Easter Island.
Chang (1967:229) also refers to the importance of ethnographic 
knowledge for archaeological reconstruction "where there is demonstrable 
cultural continuity from the prehistoric to the ethnographic, as in the 
New World as well as in the Pacific Islands . . . ." Attempts to uti­
lize such ethnographic materials in European folk-culture studies are 
well illustrated in GJessing (1955) and Clark (l95l)• Chang (1967:230) 
has maintained that all archaeological interpretation is analogy:
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 17 ===

r
81
"indeed, in a broad sense, archaeological reconstruction i_s analogy, 
with or without explicit ethnological recourse." Binford (1967b:235) 
takes exception to this statement, stressing that the inclusion of an 
analogical component in archaeological arguments does not make the 
argument an analogy. He emphasized the need for logico-deductive argu­
ments, to be distinguished from argument by analogy, in archaeology 
(1967a, 1967b), but further demonstration of the relevance of analogi­
cal arguments for interpretation of and elaboration upon archaeological 
remains is well illustrated in his examination of the cultural context 
of smudge pits in the midwest part of the United States (Binford 1967a). 
Establishing relevance for Binford requires the following steps:
A . The Analogy
1. The recognition and demonstration of formal positive 
analogy between a class of archaeologically observed 
phenomena and a class of ethnographically observed 
phenomena (Binford 1967a:9).
Here the class of observed phenomena is that of the ceremonial struc­
ture referred to as an ahu in the ethnographic case. The recognition 
of the positive analogy came as early as 1889 with the description of 
ahu recorded by William J. Thomson, who saw earlier ahu constructions 
beneath later rebuildings. The demonstration of the positive analogy 
will be made in greater detail in the descriptions of actual excavated 
structures.
2. A consideration of the positive analogy between the 
spatial distribution of the facility as documented 
archaeologically and ethnographically, and the 
observation that . . . the known distribution show 
a strong positive analogy (Binford 1967a:9).
The spatial distributions of the prehistoric, protohistoric, and his­
toric ahu structures, as far as available evidence indicates, coincide 
to an overwhelming extent, particularly in the more numerous Middle and
L

r
Late Period structures. The details of this distribution will be
spelled out more clearly below. At this point it will suffice to say
that Early, Middle, and Later Period ahu structures were generally
built on the same location, and involved primarily a remodeling of the
earlier structures.
3. A consideration of the degree to which it would be 
reasonable to expect a continuity between the archae­
ologically and ethnographically known cases, for 
example, the dating of the archaeologically known 
materials as reasonably viewed as cases of histori­
cal priority to the ethnographic data (Binford
1967a:9).
The stratigraphic position of ahu in the three major time 
periods for Easter Island prehistory, as well as a number of radiocar­
bon dates, very clearly indicates the temporal relationships among them. 
Binford maintains that the demonstration of the three steps above will 
allow postulation of analogous use.
B. The Postulate
The behavorial context of the use of the archaeologically 
known features was the same as that described ethnograph­
ically for the analogous facilities (Binford 1967a:9)•
Munson (1969:82-85), also using analogy with ethnographically observed
phenomena, refutes Binford's specific postulate that the smudge pits
were uc'ed for hide smoking in favor of a more general use; however, he
does concur in principle.
Polynesian Religion
To recognize the significance of marae and other religious 
ceremonial centers in Polynesian life, it is necessary to understand 
Polynesian religion. In the Polynesian system of defining and catego­
rizing the supernatural, ancestral gods and the manner of their worship
are of greatest interest to a discussion of the marae complex. As well,
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 19 ===

more general •underlying features of the religious system need to he 
considered.
Separable Soul and Death
The concept of a separable soul, of which dreaming and fanta- 
sying are inherent features, was a basic tenet in all the Polynesian 
religious beliefs (Han<3y 19^0:310). Belief in ghosts or spirits, rep­
resenting principally the souls of departed ancestors- but also includ­
ing nature spirits and demons, was universal in Polynesia. These ghosts 
or spirits were most commonly associated with defined regions or terri­
tories and were often incarnate in particular objects or animals.
Dreams, fantasy, clairvoyance, coma and wish-fulfillment, and rational­
ization are all ways in which a body of myth was built up about specific 
spirits (Handy 19^0:311)• The concept of a separable soul was clearly 
expressed in the worship and deification of ancestors. This activity 
was not so much to gain a secure future for oneself in another world as 
it was an attempt to insure or aid in the welfare of the living. There 
was great concern for the departed individual, but the practical-minded 
Polynesians placed greater emphasis upon what the departed ancestor 
could do for the living. There was general recognition that continued 
supplication or placation of ancestors was necessary to avoid offending 
these spirits.
Rites at birth and death were of the greatest importance at the 
extended family and ramage level of social organization. These rites 
were designed to safeguard both the soul of the person involved and the 
souls of the family involved. Protection of the individual was accom­
plished through ritual which combined worship, consisting of prayer and 
offerings, with magic consisting of potent acts or objects. This
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 20 ===

r 
12
ritual was designed to purify and to drive away evil spirits or imper­
sonal evil influences and to endow the individual with desirable quali­
ties and powers with the aid of good spiritual agencies (Handy 19^0:
313)• The safeguarding process often consisted of a series of rites 
conducted over a considerable period.
The places consecrated for such activities range from simple 
shrines to large stone temples, whose location was primarily dependent 
upon the type of supplication to be performed. The household shrine was 
typical in some areas but generally the actual graves or tombs of ances­
tors served as focal points. Erect stones or posts, at shrines or on 
marae, served to symbolize ancestral spirits and served as back-rests or 
seats * Relics of the dead, such as skulls, were commonly preserved as 
active ritual objects. Members of every occupation or craft placated 
certain patron deities or spirits at shrines (Handy 1927:166). Fisher­
men's shrines, canoe carver's shrines, and agricultural shrines were 
specific locations where a particular god or gods were appealed to for 
assistance. In all the crafts such as canoe and house building a master 
or expert called a tohunga (or a dialectical variation) was in charge of 
the operation since he, because of his skill and accuracy of execution, 
had the required mana (supernatural power) to ensure a product that 
would be maximally useful. Domestic shrines where families venerated 
their dead and where private rites such as those of birth and death were 
performed normally were located in or near the dwelling (Handy 1927:168).
Tombs or consecrated localities served as domestic shrines for 
temporary or permanent deposition of bodies. One of the main uses of 
the family shrine was for the preparation of the remains of the 
recently dead. Corpses were exposed and treated on a bier erected on
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 21 ===

r
the marae platform, and after the preliminary treatment they were often 
buried there. The family head functioned as priest of the marae in 
accordance with his position of genealogical seniority as head.
group worship of subtribe or tribe. Some festivals of tribal-wide par­
ticipation, or even of supra-tribal participation, included the observ­
ance of events in the life-cycle of the first-born of the community who 
was the first-born 
the ranking chief of the included social group 
(Handy 19^-0:315; • The first-born was a spiritual medium between the 
gods, the people, and the land. The sacred chief (ariki) was a great 
storehouse of mana. As such, he was the head of the village, tribe, and 
island-wide political unit. His soul linked men with gods and was a 
sacred vehicle and transmitter of spiritual energy. The sacredness of 
his loin cloth was symbolic of his relationship to procreation which 
affects both men and nature. His person and all associated with him, 
such as household, canoe, and relatives, were as sacred as if he were a 
god (Handy 19^0:316). The sanctity of the chief, while deriving from 
his status lineage or ramage (since he was theoretically the first-born 
of first-borns in direct line from the mythological ancestor or progeni­
tor of his lineage), was increased by rituals designed for that purpose 
conducted at his birth, marriage, and installation as an ariki. At 
death his soul was made into a god in the next world. The figure of the 
sacred chief in religion and society was an extension and intensification 
of the role of the first-born in the household and lineage group. The 
death of a chief called for universal mourning and veneration since it 
marked the passage into the other world of the soul of a person whose 
power and prestige called for a series of rites of elaborate offerings,
The pattern of family worship also extended into the larger
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 22 ===

r
sacrifice, eulogy, and finally deification rites, so that the soul of 
the communal fore-bearer became a god (Handy 19^+0:315)*
Mana
Mana and tapu are best illustrated in the person of the sacred 
chief; his particularly close relation -with the gods -was due to his high 
rank and therefore his possession of the highest degree of mana. This 
religious concept, mana, was first given currency by R. H. Codrington 
for Melanesian societies in 1891, although the term was known in 
Polynesian ethnography considerably before that time (Firth 19^0:^85).
It was the central concept of power in Polynesia and all Oceania. Ini­
tial attempts to understand its role in religious thought involved 
translating the word into precise verbal equivalents. Some of the many 
translations compiled by Firth (l9to:i4-8U) are:
Supernatural power; influence (Codrington).
Magical power; psychic force (Marett).
Impersonal religious force; totemic principle (Durkheim).
Divine force (Handy).
Effective; miracle; authority; prestige; etc. (Tregear).
True (Hocart).
Mana has also been compared to electricity (Handy 1927:28) and to luck 
(Hogbin 1936:265). The Polynesian concept of mana was a practical one, 
connoting prosperity and success, it was recognized especially in the 
leaders of a community, the chiefs, and priests (Hocart 1922).
Codrington makes the same point for mana in Melanesia, but its appear­
ance was the result of slightly different factors:
All conspicuous success is a proof that a man has mana; his 
influence depends on the impression made on the people's mind 
that he has it; he becomes a chief by virtue of it. Hence a 
man's power, though political or social in its character, is 
his mana (1891:120).
In Polynesia, Indonesia, and Micronesia mana was regarded in general as
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 23 ===

an inhereited quality; in Melanesia it could te acquired regardless of 
genealogy. Although Firth has attempted to demonstrate that no general 
concept of mana was held in common by all Oceanic communities (l9*tO), 
we may say that within Polynesia it was a primary condition of status 
and that this ideal status had a religious setting. The tapu which 
accompanied mana was a recognition of sanctity which demanded reveren­
tial avoidance. Mana was regarded everywhere as something possessed by 
or in degrees; thus in Polynesia it was graded in quantity but there 
are persons who had none (Goldman 1970:11). The variation in the 
amount of this power people had allowed distinctions between chiefly 
and priestly sanctity, although the distinction was rarely a clear one 
because Polynesian ariki always had some religious or priestly func­
tion. Ariki served sometimes as high priest as well as chief, a role 
of significance for comprehending the position of the ariki in rela­
tion to the marae or ahu.
A statement by Handy indicates the probable general association 
of mana and worship of gods in Polynesia:
. . .  in the light of the general intent of the worship of the 
higher powers through rapport, it may fairly be assumed that one 
main purpose of the prayer, sacrifice, and other elements of the 
Polynesian rites was to increase the mana of the gods worshipped, 
that, while they sought on the one hand to approach and draw on 
the strength embodied in their gods, these people were confident 
at the same time that their efforts in worship were capable of, 
indeed requisite for, empowering the very gods on whom they 
believed themselves to be dependent (Handy 1927:3*0 •
Stone was regarded as the most permanent agency that could be utilized 
as a medium or container of mana and was therefore used for the up­
rights or back-rests of marae. In many places they were used as images 
of the ancestral gods themselves. The idea behind the function of 
statues or images on marae is of great importance on islands like the
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 24 ===

r
Marquesas, Easter, and Ra'ivavae where they occur in greatest numbers. 
Transfer of sacredness through contact with the basic idea associated 
with the mana content of the stone back-rest. The stone upon which a 
man imbued with mana— an ariki or priest— rested, and especially such 
a stone used in a ceremonial context, became charged with this person's 
mana and would remain so until the tapu was released and rapport broken 
between the object and the individual through one of the rites pre­
scribed for the purpose (Handy 1927:l8o) . The mana effect, in use of 
ba,ck-rests of this sort, was cumulative. An example of the use of a 
stone as a container for mana is indicated by the practice of incor­
porating a stone from an older marae structure into a newer one.
Uniformity in the general aspects of marae structures through­
out East Polynesia, although considerable variation exists even within 
the same island, reflects the comparable congruence in basic religious 
belief which was primarily concerned with veneration of ancestors.
Handy maintains that several common elements were originally part of 
the early Polynesian cult and sacred place. These may have been: 
l) an ahu consisting of a pile of stone and/or soil; 2) perhaps orien­
tation, and also a sacred area around this ahu that may have been sur­
rounded by a fence; 3) upright stones or slabs; U) tapu rubbish pit;
5) perhaps a staging or frame on which offerings could be placed; 6) a 
shrine to be occupied by a visible representation of the patron god, or 
by a prophet (priest or chief) or by both; and 7) an association of the 
place of worship with sacred groves of trees (Handy 1927:176-177). One 
goal of this synthesis will be to examine these features in light of 
recent data. Because the greatest emphasis here is on Easter Island 
and the development of its ahu, a general introduction to the island is

necessary before proceeding with a detailed examination of the reli­

Easter Island
A thorough understanding of the nature of the marae type cere­
monial centers and their development on Easter Island can be achieved 
only through detailed examination of historic ethnography coupled with 
archaeological evidence of the prehistoric culture. A brief outline of 
the natural setting is needed as a background to such an examination.
Natural Context
Easter Island, located at 190° 2 5 1 E. longitude and 27° 08' S. 
latitude, is one of the most geographically isolated, islands in the 
world (Fig. l). The nearest island, Sala y Gomez, 210 miles (338 kilo­
meters) to the east, is a very small and uninhabitable barren rock only 
three-fourths of a mile long and one-tenth of a mile wide. The near­
est inhabited island is Pitcairn, 1200 miles (1920 kilometers) to the 
northwest. Landfall to the east, the bulge of the west side of the 
South American continent, is 2300 miles (3700 kilometers) away.
Easter Island is ll+ miles (22 kilometers) long in a northeast- 
southwest direction and 7 miles (ll kilometers) across (Fig. 2). The 
total area of the triangular island is about 2+5 square miles (117 
square kilometers). A typical oceanic high island, it was formed by 
volcanic activity at three major locations, one high volcano at each 
vertex of the triangle. The highest volcano, Terevaka, is nearly 600 
meters in elevation. 
Secondary volcanics have filled in the interior 
of the island, forming a terrain of soft rolling hills of tuff cones. 
The entire island is densely covered with scattered rocks from exposed

lava flows. Three major crater lakes contain fresh water; the lake 
(rano) in the i aldera of the volcano Rano Kau is nearly one mile across 
and of undetermined depth. The lakes in Rano Aroi, on the slope of 
Terevaka, and in Rano Raraku, near the east end of the island, are con­
siderably smaller. The two oldest volcanoes are probably Rano Kau and 
Poike (which forms the east end of the triangle), although Chubb has 
indicated that Rano Kau may be a parasitic cone of Terevaka (Chubb 
1933:33).
More recently, P. Baker considered Rano Kao to be one of the 
two earliest volcanoes and noted the presence of a parasitic crater on 
the northeast side of the larger volcano. Poike was apparently an iso­
lated peak at one time before lava flows from Terevaka and its subsidi­
ary cones filled in the area between the two. The ash slopes of the 
three volcanoes are presently smooth and grass covered but the lava 
flows joining them are rough and uneven.
Of special interest for a discussion of the prehistoric inhab­
itants is the lack of volcanic activity during the period of occupa­
tion. One of the latest flows came from a parasitic cone called Maunga 
Hivahiva near the west coast. The age of this flow has been estimated 
at two to three thousand years ago (Baker 1967:121) and it is unlikely 
that Easter was inhabited that early. Other aspects of the geology of 
the island include the near lack of calcareous sand beaches. The larg­
est one, Anakena, is only about 150 meters across. The other three or 
four are only small sand deposits today. One of the striking aspects 
of the island is its rocky coastline, with cliffs rising to heights of 
over 300 meters on the southwest side of Rano Kau, formed by erosion of 
the flanks of the major volcanoes. The total lack of streams or
L

2ol
running water of any size has, of course, been a factor in preserving 
the uneroded condition of the interior. The major sources of fresh 
water are the crater lakes and springs along the coast, most of which 
mix with the sea immediately because they come out just at sea level.
Of archaeological interest is the presence of obsidian in three differ­
ent locations at the southwest end of the island. Obsidian was exten­
sively used for weapons and cutting tools. One of the most interesting 
parasitic tuff cones of Terevaka is that of Rano Raraku from which the 
huge stone statues so characteristic of Easter Island were quarried.
The tuff is a coarse yellow material that has been exposed in high 
cliffs on the south and west sides of the crater.
Easter Island's position near the southern limit of the tropics
gives it a semi-tropical climate. Temperature averages for the seven 
years between 1955 and 1961 were 21.2 degrees C. (72.2° F.) annually, 
with a yearly range from 20.7 degrees (69.3° F.) to 22.2 degrees 
(72.0° F.). The lowest recorded temperature was about lU0 C.
(57.2° F.). The annual rainfall average over a period from 1912 to 
1963 for 2k complete years was 1171.8 millimeters (lf6.1 inches) with a 
range of 620.3 millimeters (2i+.i+ inches) to 1699 millimeters (66.9 
inches).
for a volcanic ocean island. 
Skottsberg stated that "there does not 
exist another island of the size of Easter and with such a fine climate 
where the native flora is so poor" (1920, 2 :k 8 S ) . Only about 30 spe­
cies are considered to be indigenous. A grass cover was present at the 
time of earliest European contact in 1722 and grass is still the major 
vegetation covering most of the island despite recent attempts to
The flora and fauna of the island are extremely limited, even

introduce larger plants. The lack of trees during the historic period 
is of interest because it directly relates to the question of how the 
large stone statues were moved. Some unpublished pollen studies indi­
cate that there was a much heavier vegetative cover in prehistoric 
times (Heyerdahl 1968:13*+) . The scrub trees that were still found 
growing on the island during the period of early contact included 
toro-miro (Sophora toromiro), hau (Triumfetta semitriloba), makoi

And I have been led to believe that some kind of forest once 
existed on Easter Island, formed by Sophora: and other species, 
exterminated long ago, may also have occurred (1920, 2 :1+02).
Paper mulberry or mahute (Brousonetia papyrifera), ti (Cordyline), 
marikuru (Sapindus), bananas, sugar cane, taro, yams, sweet potato, 
and a few others, including some weeds, were introduced to the island 
by the original settlers (Skottsberg 1920, 2:1+02). The fauna con­
sisted primarily of varieties of sea birds; others, including the 
lizard, chicken, and Polynesian rat, were brought by the original 
settlers. There is no evidence that the pig or dog was present in 
prehistoric times.
Only a general outline of the basic aspects of the culture will
be given here as an introduction because a detailed analysis of the 
ethnographic data pertaining especially to the ahu on Easter Island 
will be discussed later. The overall impression received of Easter 
Island culture is that it is Polynesian, modified by isolation and, 
especially in historic times, by an impoverished natural environment.
April 5, 1722 (Easter Sunday), with the arrival of Jacob Roggeveen, a 
Dutch explorer. He was looking for the land described by the English

The first recorded visit by Europeans to Easter Island came on

buccaneer, Edward Davis, who had sighted land in 1687 located "500 
leagues from Copiapo." Roggeveen anchored off the north coast of the 
island, probably at the location later known as La Perouse Bay, where 
he and a large group of men landed to survey the island. 
Shortly after 
disembarking, several natives were killed in a confrontation, thus only 
brief accounts of the landing and the islanders are available.
Roggeveen's three expedition ships left on the tenth of April after 
five days. He and his Captain of Marines, Behrens, only mentioned the 
presence of large statues or "idols" that were later to become such a 
major part of the "mystery" of Easter Island (Roggeveen 1722:15, 132).
Some H8 years later the island was "rediscovered" by Felipe 
Gonzalez y Haedo who claimed it for Spain in 1770. He took possession 
by placing three crosses on the three trachyte domes on the north slope 
of the volcano Poike. Papers were drawn up to show possession, which 
the natives "signed" by making marks of various sorts. The Spanish 
reported little exploration into the interior of the island and 
departed after six days. They probably were anchored in Hanga-o-honu 
Bay.
Captain James Cook contacted the island in 177*+ during his sec­
ond voyage into the Pacific, stayed for three days and left quite dis­
appointed by its impoverished condition. Cook and his officers were 
impressed by the number and size of the stone statues and monuments in 
contrast to’ the small number of destitute inhabitants. After exploring 
some inland terrain and the coastal areas around the village of Hanga- 
roa, Cook left, disappointed with what the island had to offer, espe­
cially in regard to food and water.

23l
The French navigator, La Perouse, landed in Cook Bay at Hanga- 
roa in 1786. An exploring expedition sent to examine the interior of 
the island followed nearly the same route as the Cook party some 12 
years earlier, and little new information was recorded. A member of 
the La Perouse group, M. Bernizet, however, made some excellent draw­
ings and sketches of the monuments (Fig. 3, H) and houses. These are 
the earliest and most accurate depictions drawn in the eighteenth cen­
tury. La Perouse attempted to introduce certain domesticated plants 
and animals, but eventually all failed.
Such are the reports of the eighteenth century visitors to the 
island. Their more specific comments dealing with Easter Island reli­
gious activities will be discussed later in the context of a detailed 
consideration of the religious complex. These earlier explorers had 
friendly relations with the islanders on the whole, even though several 
people were shot by Roggeveen and one by Cook's men. This relationship 
changed drastically during the early l800's when several more ships 
stopped at the island. One of these, the American schooner Nancy, kid­
napped 22 islanders after a bloody battle. The islanders were report­
edly needed to start a colony on the isolated island of Masafuera.
This kidnapping incident, and many others that preceded and followed 
it, caused much distrust and hard feeling for later visitors and many 
were unable to land because of the hostile attitude of the islanders.
The experiences of Kotzebue in l8l6 and Beechey in 1825 are illustra­
tive. Upon landing, these men found the islanders to be friendly, but 
shortly after disembarking the thievery of the islanders turned to real 
plundering and the group had to retreat to their boats under a hail of 
stones. These incidents led to shooting and several islanders were

killed. Easter Island has no harbor of any size that allows a shel­
tered anchorage for a large ship and going ashore in smaller boats was 
very difficult in rough weather. Because of these factors, only brief 
stays were made at Easter Island up to 1862, and only general accounts 
of the island, most of which describe the same things, are available. 
Very little information was added as a result of these contacts. How­
ever, there are a few important exceptions which do add insight into 
pre-missionary culture.
The Peruvian slave raids, beginning in 1859 and. culminating in 
1862, resulted in the capture and abduction of nearly 1000 Easter 
Islanders for work on the guano fields off the coast of Peru (Metraux 
191+0:1+2-1+3). This depletion of the population and especially the loss 
of the captured ariki and maori or "bards" resulted in a tremendous loss 
of knowledge and interest in the Polynesian culture , traditions and 
religious beliefs. This was apparent at the time of the arrival of the 
first missionary, Eugene Eyraud in 1861+. By 1868 all of the remaining 
islanders were stated to have accepted the Christian religion (Jaussen 
187^:90-115). The population at this time was estimated to be about 
1+000 by Zumbohm, one of the early missionaries (l879 » 5:663). Earlier 
reports on population during l8th and 19th centuries placed the number 
of inhabitants under 1000, a figure based primarily on the number of 
people seen at the landing. The missionary Roussel states that a cen­
sus taken between 1867 and 1870 showed 650 people. 
The decline was due 
to slave raids, introduced diseases such as tuberculosis and smallpox, 
and famine. Roussel put the maximum pre-contact population between 
5000 and 6000 because the interior of the island was thought to be 
unoccupied (Roussel 1926:1+26).

Ethnographic Outline
Although early descriptions of the Easter Islanders stress such 
things as the light color of their skin or several different skin 
colors and red hair, there is no evidence that the Easter Islanders 
exhibit other than the Polynesian physical type. 
Shapiro (in Me'traux 
19^0 :2I4-30) indicates the two schools of thought during the 19^+0 1 s 
concerning the physical type; one suspecting a closer relationship to 
the Melanesian physical type and the other asserting that there was no 
evidence for a relationship with non-Polynesian groups. The physical 
type of the Easter Islanders during the period around A.D. 1100 to the 
present was shown by Murrill to be Polynesian (Murrill 1965:225-32U; 
Murrill 1968).
Easter Island tradition concerning the initial settlement 
refers directly to the arrival of the first king, Hotu Matua, from a 
land called Marae-renga or Marae-toe-hau from which he fled because of 
feuding or defeat in battle. The major legends concern l) the arrival 
of Hotu Matua, 2) the war between the "long ears" and the "short ears," 
and 3) the war between the Tuu and Hotu-iti groups. The war between 
the "long ears" and the "short ears" is supposed to have taken place 
during the lifetime of Hotu Matua!s children (Metraux 19^0:71). Some 
writers, especially Heyerdahl (1952, 1958), consider this tradition as 
evidence for positing an earlier population or race of people on the 
island, those with the long ears. Metraux felt that the two groups 
involved were from the same Polynesian settlement and the conflict 
could refer to a later incident (l9*tO:7^). The fight between the Tuu 
people on the west side of the island and those of Hotu-iti on the east 
is related in a series of stories concerning Kainga, a warrior of the
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 37 ===

Hotu-iti. These stories appear to refer to actual conflict or feuding 
between these two districts, probably in the late prehistoric period or 
protohistoric period.
According to Metraux, Easter Island was populated by 10 tribes 
or mata (19*40:121) . These were localized groups of descendants from a 
common ancestor in the early periods of Easter Island prehistory, but 
by historic times the overlapping membership of different mata implies 
that only the main body of the tribe was localized on the actual tribal 
land. These separate social groups were, fictitiously perhaps, all 
related by tracing their origin to the king, Hotu-matua (Metraux 19*4-0: 
123). There were no recorded marriage restrictions among the tribes 
save for those of the tumu which were special relationships between 
tribes or subtribes. The Miru, on the north and west coasts of the 
island, was the mata from which the ariki mau (principal chief) or 
king of the island came. The major division of the island into two 
groups, Hotu-iti and Ko Tuu, is mentioned in traditions.
The tribes included a number of lineages or subtribes (Metraux 
19*10:126), which were localized to a degree within the larger mata and 
were associated with specific ahu. Lineages held fairly large heredi­
tary portions of land which were divided among households. Villages, 
probably consisting of the majority of the people of the lineage, were 
commonly located directly behind the ahu (this may apply only through 
late prehistoric times).
Four classes of Easter Island society have been reported: 
ariki, ivi-atua, matatoa, and kio (Metraux 19*4-0:129). However, the 
classification is a superficial one and the reality of clear-cut 
classes is in doubt. The king, or ariki mau, was the possessor of the
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 38 ===

r 
291
greatest amount or degree of mana due to his heredity and was subject 
to many tapus as a result. At the time of European, contact the king was 
not a political ruler but more of a spiritual one who presided over the 
entire island but did not engage in intertribal feuding. Very little 
is known of the role of priests (ivi-atua), but the general indications 
are that they were similar to specialists of this sort in the rest of 
Polynesia. There appears to have been a class of people who were pro­
fessional warriors (matatoa). The warrior leaders eventually took over 
the actual political control of the tribes (Me'traux 19^0:138; Goldman 
1970:116). Other important experts or craftsmen, not recognized by 
Metraux as a social class, were called maori or tahonga similar to the 
tohunga of other islands in Polynesia. The tangata rongo-rongo formed 
a special class of experts for chanting and carving rongo-rongo script 
on wooden boards. Serfs, or kio, defeated people who worked as slaves, 
were a fourth social class according to Metraux (19^40:139)-
According to tradition warfare or feuding was mainly related to 
rivalry between the mata groups of the western and eastern ends of the 
island. Shifting alliances among these kin groups seemed to have been 
common. A very slight incident could cause large-scale conflict since 
the groups would most often be involved as units. Defeated groups were 
chased and killed or taken for slaves and many captives were eaten.
Houses and fields were pillaged.
Unfortunately very little knowledge of the native religion of 
Easter Island remained to be recorded in the protohistoric and historic 
periods. Few names or accounts of the great Polynesian gods were 
recorded and even the number of lesser gods or spirits called akuaku 
that were remembered in 193*+ during Metraux's visit was only 30 as
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 39 ===

r
30]
opposed to the 90 or so recorded in 1919 (Me^traux 19^0:309). Metraux 
recognized two major classes of gods, a feature of the widespread East 
Polynesian religious system. The first class included the great fig­
ures of creation and major gods like Tangaroa, Tu, and Kongo. The sec­
ond class of deities ranged probably from the sons of the great gods to 
deified ancestors or simpler spirits. The major gods were only briefly 
mentioned in the traditional stories that were recorded. The god 
Makemake is the only one whose importance has been noted. He is the 
creator god and is most directly connected with the bird-man ceremonies 
at Orongo. Me'traux felt that this god had absorbed the personality of 
Tiki, a deity of importance in all of Polynesia (191+0:315). The lesser 
gods or spirits of Easter Island, the akuaku, appear to have been spir­
its worshipped by certain tribes or lineages but they had influence 
only in their geographically limited districts (Metraux 19^0:309).
Lower in rank than the akuaku were an innumerable group of nameless 
spirits of the dead and small nature demons.
give a list of the gods and goddesses who mated to produce most of the 
natural objects known to the natives (1891:520-522). This chant is a 
typical Polynesian one, patterned after genealogical recitals in East 
Polynesian society (Metraux 19^0:322). Dreams, as elsewhere in 
Polynesia, were considered to be predictive of future events as were 
many other types of omens. The strength of the tapu concept was very 
great and violation often meant death for the offender.

so their importance must have been considerable (Roussel 1926:360).
The "noblemen'' or ariki paka of the king’s tribe, the Miru, were 
reported to have been priests, but the terms ivi-atua and koromaki 
referred specifically to types of priests or magicians.
The bird cult associated with the site of Orongo and the three 
small offshore islands to the south were connected to the great god 
Makemake. The person who found the first egg of the sooty tern for 
his chief placed that chief in the envied position of tangata mann or 
"bird man" for one year. He was the virtual ruler of the entire 
island during this time.
Easter Island Ahu 
The term ahu on Easter Island generally refers to the stone 
structures scattered around the island coast used for burial and some­
times as a foundation for large stone statues. In the Easter Island 
dialect the word itself has been translated by Englert to mean
l) burial in stone structures of the type mentioned above, 2) a burial 
in the ground that is surrounded by stones, 3) platform of stone with­
out a burial, and k) to swell up, enlarge or expand, that is, as a 
verb (1938:1^-15). On other East Polynesian islands this term refers 
to a pile of stones or the stone platform associated with the most 
sacred area within the marae. Several different types of stone struc­
tures are referred to as ahu on Easter Island. Classifications used 
by various authors are described below.
The first person to differentiate among ahu was La Perouse in 
1786, who noted that besides the image ahu with statues still standing 
there were small pyramidal heaps of stones thought to have covered
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 41 ===

r 
32]
graves. These small cairns were considered to be more recent than the 
ahu with the colossal statues (La Perouse 1798:322).
No detailed examination of Easter Island culture was made until 
1882, the arrival of the German ethnographer, Geiseler. Geiseler 
failed to describe ahu in detail; his report also lacks classification. 
William J. Thomson was the first person to make a thorough description 
of all the larger ahu surrounding the coastline as well as to conduct 
excavations; however, he failed to differentiate among types of ahu.
He discussed each ahu only in terms of its image ahu characteristics 
(l891:*+97-513). He located 113 ahu around the coast, and found 555 
statues, of these 238 were associated with ahu. He noted that rebuild­
ing attempts were visible at some ahu; this is the first mention of 
separate stages of construction for the Easter Island ahu.
The first clear categorization of possible functional and tem­
poral differences in ahu was presented by Routledge in 1919 as a 
result of her 17-month stay on the island, 1911+-15 (Routledge 1919) .
Her distinctions were based on surface descriptions and some selected 
excavations. She made a distinction between the "image ahu," the forms 
she called "semi-pyramidal," and the "ahu poepoe." She located just 
under 100 of the image ahu and counted 231 statues associated with them 
(Routledge 1919:168). Routledge's description of the image ahu form 
includes mention of the central platform, two lateral wings, a sloping 
ramp on the landward side, and a partially paved court. She also 
observed the statue pedestals or "bed plates" on top of the central 
platform at the sea-side edge (Routledge 1919:168). A range from one 
to fifteen statues per ahu was noted. Routledge also reported that 
none of these image ahu were in their original condition and that most
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 42 ===

1 
33l
exhibited rebuilding over an earlier stage. She said that portions of 
earlier statues, sometimes of material different from that of the later 
statues, were incorporated into the seawall of the ahu (1919:171). A 
second type of ahu distinguished by Routledge is the "semi-pyramidal" 
form, some of these were built over the top of ahu which originally 
were image ahu. Their temporal relationship is thus indicated. The 
term "semi-pyramidal" derives from these structures' resemblance to a 
pyramid cut in two, with the seaside wall being triangular in face 
view. However, Routledge recorded only 13 ahu of this sort that were 
built over an earlier image ahu base and 50 to 60 semi-pyramidal ahu 
that were not located on earlier image ahu, thus not all must be later 
than image ahu (Routledge 1919:171)• Routledge's ahu poepoe consisted 
of a long, low stone platform of uncut stone, often with raised ends.
A related form, "wedge-shaped ahu," had its raised end pointed towards 
the sea (1919:230-231).
In 1937 Metraux attempted to define types of ahu on the basis 
of surface description. He called four distinct structures ahu and 
gave the following structural descriptions (Metraux 1937:106):
1) image ahu - largest and most elaborate, with statues,
2) 
semi-pyramidal ahu or ahu paepae,
3) ahu poepoe - one or both ends of the structure slope towards
the center or one extremity,
k) rectangular ahu - ahu avanga.
The image ahu are the largest structures and have the most elaborate 
stonework. The use of cut stone, although also present in other types 
of ahu, is primarily associated with the image ahu. The distinctive 
feature is the presence of statues.
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 43 ===

r
351
The image ahu consists of three main parts: l) the "central
platform," a rectangular shaped platform on which the statues stood,
2) an inclined ramp in front (landward side) of the centx’al platform. 
Two lateral walls extending out from the sides of the central platform 
form the seaward side of the ramp. These lateral extensions of the 
central platform are the "wings" of the anu, and 3) a court or plaza on
the landward side of the ahu sassw^iure. I consider the entire complex 
of central platform and court to he the equivalent of the marae found 
in other parts of Polynesia. Burial vaults are often located in the 
inclined ramp and in the interior of the central platform. The seawall 
of the central platform is of cut and fitted stones in most cases as is 
the landward side of the central platform retaining wall. The seaward 
walls of the ahu wings are also often built of cut stones.
The other three types of ahu described by Metraux are construc­
ted of uncut stone and are, in general, much smaller than the image 
ahu. The semi-pyramidal ahu, as described by Routledge, consists of a 
perpendicular wall of fitted but not cut stones which parallels the 
coast. The landward side is a ramp of rubble fill with a high ridge in 
the center leading from the ground to the top center of the seaward 
wall. The fill slopes down laterally to the ground on either side of 
the central ridge. The entire structure appears as a low bisected 
pyramid, with the slopes on the landward side. The largest examples of 
this type of structure were built over ruined image ahu foundations.
The statues of the earlier image ahu were merely included in the stone 
fill forming the plane surface. Those in which no earlier, underlying 
ahu of the image type is now observable are much smaller; maximum size
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 44 ===

r
35I
is 10 to 15 meters in length and 3 meters in height. 
Semi-pyramidal 
ahu that were investigated contained burial vaults in the central por­
tion.
The third type of structure, ahu poepoe, is quite different 
from the two types of ahu described above. Ahu poepoe, like the semi- 
pyramidal, lacks cut stone and was built only of fitted, selected 
stones. The extended length and often raised ends give rise to the 
term ahu poepoe (ahu-boat), or boat-shaped ahu. Vaults for burial 
were discovered by Routledge. A variant of this form, common on the 
north coast around Hanga-o-teo Bay, is a rectangular structure which 
has a high narrow end pointing towards the sea. The surface slopes 
gradually towards the landward end of the structure. Routledge 
reserved the term "wedge=shaped" ahu for this form; there are only six 
examples of this type (Metraux 1937:116).
The fourth type of ahu described by Metraux is the simple, rec­
tangular ahu avanga. These are rectangular stone platforms of varying 
size. All ahu of this type that have been investigated contain an 
interior burial vault. A semi-circular line of stones was observed 
adjacent to the seaward side of one of these structures. Metraux dis­
tinguished these ahu from the similar structures called hare moa which 
are reportedly chicken houses. Differentiating these two structures ^n 
the field requires excavation to determine the presence of a burial 
vault.

This study is based on field research which disclosed data con-

361
is briefly outlined as follows:
Settlement and Developmental Phase ("Early Period") 
(A.D. UOO-IOOO ?)
Ahu during this time consisted of an open court and an elevated 
platform with dressed, upright slab walls and rubble fill. 
Statues may have appeared prior to A.D. 700. Knowledge of the 
portable artifact inventory is slight.
Expansion or Ahu Moai Phase ("Middle Period") 
(A.D. 1000-1680 ?)
Ahu increase in size, variety, and show a culmination of 
dressed stone architecture early in the phase, followed by 
increasing stress on larger, standardized images (moai) placed 
on the ahu platform. The quality of stone masonry shows some 
decline. House forms, villages, and other non-ahu architec­
tural features are known. Adze kits, fishing technology, 
ground stone tools, and flaked obsidian suggest basically a 
simplified early Polynesian assemblage.
Decadent or Huri Moai Phase ("Late Period") 
(A.D. 1680-1722)
No image ahu were constructed during this phase and most ahu 
built earlier were destroyed or allowed to fall into ruin. 
Semi-pyramidal ahu were built in place of the image ahu and 
these were used for mass burial and individual stone slab 
tombs. Other non-ahu structures like those of the Expansion 
phase continued to be built. Expansion phase portable arti­
facts continued to be used. The terminal date here coincides 
with the first European contact.
Protohistoric Phase ("Late Period") 
(A.D. 1722-1868)
Ahu continued to be used for burial; in many cases burials con­
sisting of bundles of bones were simply placed under the loose
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 46 ===

stones. A Decadent phase ahu use continues; however, it is 
important to recognize that significant changes accompanied 
European contact. The terminal date is the conversion of the 
inhabitants to Christianity.
Historic Phase (A.D. 1868 - 
)
Ahu continued to be used occasionally for burial into the 
early twentieth century.
The aim of this study is to examine the developmental sequence 
in detail and to place it within a Polynesian framework. Because of 
obvious limitations and gaps both in the archaeological and ethno­
graphic records, the developmental sequence with its associated cul­
tural context which will be provided for Easter Island and more broadly 
for East Polynesia must necessarily be of exploratory nature and is 
designed to give a framework for future research. This analysis will 
then provide a framework for asking cultural questions that can be 
answered through carefully defined and executed archaeological field 
research.
Summary
As an introduction to the study of Polynesian religious centers, 
marae and rela ,ed structures are recognized in this chapter as a vital 
part of historic and prehistoric Polynesian cultural systems and as an 
important topic for ethnographic and archaeological study. The study 
deals with the problems of placing Easter Island ahu within a develop­
mental sequence, of defining the changing cultural context of such 
structures, and of placing this sequence in the larger setting of pre­
historic developments in Polynesia. The material is approached from a

ogy for prehistoric interpretation. In the following chapters, "back­

more specifically, for Easter Island cultural developments.

CHAPTER II 
THE ARCHAEOLOGICAL PERSPECTIVE
Archaeological Evidence of Change 
Considerable time depth for marae use in Polynesia is provided 
by excavations. The persistence of these stone and soil monuments 
allows recognition of complex architectural stratigraphy in several 
instances, particularly on Easter Island, but also on Hawaiian and the 
Marquesan Islands. Some alterations of Easter Island ahu are visible 
on the surface. 
Stratigraphic and absolute dating provide indications 
of a general trend in marae construction; the tendency is one toward 
increasing size and architectural complexity, particularly evident in 
the Marquesas and Hawaii, but also noticeable in other islands. In 
this chapter the evidence for changes in the patterns of marae con­
struction will be examined and the implications of such changes will 
be considered: l) to what extent can continuity of religious function
be traced; 2) are there recognizable patterns in architecture which 
allow some understanding of the diffusion of marae, and do the pres­
ences of certain kinds of platforms, walls, pits, and pavements allow 
one to discern houses or other perishable features and to speculate 
about the nature of prehistoric ritual activity, and 3) can changes 
in structure and distribution of these structures be correlated with 
social units and their development?
39
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.

Easter Island Ahu 
The description of ahu by early explorers, Geiseler (1883)? 
Thomson (1891), and later attempts to classify these remains by 
Routledge (1919)? and Metraux (1937? 19*+0) were discussed in Chapter I. 
Lavachery gives additional field data (1935? 195*0 ? he also describes 
the surface architecture of several ahu and other structures located 
at Anakena (195*0 • A brief review of more recent archaeology, par­
ticularly excavations relating to ceremonial structures of the anu 
type is necessary before considering the results of the author's 
research in 1968.
Frevious Excavations 
The Norwegian Expedition to Easter Island and the East Pacific 
in 1955-56 was the first source of sub-surface archaeological data on 
an extensive range of topics. Of the many reports of the expedition, 
those of Mulloy (1961a) and Smith (1961a) are probably the most impor­
tant for our present purposes because they deal directly with the prob­
lem of ahu and their architectural changes through time. The field 
work of William Mulloy in 1955-56, 1960-61, 1968, and 1969-70 com­
prises the major research in regard to ahu and their architecture 
(Mulloy 1961a, 1968, 1970).
Almost all of the studies of ahu structures were directed at 
the large, impressive image ahu. Because most of the semi-pyramidal 
ahu were built over destroyed image ahu, a few of these also have been 
examined. None of the other types of ahu have been excavated or 
described in detail. This synthesis also concentrates on the image
L

Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.

ahu, the religious complex most closely related to the marae of East 
Polynesia.
Vinapu
Mulloy*s initial work (l96la) includes description of the par­
tial excavation of two ahu at Yinapu on the island's southeast coast. 
Because this was the first systematic excavation of ahu and offers the 
most complete published material, I will begin with these ahu. 
Heyerdahl (1961) and Ferdon (1961a, 196le) both used the Vinapu 
sequence to support conjectures regarding island-wide Early, Middle, 
and Late Periods. These periods correspond to the first three phases 
defined in Chapter I. To avoid confusion, I will use Mulloy's origi­
nal terminology.
Ahu Ho. 1
One early structure at Vinapu, referred to as Vinapu 1, con­
sisted of a central stone platform with a high seaward facade extended 
horizontally in both directions with masonry wings; the wings were 
backed with a sloped ramp of stone fill. The structure was conceived 
of and built as a unit. The seaward facade is widely known for its 
fine stone masonry (see Mulloy 196la: Plate 7, 8 ; Fig. 5 here). This 
facade shows a significant orientation of the ahu to solar phenomena, 
i.e., to the southern summer solstice (Mulloy 196la:159, Fig. 130). 
Mulloy's excavations indicated three major periods in the utilization 
of the structure, an Early Period, a Middle Period, and a Late Period. 
His excavations and conjectural reconstructions provide considerable 
information.

The Early Period structure of Vinapu 1 was about 80 meters
long, 12 meters wide, and appears to have had a plaza about 80 meters 
wide by 90 meters long. Large slabs were used as footing stones in 
the seawall. Vertical slabs were placed on top, each was precisely 
carved down to fit adjacent stones rather than being pre-prepared 
blocks. The wing walls show the same masonry technique but there is 
less stone cutting and the wall footing is less elaborate. The wings 
consisted of two sections, an inner and an outer, formed by a curve of 
the wing retaining wall to the inland (Mulloy 196la: Fig. 124). The 
top wall stones indicate that the surface of the central platform had 
a paving of cut and fitted slabs. The top wall stones as well as the 
vertical paengas (carved stones) of the inland wall had the inside 
edge champfered, apparently to receive these surface paving slabs or 
"capstones" (Mulloy 196la:102). The inner wall of the central plat­
form, that is, the retaining wall facing the plaza, was constructed 
of carved upright slabs positioned on edge end to end. The ramp which 
abutted this elevated retaining wall is thought by Mulloy to have been 
a soil surface that sloped down towards the plaza. No burial was dis­
covered that can be associated with this stage of the construction; it 
would seem clear that this ahu was not initially built as a tomb. 
Mulloy postulated that this Early Period structure did not have stone 
statues on the central platform primarily because the only statues 
discovered were those obviously part of the later, Middle Period 
structure.

on this shoddily rebuilt structure. All the statues had cylindrical

The Late Period is characterized by the overthrow of the 
statues, by the addition of a mantle of simal 1 stones over the whole 
structure, and by the intensive use of the disguised ahu sc a burial 
mound. Burials of more important people are indicated by the presence 
of well-built tombs in the wings and in front of the central platform. 
The features of the Middle Period ahu were well masked by the mantle 
of loose stones.
Ahu No. 2
Vinapu 2 is located approximately 20 meters south of Vinapu 1 
(see Mulloy 196la: Fig. 128; Fig. 5 here) and was approximately 80 
meters long and 15 meters wide with an adjoining plaza of 125 "by 125 
meters. Although he did less excavation here, Mulloy still recognized 
the same three stages of construction or development as at Vinapu 1 
(1961a:115-135)•
The basic plan of the Early Period structure is the same as 
that of Vinapu 1, but a major difference was the shape of the stones 
and construction of the seawall. Vinapu 1 was built of precisely

fitted blocks; the seawall of Vinapu 2 consisted of irregular upright

The central platform top was apparently paved in the same way

There is strong evidence for a period of abandonment, ero­

ues of Vinapu 2 were erected. The repair was often crude and incongru­
ous. 
Base plates, generally quite rough slabs of stone, were set on 
top of the rubble core of the central platform. Apparently some of the 
Early Period capstones were used for base plates. The sloping ramp of 
this structure was paved with rounded beach stones and with small beach 
gravel in between the stones. The total structure, not just the cen­
tral platform, was remodeled; this indicates the importance of the 
entire unit in the Middle Period as in the Earl.r Period. Again, no 
burial was found that can be associated with this phase of building.
The overthrow of the nine statues marked the beginning of the 
Late Period of Vinapu 2 and its transformation into a semi-pyramidal 
type structure. A number of disturbances in the ramp and wings of the 
ahu are ascribed to the Late Period destruction. The mantle of stone 
masking the Middle Period features provided material for making tombs 
in the rubble and fill of the earlier structure and for covering the 
numerous burials.

Ahu Tepeu and Other Ahu
Smith's work (1961a) in 1955-56 on a number of ahu provid.es 
additional information on the nature of ahu construction and on stages 
or periods of use. He formulated an Early Period (ca. UOO-llOO A.D.), 
a Middle Period (ca. 1100-1680 A.D.), and a Late Period (l680- 
1868 A.D.). Smith surveyed seven ahu, Ahu Ohae, Tepeu 1 and 2,
Naunau, Hekii 1 and 2, and Te-pito-te-kura; most of these provided 
evidence for his Early Period. He also discussed Tongariki and Mai- 
taki-te-Moa, hut he did no excavations or detailed mapping of these 
two structures. Smith uses the term ahu Moroki in reference to the 
Early Period ahu because they are architecturally distinct from the 
Middle Period ahu, called Ahu Moai. The term Huri Moai is used for 
the Late Period of Easter Island prehistory when the images were being 
overthrown.
Ahu Tepeu
Ahu Tepeu 1 and 2 are located on the west coast of the island. 
The seawall of the first and largest structure is slightly convex, 
consisting of twelve large vertical slabs and some less standardized 
block masonry on the north end (see Smith 196la: Plate 18 c and d).
The seawall platform projects only slightly beyond the wing walls.
The structure is oriented to the summer solstice, that is, the rising 
solstice sun is nearly at right angles (within a few degrees) to the 
inland retaining wall or the seawall. The length of the entire struc­
ture is V7.30 meters long and is about 11.5 meters wide. The row of 
upright slabs is 16 meters long; each slab is 25 or more centimeters 
thick by 1.25 to 1.75 meters wide and 1 to 2 meters high. The bases

Other Ahu
Ahu Naunau near Anakena Bay on the north coast of the island

This ahu is Lavachery's Ahu Anakena 6 (195^:1^+8-150) and Thomson's ahu

meters

has obscured most of the inland features of the ahu as well as the 
east wing. The platform on the west end is the earlier west wing of 
the larger ahu structure; it was rebuilt to hold the b o r  5 stylized, 
Middle Period statues. Smith (l96la:209) maintained that the facing 
stones are typical of the Early Period (his variety 1, which consists 
of slabs with the long axes horizontal) and emphasized that the use 
of a stone head in the seawall indicates the rebuilding of a probable 
Early Period structure (l96la: Plate 2ko and c). The statue head and 
the construction of the seawall do not necessarily indicate the Early 
Period, especially not the early portion of that period; the existence 
of such an early ahu remains in question. Smith (l96la:208,
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 59 ===

r
50]
Plate 2kc) notes important features of the statue: 
"the top of the
head is rounded instead of flat, . . . the ears are relatively short, 
and . . . the eyes are more deeply cut than usual." No further infor­
mation is available. Golson accepts Smith's conclusion that a nearby 
ahu, Niho-pou, had an Early Period platform (1965:50).
The stone slab seawall construction of the ahu Englert calls 
Ature Huki, the one whose single statue Heyerdahl re-erected 
(Skjolsvold 196ld:372, Plate 60 and 6l), may indicate an Early Period 
type structure. Again, the lack of subsurface archaeology limits the 
conclusions that can be drawn. Lavachery illustrates this ahu and he 
calls it Ahu Anakena 5 (195^:1^6).
Ahu Ohae, Englert's number 101, may also contain a small 
Early Period unit (Smith 196la:210). This suggestion is based solely 
on the appearance of the seawall, in which some upright slabs are vis­
ible. The entire ahu is now only 8 .H meters long by 5 to 5-5 meters 
wide. The foundation of flat slabs under four large centrally located, 
vertical seawall slabs fitted by selection shows some similarities to 
other Early Period ahu. The upright slabs are 80 to 90 centimeters 
wide, 90 to llfO centimeters high (Smith 1961a: Fig. 60) . The wing of 
the later Middle Period stage may be buried by soil erosion on the 
west end of the central platform. The lack of a wing on the east end 
would be an unusual feature for the Middle Period. However, no con­
clusive evidence can be provided to demonstrate that this ahu had an 
Early Period structure.
Although there is definite evidence for two stages of construc­
tion at Ahu te Pito Kura on the north coast (Englert, Ahu Oparo, and 
Thomson, No. 
1891:505-506), there appears to be little evidence to

support Smith's conclusion that the first structure was of his Early- 
Period Ahu Moroki style. A somewhat sloppy rebuilding seems to be 
associated with the erection of the large statue, Oparo, and this 
rebuilding of the seawall includes a statue torso of Rano Raraku tuff 
but the adjacent head appears to be of Middle Period style rather than 
earlier. This, then, would indicate a rebuilding in the Middle Period 
of an earlier but nevertheless Middle Period ahu. Smith notes the 
change of precision in stone carving; he compares the earlier stones of 
the seawall to the cut and fit stones of Vinapu 1. It does not, how­
ever, appear to be of the same antiquity as Ahu Tepeu, or Ahu 
Vinapu 2.
Architectural stratigraphy is present in the north coast ahu 
Hekii 1 (Smith 196la:184-189), but the date of the earliest structure 
is not clear. It would seem to be of the Middle Period. It is quite 
well preserved so that underlying architectural features are not 
exposed. Only surface mapping has been done (Smith 196la: Fig. 50).
The length of the structure is about 8l meters, and the seawall is 
about 5 meters high. The prehistoric remodeling of the northwest end 
of the seawall incorporated part of the torso of a statue but the 
torso's features are unknown. 
Smith concludes (l96la:l89) that the 
earliest stage of Hekii 1 was built during the Middle Period.
Two other notable examples of Early Period statues included 
in Middle Period reconstruction of an ahu are Mai taki te Moa on the 
west coast and Tongariki on the southeast coast. The large upright 
slabs forming the seawall of Mai taki te Moa as well as the basalt 
statue that was used in adding to or restoring the seawall facade 
(Routledge 1919: Fig. k2; Heyerdahl 1961: Plate 68b) are evidence for
L
