an Early Period stage here. The red tuff head used as a foundation 
stone in the central platform seawall of Ahu Tongariki (Thomson I89I: 
507), which is primarily a Middle Period structure, is also statuary 
presumably associated with an earlier ahu.
The distribution of ahu with definite evidence of Early Period 
style masonry and statuary indicates an island-wide occurrence of this 
feature of religious ceremonialism. Further evidence gathered during 
the author's field research will be discussed below.
Ahu Akivi
Mulloy excavated and restored Ahu Akivi in i960, an ahu with 
seven statues standing on a Middle Period platform. This is the larg­
est inland ahu known, located 2 .2  kilometers inland from 
the west
coast, and one of 
the few that had a, COux "o pO sitioned on 
the seaward
side of the statues. According to Mulloy, an earlier platform beneath 
the Middle Period 
ahu may have had some upright slabs on 
its surface
(Emory 1970:9l). 
The plaza-side retaining wali of the Middle Period
central platform was of fitted but uncut stones; the rear wall, nor­
mally the "seawall," was of cut stone slabs with their long axes 
placed horizontally. The wings were similar to other Middle Period 
ahu like Vinapu 2.
Information on the architecture has not been published; how­
ever, dates for the Middle Period construction A.D. lh60 - 100 and 
for cremations in cists behind the central platform A.D. 1380 -  100 
and A.D. l ^ O  - 100 (corrected dates, Appendix A) are available.
These dates fit in well with other dated ahu activity; the Middle 
Period ahu is the latest known ahu construction.

A smaller ahu platform with a single, headless statue located 
about .7 kilometer to the west of Ahu Akivi was also examined. A date

as Mulloy suggests (William Mulloy, Personal communication).
Research in 1968
Archaeological research conducted by the author in 1968 con­
sisted of two parts: the excavation and restoration of ahu at the
Tahai complex one kilometer north of the present village of Hangaroa 
and an archaeological site survey of the area around this site com­
plex. Both survey and excavation provide information regarding ahu 
change.
Survey
The survey provides evidence of early architectural features 
similar to those previously discussed. The intensive archaeological 
survey program used maps which divided the island into 32 quadrangles. 
The Tahai region is in Quadrangle 8 . An area of 600 by 575 meters was 
surveyed around the site complex; 166 sites were located and described 
(Figure 6 ). Eight of the structures, sites 8-l6 , 8-66, 8-71, 8-8l,
8-119, 8 -120, 8-121, and 8-13^ all may have been some type of ahu 
structure, in addition to 8-1 , 8-2 , 8-3 , the primary coastal ahu which 
were excavated. Of the above sites, 8-66 and 8-13^ are of greatest 
interest because each exhibits some characteristics that appear to be 
early; these two ahu also appear to be similar to, and possibly con­
temporaneous with, the buried ahu, Tahai I, which was excavated and 
dated to A.D. 690 ± 130. All three of these have upright stone slabs

Heyerdahl examined ahu 8-13^ in 195& and gives a description 
of its condition at that time (l96lb:U6U):
Still another group of three aberrant statues of other 
than Rano Raraku stone were encountered inside a fenced field 
in the northern outskirts of Hanga-roa village, not far from 
the modern cemetery. The area appears to be unexplored, and 
the statues not previously reported. One complete statue, 
about 2 .5  meters long, lay face down and partly buried in the 
ground, whereas two others were completely broken, and only 
scattered fragments, including two heads, were discovered in 
the neighborhood. The site of these statues was an artifi­
cially elevated earthen terrace with coarse masonry retaining 
walls, and a row of large, irregularly eroded blocks set on 
end projected above one side of the terrace as if it were a 
crude section of a raised marae wall (pi. 67b). Immediately 
to the east of the earthen terrace, and right at its base, 
was a cluster of completely overgrown, circular and contiguous 
houses of the type identified by Skjolsvold (this vol. 
pp. 295-303) and Ferdon (ibid. pp. 305-311) as prehistoric 
dwelling sites. . . .
There was no ahu [ Presumably Heyerdahl means there was no 
standardized image ahu here. ] in the vicinity, and the heavy, 
unbroken statue was probably overturned on the spot. Presum­
ably then, all three statues formerly stood on the elevated 
ground next to the raised row of thick and heavy slabs. . . .
The three statues were carved from coarse red scoria, 
probably quarried in the crater of Puna Pau, which was only 
about two kilometers away. The overthrown statue was re­
erected . . . .
I believe these three statues to be early, like the demolished ahu with 
which they are associated. Heyerdahl also describes a quarry area on 
the exterior slopes of the volcanic cone Tuu (also known as Otuu or 
Tuu-tapu) which contains several unfinished small crude statues carved

A second ahu thought to be early was located within the Tahai 
survey area, site 8-66 (Fig. 7b), called ahu Mahanua by Englert (191*8 : 
516), which has a seawall of large, cut and fitted upright stone 
slabs. This ahu is unusual in that the retaining wall facing the 
plaza consists of large upright slabs of almost the same height and 
shape as those in the seawall, and in that the platform is only about 
3 meters wide. The author knows of no other examples of this type of 
architecture on Easter Island. Only four of the large uprights are 
now standing in the seawall, two large ones had been tipped out, but 
the original wall consisted of at least these six. Evidence of wing 
extensions, so characteristic of the later Expansion Phase (A.D. 1000- 
1680) (Middle Period) ahu are not clearly evident at this ahu, 
although some mounds of stones indicate that it may have had them at

Although precise determination of the orientation of these two

Excavations: Ahu Ko te Kiku
The survey revealed that the major ceremonial area centered 
around the three coastal ahu, Ko te ahu o te Vaka Ariki (site 8-1,
here referred to as Ahu Ko te Eiku), Ko te ahu o te Nga’ara Arika
(site 8-2, here referred to as Ahu Tahai), and Ahu Vai Uri (site 8-3) 5 
which have been excavated and restored. The name "Ahu Tahai" has been 
used elsewhere to refer to site 8-2, by Englert (19^8:516), although 
this name may have derived from the term "Tahai" which applies more 
generally to the geographic area, rather than specifically to the 
structure. Thomson (l891:500-501) calls Ahu Vai Uri (site 8-3)
"Tahai." Englert (19^8:516-517) refers to site 8-1 by the number 16, 
and he refers to Ahu Vai Uri by name and gives it number li+. Ahu
Tahai, site 8-2, is number 15 in his survey of ahu structures.
The cluster of at least seventeen boat-shaped house founda­
tions lying in a semicircle about 50 to 70 meters landward of the 
three-ahu complex is a further indication of the central position the 
ahu complex occupied in this section of the coast (see Fig. 6).
L 
j
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 69 ===

These boat-shaped house foundations were previously interpreted as 
villages and appear to date back to the Expansion Phase (cf. Mulloy 
196la:135-1^6, Smith 1961a:189, Ferdon 196la:333, etc.).
As noted in the summary of published archaeological evidence, 
very little information is available regarding the Settlement and 
Developmental Phase ahu (ca. A.D, UOO-llOO), but the available evidence 
suggests a developmental pattern. One of the major goals of the exca­
vation program was to search for and to expose earlier stages of con­
struction underlying statue bearing phases of the ahu at Tahai.
The previously discussed excavations did not reveal an associ­
ation of statues, a characteristic feature of the image ahu for the 
Expansion Phase (A.D. 1000-1680), with ahu of earlier time levels. 
Excavations conducted at Tahai, specifically those at Ahu Tahai (site 
8-2), provide some further data, but the first ahu excavated, site 8-1 , 
had only one building phase.
Actual work on the first ahu, "Ko te Riku" (site 8-l), began in 
December 19673 when Gonzalo Figueroa G.H. of the Universidad de Chile 
conducted preliminary excavations and re-erected the statue in 
research financed by Paris Match magazine. I am grateful to him for 
allowing me to use his notes and the data recovered from those excava­
tions. He determined the original position of the Expansion Phase 
statue, which had fallen over backwards toward the sea, from l) struc­
tural evidence found in profiles of a centrally located trench,
Trench GF 1 (Fig. 8 , 9 ), 2) from an old photo depicting the statue ped­
estal in its nearly original position, and 3) from the location and' 
position of the statue at the time of excavation. 
Subsequent restora­
tion was done under my direction. In contrast to most other image

ahu, Ko te Riku was not covered with a mantle of loose stone, although

central platform from the Tahai cemetery where they had been used in 
recent monuments. These slabs were used in the reconstruction.
Trenches

Trench 2, parallel to Trench 1, 8 meters to the north and cov­
ering the same length, was designed to provide further data on the 
nature of wing fill and to examine a sunken spot or depression about 
k meters square in the center of the northern end of the wing. Ini­
tially it was suspected that the depression resulted from an intrusive 
tomb. The trench stripping exposed flat paving stones to the west of 
the depression and two water-worn paving stones, poro, on the inland 
or ramp side of the wing. Charcoal and three large cut stones were 
found in the central depression of the wing (Fig. 12b). The stones 
and charcoal were embedded in rough fill stones. In contrast to 
Trench 1 where, as indicated in the profile, the gravel apparently 
surrounded the poro paving stones Trench 2 revealed very little 
gravel on the wing ramp. After the three paengas were exposed in the 
sides of Trench 2 and the trench was extended in depth well below the 
level of the fill, excavations were extended around the paengas to see 
if they were part of a tomb or were simply side stones of a large umu. 
This excavation was called Trench 1+ and will be subsequently discussed.

25.75 meters North clearly showed the sides of the pit dug into the 
fill for the firepit. Its relation to the rock fill of the wing is 
also seen in Fig. 1 5 . A charcoal sample, no. 5? and a human bone 
fragment were taken from the bottom of the firepit. The corrected 
sample age is A.D. 10U0-1310 at one standard deviation (Ayres 1973-:
1+99) which appears to be anomalous because it should post-date sample 
number 3 (A.D. 1010-1305) taken from the wing fill in Trench 1. If 
the upper end of the range, i.e., near A.D. 1300 is correct, it then 
provides a terminal date for use of the ahu.
A large mound of rock and soil was recorded during the survey 
near the end of the north wing of Ko te Riku. This mound was assumed 
to be a tomb, so exploratory excavations were undertaken. A line of 
rock on the east side was originally thought to be one of four plat­
form walls, but upon investigation no wall could be discerned except 
one on the west side, 80 centimeters high and 1 .60 meters long on the 
stone outcrop. Some paving stones were apparent after stripping the 
surface of the mound. These features indicated that the mound was 
paved and dome-shaped rather than rectangular. A 1.50 meter square 
pit was started in the top center of the mound, in which decomposed 
red scoria, and a poro, as well as a few obsidian flakes, were found 
in the first 15 centimeter level. Solid rock outcrop filled the entire 
square at a depth of 60 centimeters. After this discovery, it was 
decided to proceed with the excavation to either side (east and west) 
of the square in Mound 1 to provide a full trench profile (Trench 5) 
through the mound. Charcoal sample No. 8 was collected on the outcrop 
1.00 meter below the surface of the mound. A medium-sized incised

firepit with the ahu is unclear; it shows no real relationship other 
than that its depth may indicate it was contemporaneous with the ahu.

North Wing. 
Stripping around the end and rear of the north 
wing to determine the extent of buried pavement and to define the rear 
border of the wing was undertaken after re-filling Trench 1 and 
Trench 2. The paving stones shown in the excavation plan view 
(Fig. 11) were uncovered. Evidence for a low curbing which defined the 
rear side of the wing, similar to that on the surface at the very end
of the wing, was found. The pavement at the rear of the north wing 
was buried in places 15 to 20 centimeters deep, and some small poro 
gravel was scattered over the pavement. The wing surface was not 
stripped entirely, because the surface had slumped and the paving was 
largely missing.
Stripping behind the rear wing exposed two pocket cremations 
20 centimeters below the ground surface at the south edge of a large 
exposed stone slab extending above the level of the original paving.
The burned bone fragments were in two pits about 20 centimeters in 
diameter and 20 centimeters deep. Rocks were mixed in with the mass 
of bone fragments. One of the cremations (lk) included some small 
charcoal pieces and one large obsidian core. Both of these crema­
tions are evidence for late activity at the ahu.
The only feature uncovered in the north wing excavations 
(Trench k) was the large firepit surrounded on the south, east, and 
west by large, cut paengas. The pit caused a slumping in the central 
portion of the wing as well as displacing the poro paving stones on 
the original surface of the wing. Original paving stones were located 
only at j.2 meters West at the west side of Trench 4. These flat 
stones formed the edge of the flat stone paving in the rear and north 
end of the wing, and separated it from the poro paving rows on the top

r

centimeters deep (see plan view on map of Ko te Riku, Fig. ll). The 
extended burial (l.80 meters long), oriented with the head towards the 
east, had been covered with boards and mahute (tapa cloth) before the 
soil was replaced in the pit. The boards showed traces of paint and 
some contained nails, thus indicating that the burial was historic and 
that the boards had been part of a coffin. Copper nails also indi­
cated that the boards may have come from a ship. Ko disturbance of 
the burial was apparent and the skeleton was intact except for one 
ulna found 25-30 centimeters above the level of the burial. Resi­
dents of Hangaroa were concerned about disturbing what appeared to be 
a recent burial so the bones were not collected and no further 
descriptions of the physical characteristics of the skeleton were pos­
sible (other than by photographs). At the inhabitants' request the 
grave was refilled without further disturbance. Workmen considered 
this skeleton to be "Vaka Ariki," the person who, according to tra­
ditional accounts, had the ahu built. One of his wives, Veriamo 
Apalapala, died only two or three years before Father Sebastian 
Englert arrived in 1935, which indicates that this person (Vaka Ariki) 
is quite recent and probably not associated with the actual construc­
tion of the ahu. He may, however, have been associated with the ahu 
as land owner in more recent times.

Reconstructions
The most difficult reconstruction, moving and raising the 5.2 
meter tall statue to its original position, was performed under the 
direction of Gonzalo Figueroa in January, 1968. Subsequent restora­
tion was done by Mulloy and Ayres. Figueroa used a welded pipe frame 
around the statue and a large crane (the one used later in our recon­
structions) to lift the statue up from behind the central platform, to 
turn it around, and finally to stand it upright on the pedestal. A 
slight amount of cement was used under the pedestal and under the base 
of the statue for stabilization. A small depression was carved into 
the base of the statue before it was raised so that it could rest on a 
thin pad of nearly invisible cement. The topknot of the statue was 
carved under the direction of Figueroa especially for the reconstruc­
tion because the original, if there had been one, was not discovered. 
It is probable that the original topknot was carved up into smaller 
pieces in historic times. The topknot was placed on top of the statue 
with the crane after the statue was erect and stable.
before leaving the island was to place the initial paenga of the 
inland wall in front of the statue. The positioning of the paenga was 
based upon information from the Trench GF 1, particularly on the frag­
ment of red scoria on the ramp surface that Figueroa interpreted as 
part of a cornice surmounting the original paenga line. The recon­
structed paenga line is just behind the scoria slabs and reflects the 
contention that they fell in that position after being knocked off the 
top of the upright paengas. Additional excavations conducted by the 
author substantiated this alignment of the inland retaining wall.
The only other reconstruction Figueroa had time to complete
L

=== PAGE 100 ===

r
851
Because Ahu Ko te Riku was a single component ahu, that is, 
a single Expansion Phase construction, the reconstruction was of that 
same single structure (Fig. 25, 26, 27). Becaus: of the proximity of 
the ahu complex to the modem village of Hangaroa and its proximity to 
the "bay, the state of preservation was very poor. This greatly 
increased the amount of time necessary for rebuilding as opposed to 
stabilizing or re-adjusting. The magnitude of the rebuilding is best 
seen in the reconstructed bay retaining walls (Fig. 25). About half 
of the original ten meter high retainer was found in place. The south 
wing of the ahu and most of the central platform were almost com­
pletely demolished. 
Sufficient remnants were uncovered through exca­
vation to allow a fairly judicious conjectural reconstruction.
Restoration work began on the central platform because the 
statue had been re-erected and a trench had been put through it. As 
noted previously, a section of the seaward retaining wall was still 
intact. This allowed accurate restoration of that portion of the cen­
tral platform. The north end of the central platform was indicated by 
the termination of the foundation stones of the remaining seawall.
The south seawall corner of the central platform was indicated by a 
projection of bedrock that had been leveled off. No stones were 
found in place that were definitely foundations of either end wall of 
the central platform. Because all paengas (except Figueroa's initial 
one) had been taken from the ahu for use in construction elsewhere, 
no clear indication of the length of the inland retaining wall was at 
hand. The recovery of the paengas from the cemetery that were known 
to have been taken from Ko te Riku in historic times, as well as the 
recognition of a fairly rectangular shape for central platforms,

Removal of rock from the two large stone piles at the sea side 
of the ahu led to discovering foundation stones of a retaining wall at 
the southwest corner of the ahu (retaining wall 1 South, Fig. 11, 25). 
One worker, Juan Haoa, also recalled seeing the remains of a low 
retaining wall just north of the one rediscovered. This was no longer 
visible, but rock cleared in the second, more northerly, pile exposed 
a line of stones taken to be the foundation line of a retaining wall. 
These walls were restored as illustrated in the reconstructed plan

The north wing was in comparatively good condition except for 
the pit that had been dug into the center. The north border was 
largely intact as was a large part of the paving behind the wing. The 
curbstone border of the rear of the wing was completely absent and 
therefore its reconstruction is conjectural. The surface of the wing

reconstruction because it was not part of the original structure. The 
step reconstructed in the south wing ramp was located by projection of 
the few remaining wall stones at the extreme north end of the ramp step 
and trench excavation (see Fig. 25, 26). The step feature also occurs 
in other ahu on the island. The south wing end wall is part of the 
longer and larger upper bay retaining wall that forms the south side 
of the entire plaza. The location of the bay retainer was derived 
from projections of the remaining foundation of the original wall. 
Evidence for the ramp walkway leading from Ahu Tahai up behind Ko te 
Riku was recognizable in the remnants of the stepped retaining wall, 
of which a considerable portion of the lower end was in place; in the 
stone alignments at the start of the ramp behind Ahu Tahai; in the 
corner of the ramp walkway which was largely preserved at the south­
west corner of the south wing of Ko te Riku; and in segments of out­
crop behind the south wing which had been prepared to receive wall- 
stone s of the sloping walkway.
As indicated in the excavation plan view (Fig. ll), a consid­
erable amount of the stone paving in the south part of the court of 
Ko te Riku was still in position after the surface turf was stripped 
off. A portion of the paving stones had been recently removed (10- 
15 years ago) by the Chilean Air Force for use in construction. The 
large blank space in the upper central part of the paved area reflects 
this removal. The pavement area covers primarily the location of fill 
used to level the south side of the plaza. The pavement thus served 
to stabilize the rock and earth fill, an engineering technique widely 
used on Easter Island. This paving was restored by using rocks simi­
lar in size to the ones that still remained. The paving clearly shows

=== PAGE 107 ===

r 
m
the relationship between the two ahu, Tahai and Ko te Riku. The cre­
mation pit on the plaza was paved over in the reconstruction and the 
box with skull (removed) was left with a single paving stone as a 
cover.
The stone bordered ring in the central location on the plaza 
was also restored. Remnants of this stone line were visible before 
stripping the turf. Stripping disclosed a central disturbance which 
turned out to be an historic grave. It is not entirely clear if the 
original stones were remnants of a paina circle, that is, a foundation 
for the paina figure used in ceremonies on ahu, but the size and loca­
tion are in accord with what is known of their size and distribution.
The large mound at the north end of the ahu was re-paved as a 
dome, except in the rear where the mound and outcrop projection met, 
since no indication of side walls was found. Ko other special archi­
tectural features were reconstructed for this mound. Trench 6 , which 
disclosed a firepit at the north end of the ahu, was simply refilled.
The jumbled pile of stones at the northwest side of the ahu, 
behind the north wing, was probably a crematorium, but test excava­
tions did not reveal evidence of burned bones and time limitations 
did not allow full excavation. It was not known how to restore the 
structure so it was not rebuilt.
Excavations: Ahu Tahai
Excavations conducted by the author in the second of three 
ahu at Tahai, called Ko te Ahu o te Nga'ara Ariki or Ahu Tahai, led 
to the discovery of an early structure underlying the image ahu. This 
underlying structure, which can be called an ahu because of its
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 108 ===

r
95l
architecture, was dated by Cl4 at A.D. 690 ± 130 (1260 -  130 BP) and 
provides evidence, both in terms of location and architecture, for con­
tinued use of the area as a ceremonial center with ahu for at least
1000 years (A.D. 690 - A.D. 1680).
After initial mapping of this site (8-2) at the head of Tahai 
Bay, trenches were laid out in several locations to determine the 
nature of construction, type of fill used, and to search for underly­
ing architectural features (Fig. 28, 29, 30). 
The seawall of upright
stone slabs suggested that, if Smith's conclusions were correct
(1961a:213-214), it may have had an early stage.

of decomposed red scoria and water-washed soil ranging in thickness 
between 5 ana 10 centimeters. This is indicative of a period of aban­
donment and probable deliberate destruction of the earlier ahu,
Tahai I, before Tahai II was built. Eadiocarbon dates indicate a span 
of at least 300 years from the time of construction of Tahai I to con­
struction of Tahai II. There is no clear terminal date for use of 
Tahai I . The architectural features of a central platform for 
Tahai I were not readily observable, but there was a definite mound

in Trench 1 was originally in the inland retaining wall of this ahu 
platform. A small curb stone of red scoria was found at the west 
edge of the poro paving of Tahai I and it presumably marks the inland 
edge of the south wing.

The ramp paving and the wing of Tahai II were visible after 
stripping and fill layer 1 was clearly differentiated from the water- 
laid layer 2 below it. The interface between this layer 2 and the 
wing surface of Tahai I was considerably more difficult to discern, 
at least in the western end where a depression had been filled in to 
form a nearly level northern extension of the court and wing. The 
evidence for paving seems inconclusive and it seems strange that no 
poro paving stones were found in the trench at the level of the inter­
face. A careful examination of the trench walls, however, indicated 
a number of stones at the rear side of the north wing of Tahai I which 
were interpreted as remnants of paving. These stones are indicated on 
the profile (D-D1, Fig. 35) and the plan view (Fig. 36).
Trench profiles and exploratory excavations allow a conjec- 
tursJ reconstruction of the first structure, Tahai I (Fig. 36). The 
surface details of the later Tahai II are better known, but the basic 
outline of the early ahu is also evident. The major elements of con­
tinuity are: the seawall of upright, trimmed slabs; the central plat­
form; wing extensions; and the poro pavement on the landward side of 
the central platform and wings.

r

Due to the demolished state of the south wing of Tahai II, 
remnants of the earlier wing corner were visible on the surface as 
illustrated in the excavation plan view. This remnant consisted of 
one large rounded upright rock fitted against a smaller upright slab 
of stone with a small wedge-shaped patch that was carved to fit in 
between the two at the top. This wall foundation marker was inside 
the line of slabs on edge that formed the eastern extreme of the 
Tahai II wing wall. The location of this corner indicated it was 
part of the earlier structure rather than the corner of the south 
wing of later Tahai II. Exploratory excavations into the rubble 
overlying the canoe pavement exposed more paving stones to the north 
of the line of slabs that marked the south end of the Tahai II wing 
but these paving stones did not extend beyond the postulated end wall 
of the Tahai I wing. These two features establish the length of the 
Tahai I south wing. Unfortunately, there was little indication of 
the location of the seaside wall of the south wing except for the 
wing corner and the seawall of the central platform. The poro paving 
stones uncovered in Trench 1 establish that the inland wing surface 
was paved with rows of poro. The one remaining slab curb stone at 
the west edge of the poro pavement appears to have been part of a 
line of curb stones extending along the front edge of the paved wing. 
All of the wing had been disturbed south of this curb stone. This 
poro pavement was at the same level as the court of Tahai I, and 
there was no special marker at its eastern edge; however, the last 
row of poro was clearly defined in Trench 1 and 3 by the poro still 
in place. Trench 3 exposed only a few stones of this pavement; the 
gaps in the rows as well as the total absence of poro paving in the

northern part of the trench indicated that the stones had heen removed 
after the early ahu was no longer in use.
central platform was in line with the curb stone of red scoria defin­
ing the south wing. One indication is the line of the westernmost row 
of poro stones which, if projected, would have run directly in front 
of the retaining wall as can be seen from Fig. 36. The curb stone was 
taken to be the north end of the wing's curb boundary because it was 
directly opposite the comer upright of the seawall of the central 
platform. The paenga found lying flat over the poro paving appears to 
have been one of the slabs forming the inland retaining wall. This 
paenga was removed during the destruction of the ahu and was dragged 
about 3 meters east of the original wall. This again indicates delib­
erate destruction or dismantling of the early structure, probably for 
building materials to be used elsewhere. Other paengas may lie under 
the unexcavated area west of Trench 3* The height of the discovered 
paenga agrees approximately with the height of the seawall of Tahai I. 
In the profile of Trench 1 no evidence was seen for the foundation of 
the south end wall of a central platform of Tahai I . Excavations were 
not conducted in the vicinity of the north end of the central platform 
to the depth of the Tahai I level, but the end of the seawall clearly 
establishes the north end of the central platform. The height of the 
northern-most seawall paenga, 5-95 meters above sea level, is approxi­
mately the same as that of an inland retaining wall consisting of 
slabs on edge, like the one discovered, based slightly below the poro 
paved surface of the Tahai I court. It seems reasonable to conclude, 
then, that the seawall was built up to the height of the tallest slab

on the north end by fitting smaller cut stones, probably placed hori­
zontally rather than vertically, on top of the shorter, upright slabs. 
The top of the central platform was probably paved with cut and fitted 
stones ("capstones") as indicated in the reconstructed plan view. The 
paving stones abutting the inland wall of paengas would have presum­
ably been seated into the champfer on the upper rear edge of these 
stones. There was, of course, no indication whether the Tahai I 
structure had a statue on the central platform because only the south­

overlying the poro pavement exposed in Trench 1 could be in part from 
an erodir g statue of red scoria. A further possible indication of a 
statue on the Tahai I platform comes from a head of a red scoria 
statue found in the bay (Fig. 37). Mulloy found a large rectangular 
block of red scoria in the bay in his excavations at Tahai in 1970 
which could have been the body of the statue. This block was carved 
to fit into the wharf edge, an architectural feature associated with 
Tahai II and Ahu Ko te Riku. The head must have been included in the 
fill of the south wing of Tahai II during its construction and ended 
up in the bay when the south wing of that structure was destroyed.
Little information regarding the extent or the nature of the 
north wing was found beyond a few stones that appear to have been 
paving stones and an earlier soil horizon. No undisturbed remnants 
of the poro paving were found. That there was a north wing is 
strongly suggested by the definite existence of a south wing. The 
extension of poro stones to the south of the central platform implies 
that the paving originally extended to the north also. The absence of 
undisturbed poro is not conclusive evidence for the absence of a wing,

because poro were also absent in front of the northern half of the 
central platform. This indicates that they had been deliberately 
removed, probably for use elsewhere. The level of several flat 
stones exposed in Trench 3 and 1+ suggests some sort of paving that 
defined a north wing. It was a level extension of the court.
Surface Stripping
Surface stripping provided evidence for a number of features 
of Tahai II. An upright slab near the inner south end of the central 
platform marked the original location of an inland paenga retaining 
wall, but no other indications of paengas were found; thus the inland 
retaining wall was tentatively reconstructed of fitted stones. The 
stone foundation line of the north end wall of the central platform of 
Tahai II was visible on the surface. The south end wall of the plat­
form was completely missing, so the reconstructed wall is only a pro­
jection from the end of the seawall to the line of the inland retaining 
wall.
Stripping the inland surface of the ahu exposed the ramp or 
apron pavement as seen in the excavation plan view (Fig. 30). Suffi­
cient numbers of the poro rows remained to permit a judicious recon­
struction of that area of paving. A small pocket cremation was found 
while stripping the ramp surface at the edge of the central platform. 
Stripping the landward court area of Ahu Tahai exposed the original 
court surface which was covered in many places with charcoal at a 
depth of 20 centimeters below the present surface. These excavations 
also disclosed a jumble of rock and boat-shaped house foundation 
stones at the north central edge of the court. What was initially
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 130 ===

r
n¥l
considered to "be a closed off cave month at the northwest corner of 
the court was found to be only a depression in the outcrop ridge. The 
east end of the court was not defined by excavation and it is probable 
that it never was clearly demarcated. There is some suggestion that 
parts of the court may have been paved, but time did not allow further 
testing.
Reconstructions of Tahai II
The reconstruction of the image ahu Tahaii II (Fig. 38, 39) 
joined with the reconstructed Ahu Ko te Riku represent two thirds of 
a major ceremonial complex. The third ahu, Ahu Vai Uri, was restored 
by Mulloy in 1970. The remains of Tahai I were buried under the 
reconstruction.
The first step in reconstruction was the re-erection of the h.i)-
meter tall statue. The pedestal rested nearly in its original position 
on an intact central core of large boulders (Fig. 1+0) and only needed 
to be shifted slightly to fit into its original seat. The base of the 
statue, like that of the Ko te Riku image, was chiseled out to a depth 
of 5 centimeters to allow it to rest on a pad of cement. A large 
crane, used by courtesy of the Chilean Air Force, raised the statue 
directly to a vertical position after shifting the head to the north, 
in line with the pedestal, and resting the base on the front edge of 
the pedestal. Ko topknot was found for the statue. 
Several workmen 
reported that pieces of red scoria were lying on the ground in the 
vicinity which could have been remnants of a topknot. The statue was 
vertically aligned using plumb lines located about 50 to 70 meters 
away.

=== PAGE 135 ===

Reconstruction of the central platform of the ahu began with 
repairs on the vertical cut stone seawall to reinforce the foundation 
slabs. The large, slightly wedge-shaped stones used for rebuilding 
the short retaining wall set in from the seawall (Fig. 38) were all 
discovered in the rock pile in the bay, except one which had been 
reused in the makeshift wall of the south wing. It was assumed that 
these were the original wall stones, since they were the only ones 
found which formed a set and were tall enough to have formed this 
rear retaining wall which supported the statue pedestal and which, 
when fitted together, formed a long enough wall (10 meters). That 
they were cut or worked may indicate that they were taken from an 
earlier structure, possibly Tahai I. Upright stones were carved to 
fit on top of the lower seawall slabs; the upper ends of these slabs 
had been cut originally to receive fitted stones to level the top of 
the wall. Irregular stones, marking the north end of the central 
platform, were exposed by excavation. This wall was reconstructed by 
fitting stones, like those in the foundation, into a low, compact 
retaining wall. The same type of wall was built at the south end of 
the platform, extending from the inset seawall to the line of the 
inland retaining wall. The inland retaining wall directly in front of 
the statue was reconstructed in the same manner. The heights of the 
central platform retaining walls were determined by the pedestal ele­
vation and the height of the paengas forming the inset wall on the sea 
side.
As seen in the excavation plan view, the surface stripping of 
the north wing exposed most of the original paving, including the 
nearly intact borders. Enough of the undisturbed poro paving stones
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 136 ===

r 
U9i
were found to allow reconstruction of the patterned lines of stones 
and to indicate the extent of their placement to the north on the wing 
surface. The northern-most poro stones exposed in Trench 3 were at 
11.20 meters North. A narrow gap was observed between the paving of 
the wing ramp and the paving of the rear side of the wing which was 
attached to the court paving of Ahu Ko te Riku. The reason for this 
gap is unknown, but it may have been the location of a line of curb­
stones.
Fortunately, much of the pavement of the nearly level ramp 
face or apron of Tahai II was still in place. Only at the inland 
edge of the demolished south wing did the border disappear. A pave­
ment section was found intact under the cement and stone water tank 
which was constructed on top of the south wing in the 19^0's. Two 
paengas which could have come from either Tahai I or Ko te Riku, but 
probably from the latter, were found embedded in the north wall of 
the tank.
The major Tahai reconstruction was rebuilding the south wing 
wall which stood h .5 meters high at the southwest corner. The fitted 
stone wall abutting the upright corner slab of the central platform in 
Fig. 30 was considered to be part of the original Tahai II wing wall.
The remainder of the wall is a more recent stabilization built when a 
windmill was constructed. The crane was effective in pulling the 
large original wall rocks from the bay where they had been deposited 
by the sea and in placing them into a wall of fitted but uncut pieces. 
Extensive excavations around the comer of the south wing disclosed 
several large slabs which apparently stood upright at the corner, about 
1.3 to 1.5 meters out from the shorter south wing corner of Tahai I.
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 137 ===

r 
i2oi
These slabs were embedded in a trench to provide a firm, sea-resistant 
base for the exposed corner. No evidence remained of the surface of 
the south wing; it was conjecturally reconstructed to be the same as 
the north wing.
Excavations into the rubble at the end of the south wing dis­
closed that the pavement of the "canoe ramp" was laid down when 
Tahai I was constructed and was used later as part of the Tahai II 
complex. The only way to get down to the bay or to take canoes out 
would have been over this ramp. The flat stone paving was still in 
place (Fig. 30) except for near the water's edge. Only part of this 
ramp paving was restored in 1968 due to time limitations. Mulloy fin­
ished the reconstruction of this ramp and the wharf in 1970 (Fig. 38).
The court or plaza was changed very little in the restoration. 
Two large depressions in the south central side (the remnants of 
recent attempts to dig a well) were filled in. The north side of the 
court was stripped of 10 to 30 centimeters of accumulated water- 
deposited soil over the original court surface, indicated by some 
artifacts and burning. Stones visible on the south side of the court 
may have been remnants of pavement, but this is not certain, and it is 
most likely that the court was entirely unpaved and lacked clear 
boundaries except for a natural rock ridge on the north side and the 
edge of the Ahu Vai Uri court on the south.
A Developmental Sequence
As I have stated elsewhere (Ayres 1971)> the results of the 
Tahai research support in general the conclusions of Smith (l96la,
196lb) regarding three major periods of Easter Island ahu architecture.
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 138 ===

r
123
The Tahai -work and comparative research indicate that the change from 
Smith's Early Period ahu to the more standardized image ahu of the 
Middle Period "began earlier than 1100 A.D.; that the Early Period ahu 
may have had statues associated with them; and most importantly, that 
a cultural continuity from his Early Period through to the historic 
period is indicated in religious architecture.
Before expanding on these points, the terminology of "periods" 
for Easter Island prehistory needs to he reconsidered. Most archae­
ologists and prehistoricans recognize significant differences between 
the term "period" and other terms for chronological and spatial seg­
ments of prehistory. Rouse (1972:77) says: 
"Some southeastern pre­
historians simply refer to these peoples or cultures or both as 
'periods' (e.g. Ford 1962, Fig. 7) 5 but other prehistorians who are 
culturally more sophisticated prefer to use the term 'phase.'" Because 
we are attempting to characterize the cultural context of ahu and 
their changing forms through time, it is necessary to consider the 
implications of terms indicating change. Willey and Phillips (1958:
22) defined "phase" as one of their basic archaeological units:
. . .  an archaeological unit possessing traits sufficiently 
characteristic to distinguish it from all other units simi­
larly conceived, whether of the same or other cultures or 
civilizations, spatially limited to the order of magnitude 
of a locality or region and chronologically limited to a 
relatively brief interval of time.
In Easter Island prehistory we are dealing with a relatively short time 
span for the entire prehistoric sequence, about 1^00 years, and a spa­
tial unit of the magnitude of a region. The term "period" has served 
most often as a major chronological designation, such as great time
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 139 ===

r
122 ]
spans in world prehistory referring to major stages like "Food- 
Gathering," "Food-Producing," and "Urban-Life" (cf. Willey and Phillips 
1958:65, "chronological periods"). The term "phase," as defined by 
Willey and Phillips, has been used to characterize prehistoric units 
elsewhere in Polynesia by Golson (1959) 5 Duff (1968), by Sinoto (l97l), 
who used it in a slightly different sense, and by others. But because 
period designations are primarily chronological, they tell us very 
little about the culture of the people under discussion. A phase more 
precisely refers to the culture remains of a particular time and space 
under consideration. The lack of clarity in the use of these terms, as 
Willey and Phillips recognize (1958:65-68), results from unclear for­
mulations between culture and chronology in practically all archaeol­
ogical sequences. The social implications of the term "phase" need to 
be considered if the goal is to define the cultural context of the 
ahu use on Easter Island. Chang says that Willey and Phillips' dif­
ficulty in determining social equivalents for an archaeological unit, 
such as phase (Willey and Phillips 1958:50)5 stems from their identi­
fication of "phase" as their primary archaeological unit (1967:^0- 
Ul). Chang instead uses "settlement" as the primary archaeological 
unit, which is an archaeological reality for him; he attempts to find 
social dimensions for this reality. Rouse emphasizes that peoples 
are the primary units of prehistoric research and equates the idea of 
a "subpeople" with phase and states: 
"each subpeople has its own
distinctive phase of the culture" (1972:89, 9l)•
The goal to synthesize the data on Easter Island ahu may be 
accomplished by interpreting Easter Island prehistory in terms of

phases, defined primarily on the basis of our knowledge of one major 
non-portable artifact, the ahu.
Settlement and Developmental Phase (Early Period.) A.D. 1+00-1000 (?) 
Ahu
platform seawall built of upright slabs of basalt carved on two sides 
to fit closely together. 
Smaller rocks carved to fit intervening gaps 
formed a solid wall with a nearly level top. The end retaining walls 
of this platform, constructed of various size rock, enclose a fill of 
rubble. The inland retaining wall even of the earliest known struc­
ture consists of thin rectangular slab paengas laid end to end with 
perfectly fitted joints. Champfers on the inner top edge of the 
paengas serve as seats for capstones (paving stones) on the platform 
surface. The shift to large squared blocks of stone becomes increas­
ingly apparent late in this phase, perhaps to the total exclusion of 
the earlier style of architecture. The best example of this late 
architecture is Mulloy's Early Period at Vinapu 1. The wings of the 
early structures appear to have been basically flat pavements, extend­
ing out from either end of the central platform, but these wings 
increase in height through time; by the end of the phase they were 
generally rounded on the surface, backed by a retaining wall, and 
formed a sloping ramp on the inner or landward side. The ramp was 
paved with patterned rows of poro or beach stones beginning early in 
the phase (e.g. Tahai I), an architectural feature which continued 
through the Expansion Phase. Courts of the Settlement Phase struc­
tures are not well known, but the Vinapu evidence indicates that some
The ahu structures in this phase are characterized by a central
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 141 ===

were enclosed with soil embankments. Tahai I probably had some bound­
aries denoting the sides of the court. Courts were normally not 
paved.
The presence of statues or upright stone slabs on the ahu 
platform or court of the early structures remains in question. Mulloy 
suspects the presence of slabs on the earliest Ahu Akivi platform but 
no details are available (Emory 1970:91)- My interpretation of the 
Tahai evidence points to the use of comparatively small statues on 
this ahu by A.D. 700, but this is by no means conclusive. Several 
statues thought to be early were carved out of red scoria and in some 
cases hard basalt instead of Rano Raraku tuff which is characteristic 
of the highly stylized Expansion Phase statuary. My postulation of 
early statue use is based on the criteria of style, geography, stra­
tigraphy, size, and material composition.
If we accept vertically aligned seawall stones as character­
istic indications of early structures, then it is clear that there was 
an island-wide distribution of ahu during this phase, but these ahu 
were apparently concentrated in the western region. The early, 
unstylized statues carved of a variety of material (red scoria, basalt 
some Rano Rakaku tuff) are also found island-wide, but, again, concen­
trated in the west; this, however, may be in part a reflection of our 
greater archaeological knowledge of the western end of the island.
No architectural features are known other than ahu which can 
be dated prior to A.D. 1100; only one ahu structure can be directly 
dated (Tahai I) to earlier times. By association, of course, Ahu 
Vinapu 2, the Early Period structure, is dated at A.D. 900-950.
House forms are not known for the early phase. Eerdon suggested that

There are no definite archaeological indicators of social

Expansion or Ahu Moai Phase (Middle Period) A.D. 1000-1680 (?)
Ahu
The central platform seawall of the Expansion Phase ahu exhib­
its a considerable range in construction styles ranging from selected 
and fitted, but uncut, stones as in Ahu Ko te Riku, to the cut and 
fitted block masonry as in Ahu Tongariki, Mahatua, and Hekii in the 
east part of the island. The precise fitting of large upright slabs 
or horizontal blocks no longer appears, and this is seen in seawalls 
of Expansion Phase ahu as remnants of earlier architectural units, as 
at Vinapu 1 or 2 or Ahu Tahai. Mulloy observed in regard to the 
Vinapu ahu that the central platform and une entire ahu structure in 
the Expansion Phase appear to be designed for carrying large statues.

The end walls of the central platform are simple retaining walls with 
no special characteristics. The inland retaining walls exhibit con­
siderable variation: an irregular wall of small rocks, as at Ahu
Akivi; a line of cut paengas, as at Ko te Riku (an early ahu of the 
Expansion Phase); or a combination of the two, as at Vinapu. A cor­
rected date of A.D. li+55 - 100 for the construction of Ahu Akivi 
(Ayres 1971:^99) is the latest date for image ahu. There is no evi­
dence in these cases of surface paving of the central platform as in 
earlier times. The statue pedestals occupy the prominent central part 
of the platform. The wings reached their largest size and all, are 
paved in one form or another; poro paving continues to characterize 
the apron or sloping ramp in front of the ahu. The appearance of 
large quantities of Rano Raraku tuff and red scoria in the lower 
levels of the fill of the wings (as at Ko te Riku and Ahu Tahai, the 
only excavations from which information is available on the content 
of wing fill) indicates that the wings and the sloping ramp in front 
of the core platform were added after the statue had been put in 
place. I suspect that the increasing size of the wings is directly 
related to the increasing size of statues placed on the ahu, and the 
rock pile used in raising the statue was afterwards used as the major 
part of the wing fill. The Rano Raraku tuff beneath the wing fill 
indicates that finishing touches of the statues, carving the eyes, 
smoothing the back and so forth, took place at the ahu before the 
wings were fully completed and paved. The wings served as a way of 
getting rid of the huge pile of rock used as a platform in raising the 
statue. Fill was paved over by Easter Islanders as well as other

Polynesians to keep it in place. In Easter Island ahu the paving 
became more elaborate through time.
The courts of the Expansion Phase structures were often paved, 
particularly over man-made fill as at Ko te Riku or Ahu Vai Uri; they 
were always leveled either by filling or cutting away soil: however, 
there is no indication of attempts to enclose the court. Boundaries 
may have been natural, or defined by the edge of the fill and leveling 
of the court as at Ko te Riku.
Statues reached stupendous proportions during this phase, as 
indicated by the 30 foot tall statue "Paro" at Ahu Te Pito Kura and 
the unfinished 60 foot tall statue in the Rano Raraku quarry area.
All the statues on ahu exhibit a high degree of standardization, and 
at present one cannot even speculate on stylistic differences even 
between statues like those of Ko te Riku and Vinapu, the latter of 
which are about 300 years more recent. The technology of moving and 
erecting the statues is explained most clearly in Mulloy's recent 
article (1970) . He thinks the islander;: dragged the statues on a 
wooden sledge with the aid of a wooden bipod which would have raised 
the front end (head) of the statue as it was pulled. This method uti­
lized the weight of the elevated statue to aid in moving the statue 
forward. As with the Settlement Phase ahu, there is still no evidence 
of burial within the structure during the time of its construction.
All excavated skeletal material from Ahu Vinapu 1 and 2, Ahu Tepeu,
Ahu Te Pito Kura, Ahu Tahai, Ahu Akivi, Ahu Vai Uri, and Ahu Ko te

There are still few archaeological indicators of social organi­
zation in the Expansion Phase, but the knowledge regarding the settle­
ment pattern represents an increase over the earlier phase. The pic­
ture that emerges is one of a cluster of boat-shaped, thatched houses 
distributed in a semi-circular pattern on the landward side of an ahu 
or ahu complex; these complexes were most often located around bays 
that offered a landing place and a source of fresh water. The rela­
tionship of the other types of stone-walled dwelling structures to 
either the boat-shaped houses or ahu is unclear at the present time, 
although they were in general probably less permanent habitations.
This indicates a quite concentrated, permanent settlement in direct 
association with religious centers.
There is no increase in archaeological evidence for the fea­
tures of the Expansion Phase language. We may assume, due to isola­
tion, the development of a distinct Polynesian language by this time. 
Whether the rongo-rongo script and tablets were in use during this 
phase is unresolved. The rongo-rongo research of Fedorova, who

Osteological characteristics of the population of this phase

As far as is determined, the artifact classes of the Expansion

the flaked obsidian mata'a, which, as mentioned, may be represented by 
a few specimens in the Expansion Phase. This is the dominant and most 
characteristic artifact of the time and an indicator of increased con­
flict .

Protohistoric Phase (Late Period) A.D. 1722-1868
It is important to recognize the significant change resulting 
from initial foreign contact with Easter Island and to characterize 
it as a separate phase, even though we cannot clearly distinguish this 
archaeologically, except for the very occasional appearance of early 
trade items. Apparently European contact came at a time when the 
religious system of the island was in a state of flux as evidenced 
by the condition of ahu. Some ahu were still in use as described in 
the ethnographic section. The terminal date indicates the Christiani­
zation of the population.
Historic Phase A.D. 1868-
From the time of the first permanent European missionary in 
1864, little use was made of the ahu except for burial and occasional 
ceremonies like the paina which were held on or near the ahu. Bodies 
were exposed on ahu.
West Polynesian Mounds and Malae 
To better understand the developmental sequence proposed above 
and to compare Easter Island ahu to similar constructions that occur 
elsewhere in Polynesia, we should look at the archaeological evidence 
for such religious architecture in the West Polynesian islands where 
we have the earliest evidence for Polynesian occupation as well as 
the architectural forms which seem to indicate the wider distribution 
of marae. However, in West Polynesia religious associations with stone 
masonry and massive construction are limited to burials.
Major excavations in West Polynesia have focused on Tongatapu 
in the Tongan group (Poulsen 1964, 1968; Davidson 1964, 1965) and on
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 152 ===

r 
135I
Upolu in the Samoan group (Golson 1969; Green 1968; Green and 
Davidson 1965, 1969). Other West Polynesian islands. Uvea, Futuna,
Niue, and the Tokelau and Ellice groups, remain largely unexplored by 
archaeologi st s.
Excavations
Tonga
The first detailed discussion of the archaeological monuments 
of this island group is in McKern's monograph on Tongan archaeology 
published in 1929. This work offers a surface survey on Tongatapu, 
descriptions of the surface features of a large number of mounds and 
platforms, as well as some excavations. McKern made an initial dis­
tinction between round and rectangular platforms and mounds (1929:8); 
platforms, but not mounds, had upright, cut-slab retaining walls. He 
further distinguished four types of mounds or platforms: a) esi
mounds, b) pigeon mounds, c) house platforms, and d) grave mounds.
Of these, the esi mounds were used by individuals of high rank and 
the pigeon mounds were apparently used by high ranking individuals for 
snaring wild pigeons. Both of these types were normally circular in 
plain view and were faced with rough, small uncut stones. The house 
mounds were similar.
For our present discussion the grave mounds or platforms are 
of the greatest significance. Tongans grouped grave mounds into three 
types which reflected the social position of the person buried there: 
the tanuanga for an ordinary person; the faitoka for a chief, his 
family, and his principal retainers, and langi for a member of the Tui 
Tonga family (McKern 1929:30). The tanuanga, as one would expect, was
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 153 ===

r
1361
the simplest, only a small mound of earth or sand. The faitoka was a 
comparatively large mound of earth and sand (averaging 8 feet high and 
75 feet diameter) formed in the shape of a truncated cone, containing 
one or several burial vaults. The chief's distant relatives and his 
retainers were buried around the edges of the mound. The langi were 
rectangular platforms or stepped pyramids with cut stone retaining 
walls. 
One or more vaults (funualoto) were buried in the top of the 
platform. The vaults were similar in both the faitoka and langi and 
usually consisted of six large stone slabs placed to form a rectangular 
box.
from conical to stepped-rectangular in shape; all but the simplest 
of these had cut stone architecture, the culmination of which is seen 
in the impressive langi Paepaeotelea at Mua, Tongatabu (McKern 1929: 
51-52, Plate IV A). The abrupt appearance of refined stone architec­
ture associated with the langi type of burial construction may be a 
result of the introduction of that idea by the arrival of Tuitatui, 
traditionally a great foreign chieftain, who is dated genealogically 
about the eleventh century A.D. (McKern 1929:7^)* There is no evi­
dence for an independent development of the langi type of structure 
in Tonga. McKern discounted the validity of an early but uncertainly 
dated list of supposedly chronologically ordered langi; however, 
these appear as a developmental sequence in regard to their complexity. 
Stone construction reached its maximum development late in the pre­
historic sequence, about the sixteenth century, during the reign of 
Uluakimata I. McKern is probably most correct in his discussion of 
the possible origins of fine stone architecture in Tonga when he says:

and to verify the validity of white surface sand as an indicator of

complex pattern of use through time. Initial occupation was followed 
by the filling of earlier pits and by the raising a small area to a 
level slightly above the ground, into which burial pits were dug.
The third phase of activity consisted of the construction of a small 
mound surrounded by a ditch. The fourth phase included an enlargement 
of the mound and the addition of a new surrounding ditch. This 
enlarged the mound almost to its present size, 20 meters in diameter 
and two meters high. A fifth phase of activity showed only the

Wot all of the Tongan langi were platforms or mounds,
Wilson's splendid engraving shows that the langi next to

stood somewhere in the middle. Other langi described by 
McKern had ornamental slabs rising in the line of curbs.
This rectangular area enclosed by stone slabs and curbs is really the
basic pattern of all Polynesia.
Samoa
Golson's research in 1957 marked the beginning of a number of 
projects which have added to our knowledge of Samoan prehistory. 
Golson's excavations, at the edge of bulldozer cuts into a large earth 
mound near the village of Vailele, Upolu, Western Samoa, provided the 
first pottery from Samoa and very early dates in the first century 
A.D. In this site, a blackish-brown habitation layer was sealed 
beneath a later mound construction, the most recent of which was a 
large house platform. Golson determined that the pottery-bearing 
layer was totally unconnected with the mojind construction which

Tongan sites described above.

sequences. At least three mound rebuildings were observed.

The four mounds discussed here include two mounds constructed 
in a single stage, Va-3 and Ya-k; one with sub-mounds, Va-2; and one 
with multiple periods of build-up, Va-1. A fifth mound, Va-38, also 
shows evidence of multiple build-up periods.
The earth mound is a very widespread archaeological feature in 
Samoa as well as in Tonga. Buist distinguished stone mounds and soil 
mounds in his survey of parts of Savai'i, Western Samoa (Buist 1967)9 
and he also distinguished three types of stone mounds on the basis of 
size and shape. 
"Large" mounds are square or rectangular with a flat 
platform measuring 100 to 200 feet per side. "Small" mounds are those 
with sides of the top mound platform having less than 100 feet.
These are different from house mounds because they are considerably 
higher (Buist 1969:39)- 
"Star" mounds represent a third type of stone 
mound widespread in Samoa and are characterized by five to nine

whole" (1971:73). Recognition that early occupations of Polynesia 
were probably Lapita-derived does not presently increase our knowledge 
of marae complexes, but it indicates that the early settlements were 
not characterized by complex stone architecture.
The basic cultural differences noted by Burrows (1938, 19^0) 
between West and East Polynesia (or Central-Marginal) are reflected in 
the differences between the malae of West Polynesia and the marae/ahu 
complex of East Polynesia. Malae were cleared ground areas without 
stone construction, primarily for the purpose of councils and social 
gatherings as opposed to the stone faced platforms (ahu) and stone 
uprights of large courts in East Polynesia.
In the 19^0's the distinction between West and East Polynesia 
was supported by conclusions drawn from physical anthropological data 
on the Polynesians. 
Shapiro's research on twenty-six Polynesian popu­
lations indicated (19^3 :7-8):
The Polynesian population possesses a fundamental unity in 
physical type which necessarily implies that the successive 
immigrants were derived from a common people. . . . But I 
believe that the essential composition of these diverse ele­
ments [ stemming from the mixed origin ] occurred before the 
invasion of Polynesia began.
Recent osteological studies continue to support a distinction 
between West and East Polynesia. Distance statistics for both metrical 
and non-metrical osteological characteristics presented by 
Pietrusewsky indicate a dichotomy between Western and Eastern 
Polynesia (1970:10). Despite noting that Samoa is poorly represented 
because of inadequate samples, Pietrusewsky also recognizes a Tongan- 
Samoan-Fijian alliance (1971:11)•

Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 162 ===

r
Available archaeological information on Polynesian settlement 
indicates that Shapiro's "essential composition" at least in part 
occurred in Western Polynesia, but because now greater time depth is 
recognized for settlement there and in other Polynesian islands more 
complex composition now appears than was recognized in the 19^0 ’s. 
Many of the distinct elements described by Burrows as characteristic 
of East Polynesia, including the ahu/marae complex, developed inde­
pendently from West Polynesia.
Summary
This very brief look at the prehistoric record of West 
Polynesia indicates the late appearance of stone architecture there. 
Stone faced house platforms filled with dirt or rubble, paepae, were 
probably a precursory idea to the elaborate religious shrines that 
developed further east in Polynesia. The lack of stone construction 
in religious centers or in other units in early Samoa or Tonga impor­
tantly suggests that the later significance of this material as a 
building medium in Central and East Polynesia is to be viewed as an 
independently developed architectural style or one whose inception 
lies outside of Polynesia.
recognizable classes of ceremonial structures in the Marquesas, tohua 
and me’ae, exhibit tremendous stylistic variety. Linton recognized

r
this in his archaeological survey (1925:24):
The tohua most nearly approach uniformity, as most of the 
structures found on a given island resemble each other suf­
ficiently to permit the establishment of local type [ sic] .
The simpler of the Nukuhivan me'ae also have many elements 
in common and may be said to constitute a type, but the more 
elaborate of these and the me'ae found on the other islands 
are so variable as not to permit of classification.
The only basic constant feature of the tohua, or taha ko'ina, is the 
dance floor, normally a rectangular area of level ground. In m’ost 
cases, large platforms had to be built up on the slopes of hillsides 
to acquire a large enough area. Linton's survey of tohua in 1920-21 
led him to conclude that the size of Marquesan tohua, and the complex­
ity of the structures associated with the dance floor, increase 
fairly uniformly from south to north in the island chain. Those of 
Fatuhiva are the smallest and simplest and those of Nukuhiva the 
largest and most complex (1925:30). Linton (1925:41-42) concluded 
that: 
"The Marquesan tohua find no close Pacific parallel and must
be considered either a local development or a survival of a structural 
type which had been abandoned or modified in other parts of Polynesia 
before the beginning of the historic period."
Tribal sacred places at which regular religious rites were 
performed were referred to as me'ae, except in Nukuhiva and Uapou 
(the Northern Marquesas) where they had the name ahu. These ahu are 
the equivalent of the me'ae to the south (Linton 1925:31). Two major 
types of me’ae or ahu were "public" and "mortuary" (Linton 1925:3l)• 
These terms distinguished between those used for tribal ceremonies 
and those used primarily for the disposal of the dead.
Me'ae in the southern Marquesas regularly consisted of only a 
series of terraces and platforms, as opposed to the Nukuhiva god house
L

type (Bellwood ms:8). In Fatuhiva, Tahuata, and in all but one valley 
in Hivaoa, a public me'ae is connected with the tohua. In Uapou and 
Uahuka such association appears to have been regular but not constant 
(Linton 1925:31)* This association is lacking in Nukuhiva. Linton 
maintained that certain portions of the Nukuhivan tohua had structures 
set aside for priests; these he equated with the me'ae of the southern 
islands (Linton 1925:31). He did not explain the relationship between 
these structures and the me'ae-equivalent "ahu" referred to above.
The public me'ae connected to tohua were usually quite small and were 
located at one end of that structure. Normally the me'ae were small 
platforms about 3 to b feet high and lacked the pits for depositing 
skulls, debris, etc., found in some other ceremonial structures.
Banyan trees were often found growing on the me'ae. Occasionally, 
images were placed on public me'ae, but Linton did not find this to 
be a universal practice (1925:32). On Uapou a house was built for 
the inspirational priest on top of the me'ae; these houses were of the 
same form as ordinary dwellings but were considerably taller.
types Marquesans referred to as me'ae:
In Nukuhiva the public me'ae became fused with the tohua 
and the resulting structure is in a general way comparable to 
the enclosed sacred places of Hawaii and Tahiti. There were 
also purely secular tohua which were not used for religious 
rites but it is doubtful whether there were public me'ae which 
were not connected with tohua.
In Hivaoa, Tahuata, and Fatuhiva the public me'ae became 
structurally a part of the Tohua but retained its distinctive 
name and functions.
In Uapou and Uahuka the public me'ae was connected with the 
tohua. But there were also secular tohua which had no me'ae. 
Nearly all public me'ae were small platforms without a sacri­
fice pit or pit for skulls.
Most Nukuhivan burial me'ae were simple structures modeled 
on the lines of ordinary dwellings. Sacrifice pits were a

In Uapou and Uahuka the mortuary me'ae consisted of a

In the northeast part of Hivaoa many of the mortuary me'ae 
were of the form common on Nukuhiva, but elsewhere they were 
either series of platforms, like those of Uapou and Uahuka, 
or series of superimposed terraces. In them skull pits were 
rather common, but sacrifice pits were rare.
The mortuary me'ae of Tahuata and Fatuhiva resemble, in 
general, those of southern Hivaoa.
Another type of sacred place or structure in the northern 
Marquesas, tokai, and southern Marquesas, fanaua, is of interest here 
because one or more images were erected on all tokai and some early 
writers claimed that stone caps were placed on the heads of such 
images (Linton 1925:*H). Unfortunately, there is little verification 
for the latter statement.
important part of Marquesan me'ae and tokai; they were carved either 
of wood or stone, and many achieved great size. Practically all 
Marquesan images'were carved in a conventionalized human form. Wooden 
and stone figures were used in the same way, often found on the same 
site, and differences between the two seem to be largely a result of 
limitations of the art medium. Stone images were most common in the 
southern Marquesas, particularly Hivaoa. These stone images do show 
a greater degree of variation than the wooden ones. Two types of 
stone images are distinguished by Linton, true images and architectural 
figures (1925:7*0- The true images were placed on platforms of sacred 
places, the architectural figures were incorporated into terrace or 
platform walls of secular and ceremonial structures. Linton felt 
that the stone images were derived from wooden prototypes (1925:7*0 -

Excavations in the 1950's
Robert Suggs' contribution to our limited knowledge of pre­
historic cultural developments in the Marquesas resulted from his 
sub-surface work done in 1956 and 1957. Suggs' conclusions, based 
upon survey and excavation, are presented in the form of a develop­
mental sequence of Marquesan culture which extends from 150 B.C. to 
historic times. 
This sequence consists of a Settlement Period 
(150 B.C. - A.D. 100), a Developmental Period (A.D. 100-1100), an 
Expansion Period (A.D. 1100-1400), a Classic Period (A.D. 1^(00-1790), 
and an Historic Period beginning in 1790 A.D. (Suggs 196l:l8o). A 
major feature of each of these periods is the appearance of a dis­
tinct masonry or an architectural form which is of significance in 
our examination of religious architecture of the marae type. The 
characteristics and the validity of Suggs' sequence are considered 
below.
The date of settlement for the Marquesas, the beginning of 
Suggs' Settlement Period (150 B.C. - A.D. 100), was fixed from the 
radiocarbon dates obtained at site EHaa 1 (2080 - 150 and 1910 -  
180 years before 1956) (Shapiro and Suggs 1959:13). Cultural features 
associated with this period are as follows:
Settlements consisting of small house clusters were made 
on the beaches near the stream mouths, where both drinking 
water and the sea were within easy reach. Houses were of the 
Ovoid House type, possibly with an outdoor pavement, and were 
frequently rebuilt. Temple structures were separated from the 
dwelling area. They were probably of both the Eastern 
Polynesian ahu and forecourt types, as well as the Western 
Polynesian god-house type. House clusters were probably
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 167 ===

r
inhabited by extended family groups. Primary, flexed and 
extended, and secondary burials were made in dwelling areas 
as well as in the sacred precincts. Exposure of the dead 
and bone repositories can be inferred from the secondary 
burial customs. . . .
Private ceremonialism was well developed in this period.
A skull cult, involving trophy or ancestral skulls, func­
tioned. Ancestor worship may be inferred from the numerous 
incomplete burials. Food offerings were made to the dead.
Few if any, material goods were placed with burials. Some 
cannibalism was in evidence.
Burials of the Settlement Period infrequently suggested 
marked status differences, although status may have been 
indicated by perishable burial accompaniments. Information 
on the physical type of the early Marquesans is not yet 
available . . . (Suggs 196l:l8l).
Suggs recovered little detailed information regarding cultural
change during the 1000 year-long Developmental Period (A.D. 100-1100);
this is unfortunate, because it is most important from the standpoint
of examining Easter Island settlement. The earlier part of the period
is seen to be a continuation of the Settlement Period, but later
changes include:
Simple community ceremonial structures may have appeared at 
this time in association with temples, such as the gravel paved 
area at NHaa 1 which may be an early tohua. 
Ovoid houses gave 
way to houses constructed on Paved Paepae structures.
Small Paved Paepae are found in the ceremonial areas above 
the Early Eastern Polynesian simple temple structure, which indi­
cates that the Western Polynesian god-house temple had gained 
popularity. By the end of the period some structures attained a 
length of 4o feet. . . .
Status differences, in general, were apparently not marked. 
However, the increased sizes of some of the house structures 
later in this period suggest . . .  a gradual increase in pres­
tige or rank . . . .
Private ceremonialism, which included the head cult, 
ancestor cult, and cannibalism, remained unchanged. . . .
Material culture did change. Some of the artifact types 
found in the Settlement and early Developmental periods were 
discarded by the end of the Developmental Period. . . .
[almost the entire Melanesoid complex]
The artifacts of the Settlement and Developmental periods 
present frequent and striking similarities to artifacts 
recovered on Easter Island. . . . The early temple structure 
at WHaa 1 may represent a basic form from which the ahu of 
Easter can have developed (I96l:l8l-l82).

Because these two periods are crucial for understanding the relation­
ship between the Marquesas and Easter Island, we should look at the 
evidence Suggs uses in constructing his sequence. Only one site was 
excavated by Suggs which he suspected of having a Settlement Period 
occupation, the critical site of NHaa 1, at Ha'atuatua Bay on the 
east coast of Nukuhiva. This site consisted of a large open village 
on the sand hill just behind the beach. Tidal waves and high seas had 
disturbed most of the site, but Suggs found two locations, A and B, 
where important cultural remains were uncovered. Location A was pri­
marily a burial area at the south end of the site which extended along 
the beach for about 500 yards (Suggs referred to the entire deposit as 
one site). The midden deposit was only 20 to 25 inches thick, with a 
major break at about 10 inches below the top of the cultural layer.
The overburden was mainly wind-blown sand. Two architectural features 
as well as at least 50 individuals buried contemporaneously with the 
structures, according to Suggs, were uncovered in the stratigraph- 
ically complex locality.

stratum which Suggs associated with the late Developmental Period 
contained at least two paved paepae. 
One of these, uncovered in 
Suggs' Cut 1, was roughly rectangular, 30 feet long and over 10 feet 
wide, and had three post holes in the floor (Suggs 1961:55)• No 
further information is provided. A second Paved Paepae of the late 
Developmental Period was uncovered in Cut 2 beneath a ruined 
Megalithic Paepae. Suggs recovered one sherd of poorly fired pottery 
in association with the Paved Paepae (1961:56). The two deposits 
represent two distinct occupations separated hy a long time period, 
with the later (Stratum II) dating to the Classic Period. The strati­
fication of Megalithic Paepae over the simpler Paved Paepae as well 
as the pottery are important. These occupations were dated by arti­
fact seriation only.
Suggs’ analysis of pottery fragments found in Settlement and 
Developmental Period layers at NHaa 1 and NHo 3 indicated to him simi­
larities in rim style with Lapita pottery from New Caledonia and even 
closer similarities with Fijian Lapita pottery. Suggs maintained that 
at least two of his sherds were not manufactured locally, and he sug­
gested Tonga as their source (1961:96). 
Sinoto (1966, 1970:112) 
determined through petrographic analysis that all the pottery is of 
local manufacture, but he has not speculated on the possible sources 
of the pottery tradition.
All of the other sites examined by Suggs appear to be more 
recent and date only from the Expansion Period on. In the Expansion 
Period (llOO-lUOO A.D.) tohua structures as described by Linton were 
used. The rectangular plazas were enlarged, some reached a length of 
500 feet. Low stepped terraces were built along one or both sides of
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 173 ===

r
156]
the tohua plaza, and houses were constructed along their edges. In 
the late Expansion Period the plazas were elevated on large artificial 
terraces, rather than on the roughly leveled ground (Suggs 196l:12l). 
These tohua may have been public and secular rather than religious.
In Suggs' Classic Period (llfOO-1790 A.D.) the paepae (raised, 
terraced platforms) were further elaborated and enlarged. The use of 
huge stone facing walls became common. The tohua retained their huge 
sizes; the largest known is 600 by 80 feet. An excellent example of 
their complexity is seen in Suggs' sites NT2 and NT3 (Fig. ^3).
Facing walls were often built of three ton boulders with dirt fill 
forming the platform top. Low stadium-like steps held spectators.
At this time large ceremonial buildings appeared away from the tohua. 
These were called me'ae and were built in the form of the ordinary 
dwelling house, but were intended for habitation by the deity to whom 
they were dedicated (according to ethnographic analogy). The servants 
of the deity resided there. This temple form occurred in smaller 
proper i/ions in the earlier periods (Developmental and Expansion).
Large statues occur in me'ae in the southern islands where stone carv­
ing of monumental proportion had been more highly developed.

NHaa 1 site on Nukuhiva as a single unit, Sinoto observed at least
