Sinoto's reinterpretation of Suggs' early materials and his 
excavations in new sites on Uahuka and Hiva Oa led to some changes in 
both the chronology and content of Suggs' periods. 
Sinoto outlines 
the following cultural sequences or phases for the Marquesas (1970:
113):
Phase IV 
A.D. 1600-1800
Phase III 
A.D. 1300-1600
Phase II 
A.D. 600-1300
Phase I 
A.D. 300- 600
Phase I, Initial Settlement (A.D. 300 to 600), is characterized 
by rectangular house foundations with post holes (some stone pavements 
appear late) and a maritime economy reflected by a substantial amount 
of line fishing equipment. This equipment includes a predominance of 
one-piece hooks accompanied by trolling hooks, porites coral and sea 
urchin files, predominantly type 2k and Suggs' Hatiheu type adzes, 
and pecked tang adzes. Pottery was used throughout this phase, but 
was never made in great quantity. There is no archaeological evidence 
regarding religious structures during,this phase.
Phase II or Developmental Stage (A.D. 600 to 1300) continues 
to a great extent the same tool making traditions of the first phase. 
Fishhooks increase in size; the same adze types continue, with tang­
ing becoming a more common feature. Flat, single course house pave­
ments for rectangular floor-plan dwellings continue to be used. Pot­
tery continues into this phase, at least in the northern group of
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 178 ===

r
161]
islands. There is still very little evidence for the characteristics 
of religious architecture. Suggs' Simple Temple structure appears to 
he a limited expression of religious architecture for Sinoto's late 
Phase II. As mentioned above, Suggs' second "early" religious struc­
ture at NHaa 1, the Paved Paepae and "tohua" court, are placed by 
Sinoto to much later historic times. The lack of differentiation of 
material culture seen through the 1000 year span of Suggs' long 
Developmental Period (A.D. 100-1100) is still unfortunately charac­
teristic of Sinoto's Phases I and II, a span of 1000 years. This 
lack of detailed knowledge about cultural changes during this crucial 
time period makes it difficult to speculate about the time of possible 
dispersal to other islands such as Easter Island.
Sinoto's Phase III, the Expansion (A.D. 1300-1600), is asso­
ciated with population movements into all parts of the islands with 
complex architectural features such as low-platform house foundations 
with divided sleeping quarters and a front terrace appearing for the 
first time (Sinoto 1970:110). These are the house foundations Suggs 
referred to as Transitional and Terraced Paepae (l96l:159-l6o). 
Fishhooks became smaller. Adzes developed tangs and trapezoidal cross 
sections. Pottery passed out of use and dogs appear to have been 
decreasing, but pigs had probably increased in numbers.
His final phase, IV, the Classic (A.D. 1600-1800), shows lit­
tle change in portable artifact assemblages but within this phase 
major increases are seen in the complexity of ceremonial and reli­
gious architecture.

In Hivaoa or the southern Marquesas there are no other statues
comparable in size to those of Oipona me'ae. A limited number of 
smaller stone figures, very rarely reaching 1 meter in height, occur 
in very few cult sites. In most cases they are built into masonry 
walls as an architectural feature, and in some very rare cases there 
may be as many as two or three in a row. The bulk of these sites, 
like Oipona, are in Puamau Valley (Heyerdahl 1965:137). Large size 
stone statues in the southern Marquesas are limited to the Oipona 
site; only two isolated specimens are known in the northern Marquesas 
outside of the unique assemblage at Paeke. One of these is on 
Nukuhiva (Porter 1822, 2:110) and the other is on Uahuka (Heyerdahl
Hane excavations; however, these are considerably smaller than the 
Paeke statue dated by Ferdon, or those from Oipona. Twenty-two
1965:138).

Society Islands
Windward (Tahiti, Moorea, and Meetia) and Leeward (Raiatea, Tahaa, 
Borabora, and Huahine). The marae architecture of the windward group 
is characterized by worked and fitted small limestone and tuff blocks 
and by small vesicular basalt stones in the later stages of construc­
tion. There is shaping as well as fitting of stone in marae construc­
tion. Leeward marae were built with great limestone slabs, trimmed 
square and set on end side to side. Wo tuff or basalt was used. The 
differences, then, exist mainly in the type of stone facing and 
enclosure of the court.
l) The rectangular court or large open space, often paved, and always 
leveled off, and enclosed generally by a stone or, rarely, by an 
earthen wall. 2) A low ahu mound at one end, but in the later forms 
it was a raised platform, sometimes with other smaller platforms on 
top which formed a terraced, truncated pyramid. 
3) The stone uprights 
positioned at several points in the marae court and on or near the 
ahu. These uprights near or on the ahu of the marae were either back­
rests for the gods or were material objects into which the god 
entered. The former significance of marae uprights is indicated by 
the statement of the Spaniard Varela in 1772:
They [ do ] duty as leaning stocks when those who meet 
together for their religious ceremonies sit down at these 
spots; but only the Epure priests and their arii and his
Society Islands marae are best discussed in two groups:
The Society Islands marae consisted of three main parts:
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 185 ===

"brothers do sit down there, and although some of these stones 
remain vacant they pertain to the deceased fathers and ances­
tors and personages and nobody may seat himself against them 
(Corney 1915, 2:260).
Emory interprets this statement to mean that the function of 
the uprights on or immediately in front of the ahu was as back-rest 
seats reserved for ancestral spirits (Emory 1970:76). Other uprights 
on the court would be for priests and chiefs. There were often 
smaller platforms adjoining or close by the larger ahu.
The most common distribution of stone uprights is three in 
front of the ahu, one centrally located with the other two flanking it 
at or near the ends of the ahu. Many ahu had three similar uprights 
along the back side of the ahu. 
Similarities will be seen between 
these Society Island marae and those of the Tuamotus and those of 
Necker Island in Hawaii, discussed below. One upright was usually 
placed in the center of the court, in the midline of the ahu. Other 
uprights were located in various positions on the marae court (see 
Fig. 1*5).
Smaller, usually enclosed, rectangular courts with three 
uprights along one end were often, but not always, attached to marae 
in the Windward Society Islands. Emory has called these "shrines" 
(1933:66). He recently referred to these shrines as "miniature marae 
lacking ahu" (1970:7*+) • In these shrines another upright or back­
rest may stand opposite the central upright of the shrine. Green 
found in his survey of marae structures in the Opunoho Valley of 
Moorea (Green, et al. 1967:11+1) that a small stone image often occu­
pies this position on the shrine.

Emory distinguished eight types of facing used on stone con­
structed marae in the Society Islands, some of these appear to have 
historical significance (Emory 1933:5-10). In the Windward examples 
marae comers were made with great care, often with cut stone, to sup­
port the walls above. The fill of the marae ahu was generally coral 
in the Society Islands. Marae courts were often paved with flagstones 
and occasionally, they had slab-linsd vaults in them or in the ahu.
The developmental, sequence of marae in the Society Islands 
postualted by Emory (1933:2^-379 50-55) illustrates a suspected gen­
eral trend in the development of all marae construction in East and 
Central Polynesia, so Emory's sequence will be elaborated on here and 
referred to again. Tahitian inland, intermediate, and coastal marae 
represent the three types in the sequence.
The inland marae had rectangular courts, which were either 
platforms, terraces, enclosures or a combination of these (see 
Fig. ^5a, b, c). A low ahu was usually constructed at one end.
Three uprights were placed on the rear wall of the ahu, two on each 
end and one, the tallest, in the middle. Three uprights were placed 
also against the front face of the ahu, and one or a, pair stood in 
the middle of the court opposite the ahu. These marae were often 
attached to a larger court which Emory equated in some respects to 
the Marquesan "tohua" (Emory 1933:25). One type of very small inland 
marae had no ahu. Ahu of larger inland marae averaged 1.5 feet high 
by 3 to h feet wide by ll+ feet long. The ahu was faced with a single 
course of unworked stone and had a fill of soil and small stones.
The ahu was a few feet from the end of the court, and did not reach
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 188 ===

I~ 
in\
the sides. The court itself averaged 25 feet by 50 or 75 feet, was 
not oriented directionally, and was often paved. Cut stone was not 
used except in a few cornerstones of the enclosing walls.
The intermediate marae (see Fig. ^5f) is represented by fewer 
examples (Emory 1933:31). Suggs thought these to be representative of 
the period about A.D. 1000-1100 when the inland type changed into this 
intermediate style (l960a:li(-2). The ahu were higher than the inland 
type, and while faced with dressed stone blocks, they were not stepped. 
The ahu was placed directly against the rear of the court but did not
extend all the way across it. These intermediate marae were found
>
both inland and on the coast in contrast to the inland variety.
Three uprights were positioned in front of the ahu, with the central 
one being the highest. Round-faced stones were not used in the inter­
mediate marae; the facing was flat like in the inland type.
The elaborate coast marae (see Fig. h5d, e) were traditionally 
associated with the ari'i and ra1atira classes. A stepped ahu and 
extensive use of specially selected, or worked, stone in facing ahu 
and court walls were characteristic. The ahus were built against the 
end wall and extended along the full width of the court. No uprights 
were used on the stepped platform. Three uprights were placed in 
front of the ahu, with the central one being generally tallest. The 
average ahu size was 60 feet long, 15 feet wide, and 10 to 12 feet 
high. Most of the coastal ahu were three-stepped on the interior of 
the court and four-stepped on the exterior. Single, large squared 
stones formed the corners. The marae court, averaging 120 by 60 feet, 
was paved and was enclosed with stone walls. Enclosures of several 
marae were common. The marae did not seem to be oriented in any
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 189 ===

r
1721
special direction, although when on the shore, most of them had the 
ahu end towards the sea and the rest of the complex was perpendicular 
to it. These marae were often located on flat sandy points along hay 
shores or on level valley bottoms. The culmination of these tenden­
cies is seen in the marae of Mahaiatea at Papara, Tahiti, built 
between 1766 and 1768 (Emory 1970:73), which had an ahu of 10 steps 
about 50 feet high; its court was 267 feet by 377 feet.
The Leeward Islands marae development was somewhat different 
from that seen in Tahiti and in the rest of the Windward group. The 
main difference was in the absence of stepped ahu (except in two
instances), and in the use of huge slabs exclusively for ahu
(Fig. J46) . The latter difference, however, is the result of avail­
ability of local stone material. Emory says that it is impossible to
differentiate Leeward marae from Windward marae functionally and that
If uprights on the ahu were an early feature of Society Island 
marae, by the time the stepped ahu was used on the Windward 
Island marae and the megalithic slab-faced ahu appeared on the 
Leeward Island marae this feature had been dropped on such 
structures. Uprights marking positions on the ahu, seem there­
fore to have become a feature not necessary to these ahu, 
which would still serve to elevate the position of the gods 
worshipped at the marae.

Society Island marae. Green's survey of 'Opunoho Valley in Moorea led 
to a more detailed classification system for Society Islands religious 
structures. Eighty-nine marae and 108 shrines, about half of which 
were attached to marae, were located (Green 196la:170-17l). Green 
treated marae structures as non-portable artifacts susceptible to the

same kind of typological analysis characteristic of archaeological 
description and analysis of portable artifacts. He found that Emory's 
classification of "inland, intermediate, and coastal" could be applied 
to the 'Opunoho marae but that refinements in description were possible 
(Green 196la:17l). Green's classification of five major kinds of 
ruins included religious structures as a Group III. A basic distinc­
tion within this group is one between marae and "shrine."
"a rectangular and unenclosed stone pavement, its longest dimension
in general less than 20 ft, and bearing at least three uprights at
one end and a backrest in the midline at the opposite end" (1961a:
171). The shrine in 'Opunoho was observed to be both attached to and
separate from a marae and characteristically had a ti'i, a human
image, crudely carved in stone and situated in the midline between the
central upright and the back-rest. Green (1967:1^-1) noted: 
"This
demonstrable association of the stone ti'i with the shrine is also
supported by the fact that, of the 20 figures that we found, none was
in a context from which it was possible to infer an association with
the inland marae except where a ti'i was found on a shrine attached
to the marae." The shrine is Green's Type III A.
Marae were classed according to structural complexity as
follows (1961a:172):
III B: a rectangular raised pavement with wall facing of
dressed curbs and round-ended stones. At one end of the 
pavement an ahu with a similar facing rises in two steps.
Ill C: other marae in which dressed stones and curbs appear
as facing in the ahu or enclosing wall, but the ahu lacks 
steps.
An altered definition of Emory's "shrine" was used by Green:

r
175I
III D: a large rectangular enclosing wall usually with
'flat facing' and capped by selected stone slabs; a stepped 
ahu with coral blocks as facing is attached to the enclosing 
wall and extends completely across one of the shorter sides.
Ill E: a large rectangular enclosing wall usually with 'flat
facing' and capped by selected slabs; the ahu with a facing 
of coral blocks stands as high as or higher than the enclosing 
wall to which it is attached.
Ill F: a large rectangular enclosing wall usually with 'flat
facing' and capped by selected slabs; the ahu which is sepa­
rated from and slightly lower than the enclosing wall is faced 
with bands of dyke stone laid sideways alternating with coral 
blocks; a cist or vault lies within the long wall at the 
opposite end from the ahu.
Ill G: a carefully laid rectangular enclosing wall to which
a stone ahu lower than the wall is attached, usually in one 
corner, the front wall of the ahu is commonly decorated with 
a thin low coral veneer.
Ill H: a carefully laid rectangular enclosing wall with a low
and detached stone ahu, generally with a thin low coral veneer 
across the front of the ahu; in a few cases a cist exists in 
one of the longer walls.
Ill I: an enclosing wall around a pavement within ahu; only
uprights and backrests lie within the court.
Ill J : a rectangular paved platform or terrace platform on
one end of which is a low ahu; in front on the pavement are 
uprights and backrests.
Ill K: a rectangular pavement or terrace platform without
ahu; only major and subsidiary sets of uprights and backrests 
occupy the pavement.
Ill L: marae which for a variety of reasons are unclassified.

chronological detail on this type of marae structure (see Fig. 1+7).

The maximum length of the marae is 36 meters -with a maximum width of 
15 meters (Emory, 122 by 52 feet). Emory (1933:106) described the site 
(number 99):
Site 99* Large marae at Titiroa, interior of Opunoho Valley, 
in a dense grove of mape trees and on a flat-topped ridge 
(fig. 70). The corners of the enclosure are constructed of 
natural blocks of dike stone carefully selected and fitted to 
make an even and perpendicular wall. Slabs are laid along 
the top of the wall.
The ahu or small platform at one end of the walled enclosure was about 
20 inches high and was faced with natural stones. The enclosing wall 
surrounding the partly paved court stood 2 to 3.5 feet tall and was 3 
to 3-5 feet wide. This wall was double faced with rubble fill. Num­
bers 1-21 on the plan view (Fig. i+7) are basalt uprights at various 
locations on the court. Upright number 1 was the tallest at 3 feet, 
but now lies prone on the ahu surface. Number 3, in the front center 
of the ahu, is characteristically the tallest (28 inches) of the 
remaining uprights which range down to 8 inches above the court sur­
face. Features a, b, and c are small coral slab framed boxes or 
cists. Green’s (1967:153) more detailed investigation of this marae

stone forming the outside southwest corner and some of the stones in 
the northwest corner had dressed outer surfaces, a feature common to 
this type marae (ill F). Excavation of the three coral slab framed 
boxes provided very little information regarding their use because 
they contained only soil and accumulated debris. The slab projections 
above the court pavement indicate that the cists probably had covers 
but these were not present. At least on Moorea where the cist has 
been present, it has been associated with only two late marae types

(Green 1967:153)• Excavations conducted around eight of the uprights

Emory concludes in his report of 1933 that the Society 
Islands inland marae are 
closely related to those of Necker and Nehoa
ir. Hawaii and to those of the Tuamotus. He further maintains in his
1970 article that the marae of the Malden, Austral, Cook, Tuamotu, 
Hawaiian, and Society Islands are all closely related and that they 
may have all diffused from Tahiti (Emory 1970). Bellwood (1970:101) 
concurs in the view that the maraes of the Cook Islands, although 
showing a great deal of internal variation, are to be included in 
this grouping. 
Recognizing the structural similarities within these 
groups, we can begin by looking at the marae of the Tuamotus, which 
are the most closely related to those of the Society Islands.

Tuamotus
Emory, in looking for simple structures related to those of 
Tahiti which he believed to be early and in posing questions about 
their origin, found very close similarities in the Tuamotuan marae 
which he described in detail in 1934, 1939, and 1947. He was able 
to obtain important information about marae ceremonies in the 
Tuamotus as shown in Chapter III.
The central and western Tuamotus were characterized by a 
remarkable uniformity in marae constructions (Emory 1970:77)- Except 
in the extreme eastern part of the Tuamotus, at Reao, Pukarua, and 
Tatakoto, the marae on one island differed little from the marae of 
other islands. This mapae had an ahu faced with slabs of limestone 
set on edge and three uprights along the back of the ahu which faced 
on an open, unpaved rectangular court (Fig. 49). Three uprights were 
placed also in front of the ahu; usually each of these uprights had 
its own small platform. A small platform generally was placed on the 
court opposite the center of the ahu. An upright at the back of this 
small platform served as a seat and back-rest for the chief person at 
the marae.
Emory compared the Tuamotuan marae and their significance
with the marae of Tahiti (1970:77):
These Tuamotuan marae are identical with marae we have seen in 
the Society Islands. We can assume therefore that this type 
diffused from the Society Islands at an early time, became 
stabilized in the Tuamotus, and persisted in both areas into 
historic times.
The eastern marae have higher ahu and a greater number of uprights 
along the back of the ahu than marae farther west. These local

Mangareva
missionary times, Emory sees close similarities between those remain­
ing there and the well-preserved marae structures on Temoe, an atoll 
east of Mangareva: 
"Fortunately, the stone structures left by the
ancient Mangarevan inhabitants of Temoe stand almost intact." Emory 
compared the Ruanuku ruins on Mangareva with the marae Toa-maora on 
Temoe. This latter structure seems to be generally characteristic of 
the larger Mangareva marae. Toa-maora, the largest recorded marae, 
had a platform 21 feet wide and 58 feet long with an unenclosed court 
on the inland side. The platform was constructed of an initial 
course of limestone slabs set on edge with upper courses of horizontal 
slabs. The court face of the platform had two high steps, and Emory 
maintained this feature was also characteristic of Mangarevan marae 
(1939:9). A centrally located vault was used for burial and vaults 
built into each end of the platform at ground level were most likely 
used for storing sacred objects used in marae rituals. Most of the 
small stepped platforms on Temoe may be marae while the unstepped 
ones were probably just tombs. There is no evidence that Mangarevan 
marae had slab back-rests or pillars. Emory (1939:49) also states:
The Mangarevan marae, judging from the Temoe remains, was 
a rectangular platform placed across one end of an unenclosed 
court, with the court face of the platform stepped with one 
or two steps.

discussion of the similarities between Mangarevan marae and Easter 
Island ahu illustrates their close relationship.
Austral Islands (Ra'ivavae, Tubuai, Rurutu)
Recently Emory has published some descriptions of Ra'ivavae 
marae which were recorded by J. F. G. Stokes in 1922 and by J. Frank 
Stimson in the 1930's (Emory 1970:80-83). Ra'ivavae marae were rec­
tangular courts entirely surrounded by upright slabs of basalt or 
limestone. At least one of these marae described by Stokes had stone 
statues. Most of the courts of the marae were paved; none had ahu 
platforms (Emory 1970:8o). The uprights along one side or one end of 
the marae court were the tallest, and the central one was always the 
tallest of these. Burial places and stone statues were sometimes 
found in association with marae on Ra'ivavae.
Skjolsvold (1965) excavated the ceremonial center of Te Rae 
Rae in 1956, as part of the research conducted by the Norwegian 
Expedition to the Pacific. Te Rae Rae had tombs and statues adja­
cent to the marae enclosure which was about 1 8.5  meters long and 6 
to 6.5 meters wide. Stone tiki were usually placed near tombs at 
marae (Skjblsvold 1965:106). The number and size of statues used 
at marae varied greatly, with the two largest known reaching 2.72 
meters and 2.17 meters in height. Burial chambers were placed always 
outside the enclosure and usually at a short distance from it, as at 
Te Rae Rae. 
SkjBlsvold also referred to another type of marae, a 
circular or elongated pile of stones without walls or fences (1965: 
106). 
Skjolsvold recognized strong similarities between the marae of
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 203 ===

r
Tubuai and Ra’ivavae and emphasized the difference between them and 
the marae of the Society Islands (1965:106).
According to Emory, Ra'ivavae marae are clearly related to
other Polynesian structures (1970:82-83):
We could conclude, therefore, that the Raivavae marae, like 
the Tongarevan marae, evolved from the Tahitian-Tuamotuan 
marae, but there is another possibility. A council platform 
at Maeva, Huahine, had upright stone back rests standing along 
its sides, and several other similar structures evidently 
existed in the Leeward Society Islands (Emory, 1933:37-38, 
fig. 10b). It is possible that these could have evolved into 
the Raivavae type of marae, or the Raivavae marae could have 
resulted from a fusion of the Tahitian marae with the coun­
cil platform. In any case, its kinship with the Society 
Island marae is unmistakable, and of the two forms of struc­
tures, the Raivavae marae is more likely to have evolved 
from the Tahitian marae with the loss of the ahu and the 
emphasis on the stone uprights decorating the marae - than 
from the council platform.
The marae of Tubuai were simple rectangular courts without an 
ahu and enclosed on three sides by large upright slabs (Aitken 1930: 
118-129). They are basically simple versions of the Ra'ivavae marae.
Emory reports, quoting a manuscript by Seabrook, that Rurutu 
marae were practically identical with Tahitian-Tuamotuan marae, 
having three uprights standing on a low platform which faced a court 
with an arrangement of uprights (1970:84). There is a considerable 
amount of variation, although some marae lack ahu and consist pri­
marily of rows of uprights.
Cook Islands (Tongareva, Manihiki-Rakahanga, Rarotonga, Aitutaki)
Buck recorded plan views and details for seventeen of the 24 
marae known from the atoll of Tongareva in the northern Cook Islands 
(1932a:148-176). Ahu were found on four of the quadrangular marae 
courts. Spaced upright slabs stood at the rear of the ahu and
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 204 ===

r
187I
continued around the entire perimeter of the marae court. Low curb 
stones connected the uprights around the court except on the ahu end. 
Some of the upright slabs had been shaped, resulting in flanges or 
notches in the top of the stone. Buck also noted that in some cases a 
sacred house was located on the marae court (l932a:158, 172). Emory 
has stressed the similarities of the Tongarevan marae with those of 
the Tuamotus and Society Islands (Emory 1970:80), specifically, the 
raised platform at one end of the quadrangular court, and the flanged 
and notched ornamentation of uprights. Buck reported that marae struc­
tures on neighboring Manihiki and Rakahanga had all been destroyed 
(1932b:208).
Peter Bellwood's recent research in the southern Cook Islands 
aids our discussion of marae and their variations in Polynesia because 
prior to his research little was known of the marae of these islands 
(1969a, 1969b, 1970). His fieldwork in Rarotonga and Aitutaki gives 
a basic outline of what these marae were like. He identified five 
types of marae on Rarotonga and five types on Aitutaki (1969a:522, 
525-526). All of the described Rarotongan marae are considered to be 
late (Bellwood 1969a:528). Rarotongan marae were more like those of 
the Society Islands in that they had raised stone platforms with up­
rights and an open rectangular court which also had some uprights. 
Aitutaki marae appear in some instances to be like miniature Ra'ivavae 
structures with their fences of upright stone slabs (Emory 1970:8U). 
Some Aitutaki marae also had patterned layouts of large uprights at­
tached to the regular marae enclosure; the largest of these was 
Paengariki which consisted of more than 60 large uprights arranged in 
four major rows extending over an area Uo meters wide and 80 meters long
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 205 ===

r
1881
(Bellwood 1969a:526). Emory noted some similarities of these rows of
uprights in marae with those in the Society Islands:
The great field of uprights in parallel rows at marae Paengariki 
is an expansion of the above arrangements and reveals a great 
emphasis on court uprights seemingly marking the position of 
attendants, or serving as memorials of those who did attend.
This tendency has been expressed in a number of Society Island 
marae, notably at one on Meetia, which had over 10 court 
uprights arranged more or less in rows (Emory 1933:27, fig. 5? 
and 117, fig. 79)•
Bellwood put less emphasis on similarities in marae forms; he 
stressed the differences between marae in Rarotonga and Aitutaki, 
despite the similarities in other types of material culture, and 
between marae in these islands and the rest of East Polynesia (1969a: 
528). Recently he has emphasized the diversity within each of the 
two islands in arguing against the importance of secondary dispersals 
within East Polynesia (Bellwood 1970:10^-).
Hawaii
Bihoa and Becker, volcanic islands in the northern Hawaiian 
chain, are sparsely vegetated, less than a mile in length, and rise 
steeply from the sea. The unique stone images and the marae uprights 
of Becker were known as early as 189^. Kenneth Emory, through research 
conducted as part of the Tanager Expedition to these islands in 1923 
and 192^, has provided a detailed description of the religious struc­
tures found there (Emory 1928). Because of the lack of historic 
activity on the islands, the preservation of the remains was unusually 
good.

Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.

Marae
Emory's study of 33 Keeker island marae led him to the fol­
lowing composite description (1928:60-69, Fig. 25). The marae ruins 
belong to one fixed type, only five out of the 33 vary significantly. 
The basic features of the marae are two (see Fig. 50); a low, long 
rectangular platform (or ahu) and an open rectangular court of paved 
terrace. Keeker marae had a row of upright slabs along the rear wall 
of the rectangular platform. These averaged 2.\ feet in height, l|- 
feet in width and 8 inches in thickness (1928:60). A smaller upright
stood on the front edge of the platform, opposite the central of the
rear uprights. Two small uprights stood side by side against the face 
of the platform, directly in line with the central rear upright and 
the small upright at the front edge of the platform. Another pair of 
uprights, or in some cases, a single upright, stood at the front edge 
of the terrace pavement in line with the three positions just 
described. A number of uprights were placed in the right and left 
corners of the terrace or plaza. These were either parallel or per­
pendicular to the platform (ahu). These marae were built of uncut, 
rough .'■tones. The edges of the platform and terrace were formed by 
rough blocks set on edge. No coral or beach stones 
were used 
in the
construction of the Necker marae (Emory 1928:61).
The five divergent marae recorded by Emory differ from the 
common structural characteristics in that they all lack a platform 
(ahu) and all but one, marae number 1 0 , had three enclosing walls.
Of these five, No. 12 is the most interesting because apparently all

the stone images described helow came from this structure (1928:100-

Statuary
Although the thirteen Necker images were made on the island, 
Emory cannot explain why no images were found at other marae or why 
few image manufacturing tools were discovered. All the statues are 
stylized, are made of vesicular basalt, range in height between 8 
inches and 18 inches and weigh between ^ and 25 pounds each. The major 
manufacturing technique was pecking, but filing and grinding are also 
evident (1928:102). The emphasis on the head is indicated by its 
proportion; more than one fourth of the body. The neck is absent and 
the knob-like, projecting ears are nearly at shoulder level. The 
trunk is slab-like and the arms hang straight down from the shoulder 
and are as long as the trunk. The legs are quite short and heavy and 
a carved groove separates them. Two images have feet.
Emory noted similarities between some of the fifteen Nihoa 
structures to which he attached religious significance and the Necker 
marae (1928:12). One combined structure, particularly, Site 50 on 
Nihoa, is similar to the enclosed, platformless Necker marae (Emory 
1928:106). No statuary was recovered from Nihoa.
Uniformity in the cultural remains of Necker Island convinced 
Emory that the island was occupied during a single, not necessarily 
short, period (1928:116). The marae on Necker appear to have been 
built by people coming from Nihoa; Nihoa, unlike the sparsely popu­
lated Necker, could have supported up to 200 inhabitants. Emory's

Although Emory knew of no Hawaiian term for these religious structures

great similarity to Tahitian marae. Heiau is the most widely used 
term referring to Hawaiian religious centers which were in use at the 
time of European contact.

Heiau
The Hawaiian heiau was first described by Kamakau (1865) who 
distinguished rectangular, square, canoe shaped, and irregularly oval 
ones. Two basic reasons why heiau show great variability in shape 
and architecture are that they were often rebuilt, and that special 
priests, kahuna kuhikuhi puuone, spent considerable time studying heiau 
plans to design new ones that would be more effective or successful.
Bennett (1931:30-31) provided a more detailed classification 
of the heterogeneous heiau architectural unit in his archaeological 
survey of Kauai in 1928-29* He recorded 122 heiau. 
Some natural 
sites, his type 1 , were referred to as heiau because they were con­
sidered sacred. Type 2 heiau were small, less than 50 feet on the
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 212 ===

r
195 I
longest side. Bennett referred to Emory's description of a small
heiau on Lanai as also applicable to those on Kauai:
Presumably these heiaus were used in private family worship, 
or in the practices of an interior order of the priesthood.
Most of the structures for worship of this kind were single 
houses surrounded by a paling fence or a stone wall, or a 
simple, unroofed enclosure— structures which would leave 
ruins similar to those of an ordinary house site, or so incon­
spicuous as to escape notice (Emory 1969:68).
Bennett distinguished three categories of Type 2 heiau: 
open platforms,
walled enclosures, and terraced platforms (l931:3l). His type 3 
heiau were large and within these he distinguished five major cate­
gories. Heiau in the first of these five categories had platforms 
which were large and open and were on level ground or terraced against 
a slope, on the crown of a hill, or consisted of two more terraced 
divisions in which the division is not prominent. Heiau in the sec­
ond category were walled enclosures that were either square or rectan­
gular, divisioned enclosures, or compound enclosures. The third cate­
gory included the terraced heiau, a series of platforms or enclosures 
on slopes which required terracing to level the platforms. It is a 
combination of platform, terrace, and enclosure; however, the third 
category is distinct because of the definite division by facings If to 
10 feet tall (1931:3^+). Bennett divided large, terraced heiau into 
three groups: two-terrace types, three-terrace types, and four-terrace
types. His fourth category was the round heiau. His fifth category 
included unclassified structures, of which he found only two on Kauai 
(1931:35). Cut stone was almost never used in the construction of 
heiau; one recently discovered example on the island of Hawaii has not 
been described.

Heiau features noted by Bennett include house foundations 
from 8 by 10 feet to 20 by 75 feet; terraces along one end or side of 
the heiau which served as bases for other temple features such as 
uprights, oracle towers, and images; raised stone-paved platforms 
interpreted to be altars, which averaged 8 feet wide by 10 feet long 
and 1 to 2 feet high; in some sites, foundation stones which sup­
ported the anuu or oracle tower; steps or entryways; and a number of 
pits, luakini, for tapu rubbish, which range in diameter from 5 to 15 
feet (Bennett 1931:M+-^5)• Upright slabs of wood or stone represented 
another important heiau feature, although Bennett found only one 
upright stone slab on Kauai and of course, most of the wood uprights 
and images were no longer preserved. One or two heiau included unique 
features such as chambers, stone towers, springs, ponds, pens, and 
ovens. Graves were fairly common on heiau (Bennett 1931:^-6) .
Bennett noted the presence of extended burial in small stone cist 
graves in heiau as well as near house sites (1931:28-29).

New Zealand
An important question for understanding the relation of Maori 
culture to the rest of Polynesia concerns the absence of anything like 
the large marae so widely distributed throughout the rest of 
Polynesia. The absence of such structures in the otherwise clearly 
Polynesian culture is most often explained by the contention that the 
Maori settlers had left central Polynesia before the marae developed 
there.
There are a number of recorded sacred places in Wew Zealand 
and one on closely related Chatham Island (Simmons 1964:67, Fig. 5), 
which in some features resemble simple marae structures further north
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 217 ===

in central Polynesia. The "tuahu" described by Best (192*+, 1:288-289) 
were "small open spaces in brush, bush, braken, or flax grove. Some 
were marked by one or more unworked stones, or a small wooden post."
He illustrates one with four upright stone slabs in a row.
Pitcairn
Although we know almost nothing of the marae features on
Pitcairn Island, the appearance of stone statues and some early marae
description suggest similarities with Easter Island; this fact, as
well as the island's location, make it important. Beechey provided
the earliest documentation for marae and statues on the island, who,
quoting the last surviving Bounty mutineer, reports:
Upon this eminence the mutineers, on their arrival, found 
four images about six feet in height, placed upon a plat­
form; and according to Adam's description, not unlike the 
morais of Easter Island, excepting that they were on a much 
smaller scale. One of these images, which had been preserved, 
was a rude representation of the human figure to the hips, 
and was hewn out of a piece of red lava (Beechey 1931,l:8l).
Bones were also found in the vicinity of the marae ruins.
Moerenhout who visited the island in 1829 comments on a marae 
that reportedly had eight to ten foot statues at corners of platforms 
made of well-joined masonry (1837, 1 :53-5*0 * He also saw a three and 
a half foot tall stone bust.

but the stones and three statues which reportedly stood on this marae 
were missing (Routledge 1919:313-31*0 * She mentioned finding an image

Structural 
continuity is recognizable in the overall form; in the use of special 
kinds of carved or dressed stone, especially on Easter Island; in the 
persistence of specialized architectural features, such as stylized 
pavements, curbs and cists; and in the continued use of stone 
uprights or statuary. The sanctity (tapu status) of marae is 
reflected by prehistoric settlement patterns, in which persistent 
spatial disjunction is evident between marae and dwelling structures.

on the antiquity of marae in Polynesia. It is clear that Easter 
Island has the earliest dated religious architecture in Polynesia 
and that the earliest known religious structures of other islands 
were constructed considerably later. Despite its isolation, Easter 
Island was settled very early. The Marquesas and Hawaii exhibit a 
more complex pattern of internal organization in religious ceremonial 
centers, but the earliest two Marquesan examples, platforms under­
lying later modifications, date back only to about A.D. 1300. Some 
Marquesan me'ae expansions took place in protohistoric times. In 
Hawaii the earliest radiocarbon dated heiau foundation is from 
around A.D. 1600, even though there must be earlier ones. Contrary 
to what Suggs said, no ancient marae forms are known, except in 
hypothetical terms, outside of Easter Island. Emory's association 
of A.D. 1000 (a very problematic date) with the Decker Island marae 
is the earliest indication of non-Easter Island marae. Dated marae 
from other parts of Polynesia are late in time; many were observed 
in use during historic times.

203 ]
Stone and wood images constituted an important part of marae 
in the Marquesas and Hawaii; stone statues were used on Ra'ivavae, 
Easter Island, and Pitcairn. Almost all of the other East Polynesian 
islands had wood or stone images, though these were not as large or 
as important as in the islands above. Some small stone images were 
found in dated archaeological contexts. Those recovered by Emory 
from Necker Island may be nearly 1000 years old. Sinoto found some 
small statues in the Marquesas which he placed in his cultural phase 
A.D. 600 to 1300: these images were similar to those from Necker 
Island. All the large images from the Marquesas are dated later 
than A.D. 1500. The earliest dated Easter Island statues carved of 
Rano Raraku tuff are the two from Tahai; both of these date between 
A.D. 1000 and 1200. The larger of these, the one on Ahu Ko te Riku, 
was over 5 meters tall and about 18 tons. Easter Islanders, like the 
Marquesans, used red pigment to paint some of the large stone statues.
Stone or wood court uprights, characteristic of Central 
Polynesia, also occurred in the Marquesas and occasionally in Hawaii, 
The original pattern of uprights on the platform seems to have been 
replaced by statues in the Marquesas and on Easter Island. Marae 
usually had multiple platforms and subsidiary altars, this is espe­
cially clear in the Tuamotus. Cists or pits of some form were 
present in all marae. Burials were found only sporadically in pre­
historic marae even though marae were reportedly used in historic 
times as tombs. There were tombs and burial pits constructed in 
marae but on Easter Island, Tahiti, and on other islands they were 
put in after the marae were constructed. Other tombs outside the 
marae complex obscure the relationship between East Polynesian marae
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 221 ===

r~ 
2dm
marae and the tombs of West Polynesia. Marae were consecrated for 
religious activity by the head of the kin group who built the struc­
ture. Marae were not constructed as tombs, but as altars. A change 
from an altar to a tomb or cemetery occurred on Easter Island during 
the Decadent Phase, that is, after the images were no longer appealed 
to.
Perishable structures on the marae are not well attested to 
archaeologically. However, a number of platforms can be recognized 
as house foundations in Marquesan tohua and me'ae as well as in 
Hawaiian heiau. Houses on marae are examined in greater detail in 
Chapter III.
Religious structures associated with distinct kin-defined 
social units have been recorded on Easter Island. Recognition of 
this association is possible as a result of archaeological survey.
The importance of the relationship of social units to religious struc­
tures will be illustrated in a later discussion of social organization.
The Emerging Pattern of Marae 
Development in Polynesia
The discussion of temporal relationships between marae and 
their diffusion is limited if only dated structures are examined. 
Suggested relationships can be expanded by considering the evidence 
from portable artifacts. It is possible to expand the cultural con­
text of the evolving marae complex with added data on language and 
ethnography presented in Chapter III.
Portable artifacts suggest a pattern of Polynesian settlement 
that began with the initial occupation of Tonga and Samoa, followed 
by the first settlement of the Marquesas, possibly as early as
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 222 ===

f-  
205]
100 B.C. 
Subsequent migration from the Marquesas to the Society 
Islands was followed by a third movement to Easter Island around 
A.D. 1|00 to 500 (Sinoto 1966, 1968). Some artifacts, primarily adzes 
and fishing equipment, provide some insight into the early contacts, 
even though at present Easter Island tools show little time depth.
The Marquesas are recognized as the most likely primary dispersal 
center for East Polynesia because of the early date of settlement 
and because of artifact comparisons. The rest of East Polynesia, 
according to Sinoto (1968:116-11.8), was settled by later migrations 
from two major centers, the Marquesas and the Society Islands, in 
the following order: 
from the Society Islands to New Zealand, from
the Marquesas to Hawaii, from the Marquesas to New Zealand, and 
from the Society Islands to Hawaii.
Bellwood (1970) questioned Sinoto's case for the Marquesas as 
a primary dispersal center and Emory's idea that the Marquesas group 
was the primary point of derivation for all East Polynesian marae. 
Although Emory (1939:50) saw close relationships between Mangarevan 
marae and Easter Island ahu and related both to the Marquesas, Bellwood 
(1970:101) rightly pointed out that on the basis of known marae archi­
tecture the two are not clearly associated with the Marquesas; there 
are, however, close similarities in other cultural aspects.
An early East Polynesian marae pattern, including especially 
an altar, court, and uprights, is recognizable in all of the islands 
examined here. A later association of larger, more complicated marae 
is identifiable in Hawaii and the Marquesas. Mangareva and Easter 
Island may be part of this later pattern because of the absence of 
court uprights.

Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.

CHAPTER III 
THE ETHNOGRAPHIC PERSPECTIVE
Comparing ethnographic data on the use of Polynesian religious 
centers is essential for this study, because the record of religious 
behavior for Polynesian island cultures is -uneven and because there 
is almost no direct information regarding the use of ahu on Easter 
Island. The term ’’religious behavior" is used here in a broad sense, 
indicating behavior that is in consequence of belief in the supernat­
ural as defined within the cultural system. A composite picture of 
Polynesian religion, one that fills in gaps in the uneven ethnographic 
record, is best viewed by looking at the totality of Polynesian cul­
tural systems. The use of marae in the islands of central Polynesia, 
in the Marquesas, and in Hawaii, provide insights into Easter Island 
ahu use in historic and, by projection, in prehistoric times.
West Polynesia
Any discussion of the ethnographic data on Polynesian reli­
gion and marae use must determine the relationship between the Western 
and Eastern (or Central-Marginal) islands within the broader culture 
area. The degree of contrast and divergence is important because con­
siderable differences exist between the religious ceremonial structures 
of West and East Polynesia and because of the differences in the basic 
content of the religious complexes.
L 
206 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 224 ===

r
207l
Burrows (1938, 1939s 19*+0) first detailed the cultural differ­
ences between the two regions of Polynesia. He saw a fundamental unity 
of Polynesian culture corresponding to the unity of the language.
Until recently Polynesian languages were thought to reflect the cul­
tural dichotomy between West and East Polynesia as suggested by 
Burrows, that is, West and East Polynesian were major language sub­
groups whose member languages coincide with societies Burrows identi­
fied as belonging to the geographically and culturally defined areas 
of West and East Polynesia (Green 1966:8). However, recent linguistic 
research indicates that the dichotomy between West and East Polynesia 
is not as clear-cut as was once thought and that the Samoan language 
particularly shows closer relationships to East Polynesian languages 
than Tongan does (Pawley 1966; Grace 1968). This view suggests that 
there is a Tongic subgroup coordinate with a Nuclear Polynesian sub­
group of Polynesian. Samoic and Eastern Polynesian appear to be sub­
groups within the Nuclear Polynesian, expressing a closer relation­
ship. The linguistic affinity according to Green (1967a, 1968, 1969) 
is supported by archaeological evidence, although this is disputed 
by Gol. 
(1971)5 as was shown in Chapter II.
Burrows listed forty cultural traits which distinguished West 
from Central-Marginal Polynesia; many of these were items of material 
culture (1938:88-90). Ethnographic items discussed by Burrows should 
still be considered as reflecting the basic dichotomy between West and 
East Polynesia, at least in historic times. 
Some of Burrow's distinc­
tions regarded the religious systems. Mythology explaining the creation 
of mankind shows this dichotomy. West Polynesia has an evolutionary 
type of myth which holds that the first human beings emerged from
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 225 ===

r
rocks or earth, either directly or through intermediate forms, such 
as plants or maggots. If a deity is mentioned, his role is that of a 
spectator or, at most, that of an assistant rather than as a "creator" 
(Burrows 191+0:350). Characteristic of East Polynesia is a procreative 
myth in which a "male god brings mankind into existence either by 
copulation with a mass of earth or by forming a female being out of 
earth and impregnating her" (Burrows 19^0:351). Another type of myth 
which shows regional differences refers to an underworld or mythical 
island to the west. In West Polynesia this legendary place is Pulotu, 
the post-mortal resting place of certain high-ranking individuals and 
gods. The legendary island to the west is called Hawaiki in East 
Polynesia. Occasionally Hawaiki is referred to as an underworld 
(Po); however, it is primarily thought of as an island to the west and 
as an ancestral homeland rather than a resting place for the dead as 
Pulotu is in West Polynesia. As far as is known Easter Island myth­
ology does not have the Hawaiki concept and is unique in this respect.
Among the gods of Polynesian religion, Tangaloa is reportedly 
the only one of the four widely known gods (Tangaroa, Tane, Tu, and 
Rongo) of importance in West Polynesia. Burrows (19^:35^) stated: 
"Tangaloa was the only primal god (associated with creation and com­
monly with great regions as the sky or the sea) in Tonga, Eutuna,
Uvea, and Niue." Tangaloa is also dominant in Samoa, although Tu is 
also mentioned. Handy (1927:97) writes:
The Samoan and Tongan mythologies postulate the preexis­
tence of Tangaloa, the Supreme Being and creator, as does the 
lore of the Society Islands that of Taaroa; but in Samoa and 
Tonga there is no suggestion of the name Io or Iho.
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 226 ===

r
2 0 9 !
Handy took this to he a major difference between West and East 
Polynesia; however, Tregear's (190^:456) contention that the concept 
of a supreme creator (Io or Iho) was a widespread one in East 
Polynesia has been demonstrated by Emory (1938) and Barrere (1967:
103, 106-107) to be the result of recent revisions in creation myths 
due to contact with European missionaries. Therefore, this can no 
longer be viewed as a major difference between the aboriginal reli­
gious concepts.
Margaret Mead indicated one important religious concept on 
Manu'a which differentiates West from East Polynesia, the concept of 
tapu (1969:117):
The essential Polynesian tapu attitude is one based upon 
the concept of the contagious qualities of sacred and defiling 
things, and the infinite extension of this contagious quality, 
as in the famous Maori story of the people who died of eating 
vegetables which grew on the site where a chief's house had 
once stood. Using a chief's name, eating the remainder of 
his food, sitting where he sat, drinking where he has drunk, 
are all parts of this same concept of contagion. Whether the 
original sanctity or defilement be the result of an inherent 
quality like sex or rank, of a state like childbirth, or men­
struation, or of an activity such as building a war canoe, 
the principle remains the same— people, objects, and places 
are possessed of a contagious quality. Without this element 
of contagion the tapu may be said to have lost its Polynesian 
character.
There are some contrasts to this in the way the Samoans view tapus 
associated with family gods; Mead noted (1969:12^, 118):
It was forbidden to injure, kill, or eat of the plant or 
animal in which one 's family god or village god was embodied, 
under penalty of the wrath and chastisement of the god. Wo 
object which had been dedicated to the god might be given 
away. The idea of contagion was not involved in this prohi­
bition.
and:
The prohibition surrounding the family god looks westward to 
Melanesian totemism for its closest parallels.

=== PAGE 227 ===

r
Thus, the typical Polynesian tapu of birth rank has been greatly atten­
uated or reinterpreted in Samoa. Mead found the tapus surrounding the 
Tui Manu'a, whose food, hair, fingernail clippings, and bed had a con­
tagious quality, to be the sole exception (1969:118-119).
In addition to the above general distinctions, ethnographic 
materials give considerable insight into the differences between the 
religious complexes of the major western Polynesian islands and those 
of the eastern Polynesian islands. A brief outline of the significant 
features of religious concepts, gods, ceremonial centers, priests and 
ceremonies is presented for the Polynesian islands.
Tongan gods were thought of as formless spirits who could 
become incarnate into any shape. Besides manifesting themselves in 
human form, as a priest or priestess, the gods also appeared in animal
god and the priest was the taula otua (anchor of the god).
As mentioned, Tangaloa was well known and existed in several 
forms; however, he was always a sky god rather than a sea god, more 
characteristic of his East Polynesian association. No temples or 
priests were dedicated to Tangaloa, although his role in the creation 
of solid land and man is clear in Tongan mythology. Hina, revered 
throughout Polynesia, also lacked temples and priests. One manifesta­
tion of Tangaloa, Tangaloa Tufunga, was a patron of carpenters; he 
was the only occupational deity Gifford found (1929:319). The widely 
known culture heroes or gods called the Maui were thought of in
The Religious Features of Island Groups
Tonga
form, and in this case they were referred to as the vaka (boat) of the
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 228 ===

Tonga as culture heroes rather than gods (Gifford 1929:290). Another 
important deity was Hikuleo, the god of the Tui Tonga, or king, who 
served as his priest and intermediary (Farmer 1855:129)• Gifford could 
not ascertain whether the deceased Tui Tonga were actually worshipped, 
even though offerings were placed at their tombs. A temple for 
Hikuleo, a famous place of refuge, once stood at the site of the pres­
ent town of Haano, Haapai. Sanctuaries were important in all 
Polynesian societies; in Tonga sanctuaries to which fugitives might 
flee were called hufanga or fale hufanga. The deity Tui Haa Fakafanua 
had one major sanctuary in Tongatabu and one or two in Haapai; he was 
still an extant god at the time of Gifford's study in 1920-21. McKern 
excavated a mound that was formerly a site of a temple for Tui Haa 
Fakafanua on Pangaimotu (1929:101+).
The shrines or temples of gods like Hikuleo, Taufa, and 
Fehulini, were like ordinary Tongan houses in contrast to the elabo­
rate temples of East Polynesia. Tonga and Samoa, in conformity with 
a less formalized system of worship, had no elaborate temples, though 
there were tombs (langi) for sacred chiefs which correspond in form 
and use to tombs in Hawaii, the Society, Marquesas, and Cook islands 
(Handy 1927:175)* The tombs in Tonga and the less pretentious ones 
in Samoa served as places of worship at the time of funerary rites, 
memorial festivals, and seasonal offerings of first fruits (Handy 
1927:176).
Each lineage seems to have had a deity and a presiding priest 
who was in charge of the fakafaanga, precious and sacred possessions, 
such as fine mats, of the god. All members of a lineage worshipped the 
god which was represented by its chief. Children worshipped the god
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 229 ===

of their father and, usually, the wife adopted the god of her husband 
(Gifford 1929:318). Gods were associated with patrilineal kin groups, 
and one speaks of the gods of lineages, not of the gods of localities. 
Often more than one god was attached to a lineage.
Tongan priests, male or female, became inspired by gods, by 
lesser spirits, by spirits of ancestors of chiefs or by recently 
deceased chiefs. If a spirit that manifested itself in a priest was 
of human origin, it was invariably that of a great chief, such as the 
Tui Tonga. Allegedly, mediums who were animated by the spirits of 
dead Tui Tonga had no special houses of worship. Apparently they were 
not regular priests already dedicated to the service of some god, but 
were individuals who were suddenly inspired.
Furthermore, Tongan priests functioned as curers of disease 
through intercession with the gods. Sacrifices were offered and 
oracles were obtained through the priest who became inspired by the 
god; the oracle usually foretold the outcome of the illness.
Priesthood was generally retained in families; it usually 
passed from father to son, but when a spirit unexpectedly took pos­
session of a person, this person could be elevated to priesthood by 
the Tui Tonga. The terms taula otua, taula eiki, taula tevolo, 
toumama, faahikehe, and perhaps feaa were used for priest (Gifford 
19°9:3l6).
The priest of a lineage or family acted not only as a medium 
between the lineage and the gods, but also as a medium between the 
lineage and spirits of its dead. It was thought that ancestors could 
cause illness, a belief which doubtless led to the making of propi­
tiatory offerings. However, other, more direct, and retaliatory
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 230 ===

r
213]
means were sometimes resorted to. The grave of the offending ancestor 
might he broken into, the bones taken out and beaten, and imprecations 
uttered (Gifford 1929:319).
The priests of the gods did not officiate at sacrifices, this 
was done by the supplicants. Human sacrifice, performed less fre­
quently than the common finger amputation, occurred under the follow­
ing circumstances (Gifford 1929:321):
1 . 
sometimes to secure the recovery of a great chief from 
illness,
2. following the death of a great chief when his widows 
might be strangled or burned alive in ancient times,
3. at the funeral of a great chief, when the undertaker 
and (or) certain commoners would be imprisoned in the 
tomb,
k. at the dedication of certain temples,
5. perhaps on the occasion of the launching of great 
vessels.
On Tonga, as in Samoa, the tombs, faitoka, of chiefs were 
places of communal worship, but strictly speaking, they were not 
temples. On both islands a class of houses was consecrated to local
tutelary deities, and these were, at least part of the time, quite
distinct from the sepulchral sites. In Tonga, besides the tombs that 
served as places of worship, special houses were built in the usual 
style of dwellings; these stood in carefully tended enclosures which 
were dedicated to particular gods of their cult and to their priests
(Handy 1927:176). These god houses stood on a village assembly 
ground, called malae, in some cases, on mounds or in some other 
place.

=== PAGE 231 ===

r
Uvea
Uvean culture was essentially Tongan (Burrows 1937) and there­
fore supplies further detail on West Polynesian religious life.
Burrows, quoting Henquel, described the classes of Uvean gods (1937:
81+):
There were three kinds of gods. The first kind consisted 
of primal or creator gods, atua tupua. These were the great 
gods . . . .  These gods ruled the universe, but did not work 
and did not take possession of men (ulusino) as oracles, but 
remained aloof in chiefly majesty.
The second kind of gods were atua tanutanu. These were 
the spirits of men of distinction during their lifetime, prin­
cipally chiefs; and when they were dead they became gods . .
. . It is these gods who took possession of either men or 
women . . . .
The third kind of gods were the atua muli (rear or lesser 
gods). They did not take possession of men but remained in 
special objects called vaka (canoe, vessel), or fakafaanga.
It was they who had power to inflict disease.
The primal gods were Tangaloa, Mafuike, and Hikuleo, Maui Atalanga 
and Maui Kisikisi were primal demigods.
The Uveans had some sort of god house; Henquel called these 
malumalunga. They also had a kind of idol, tamapua, to which they 
paid divine honors (Burrows 1937:86). Burrows made little mention 
of the malae, except to note (1937:67): "The malae, an open space
usually in front of the chief's house, is public to the extent that 
all public assemblies are held there." The scale of public construc­
tion was small; this reflects the lower status of Uvean rulers in 
comparison to the rulers of Tonga. The royal tombs, small versions 
of Tongan models, were earthen mounds faced with stone slabs, the 
largest of which, Tongatapu, was 30 feet wide by 15 feet long and 
about 3 feet high. Goldman referred to crude boulders on malae which 
served as back-rests for chiefs (1970:319)*
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 232 ===

r
Priests officiated at some public ceremonies, they practiced 
socery (faka-lauakau) and healing (fai-too). The major type of 
priest was called taula-atua (god-anchor-lines).
Available description of Uvean malae and ceremonies lacks 
details regarding the relation of the malae to individual chiefs and 
priests or kin groups. All Goldman is able to say, for example, is 
that the ramified lineage is dependent upon some ritual connection 
which is predicated upon genealogical rank (1970:326).
Futuna
Burrows found the categorization of gods in Futuna to be simi­
lar to that of Uvea. Here, too, Tangaloa and Mauike were primal gods, 
with the addition of Sina and Tsinilau (1936:105). There is no trace 
of the gods Tu, Tane, or Rongo. Reference in old songs to rocks of 
Tangaloa, Kula, and Lita shows that certain rocks were associated 
with these gods, perhaps regarded as embodiments of them. These were 
called pae-atua or divine stones (Burrows 1936:108). 
Some tombs and 
malae were sacred. Burrows was told that nothing is planted on the 
malae of the old Toloke kings, because when this was tried the spirits 
tore out the seeds and destroyed them. Burrows described a malae at 
the old village of Loka, Alofi, as a large, artificially leveled area 
about 180 feet long and 120 feet wide having a stone terrace wall on 
the sea side and a row of upright stones on the landward side (1936: 
12l+). This is the only well preserved Futuna malae and the one Emory 
said "closely resembles the most elementary marae of eastern 
Polynesia" (Burrows 1936:233).
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 233 ===

r 
2i6]
Persons in whom the gods descended were priests called toe- 
matua, vaka-atua (vessel of the god), or fa'asinga-atua (kind of god, 
or dynasty of gods). The name taula-atua was not used in Futuna. 
Ceremonies held on the malae included feasts to appease the gods. A 
ceremony aimed at ending droughts, in which participants go to the 
top of a hill to pray for rain, has parallels to a similar ceremony 
on Easter Island.
Samoa
Most of the features discussed here are characteristic of 
Manu'a. Mead thought that religion in Manu'a was never as developed 
or diversified as in western Samoa, because of the small population 
and the lack of war. The basic religious complex, however, is 
believed to be the same as in western Samoa.
Manu'ans distinguished between atua (god), tupua (local and 
family gods), and aitu (demons and ghosts of the dead). Samoan 
mythology gives clear indication of Tangaloa's importance in reli­
gion. Mead noted that in Manu'an vocabulary Tangaloa is spoken of as 
an atua, while many of the lesser gods were called aitu, meaning 
simply spirit (1969:157). Elevating Tangaloa to the status of high­
est god may be in part due to the effects of Christianity.
Tangaloa's children were described as Sao (patron of housebuilders), 
Sini, Tingilau, and Tangaloa-a-u (patron of travelers). According to 
Mead (1969:158), they may have been atua in earlier times. Atua had 
no manifestations; they were said to have been prayed to only by very 
high chiefs or upon formal social occasions. Pili, possibly also an
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 234 ===

r
2171
atua, was the patron of agriculture and fishing in western Samoa and 
in Fiji.
Ordinary people prayed to tupua who were incarnate in some 
bird, fish, or plant and sometimes to gods of villages. Mead referred 
to tupua as totems (1969:159)- In Manu'a Mead found no belief in 
descent from the tupua; that is, tupua are not considered to be ances­
tors of their worshippers. The tupua descended in families in the 
male line and the matai (head) of the family was responsible for 
their service. Certain tapus were associated with them; the estab­
lishment of tapus and warnings of danger and death seem to have been 
the main services performed by tupua for their worshippers (Mead 
1969:159) • The tupua were formally invoked in the morning and evening 
kava ceremonies and in events of illness or misfortune. Sometimes 
they spoke through mediums (taula aitu) and declared the reason for 
their wrath— usually some careless infringement of a tapu. The vil­
lage gods, like Fuilelagi of Olosega, described by Mead (1969:160) 
were not different in kind, in form, in tapu, or in function. The 
village gods may have been outgrowths of tupua.
Aitu included several types of spirits: demon spirits, spirit
guardians, and ghosts of the dead. Spirit guardians included aitu tau 
mau, spirits that haunt specific localities, and tapua fanua, spirits 
that guard land boundaries. All aitus were addressed by the courtesy 
term of saua ali1i (cruel chiefs) (Mead 1969:160). Freeman corrects 
this to read sauali’i (1972:76).
Manu'ans recognized two types of priests: taula aitu who
could converse with spirits or be directly possessed by spirits and 
va'a Tagaloa who possessed power to utter curses (fai tui), find lost
L

2181
objects or find out thieves. Little information is available about 
either of these practitioners.
Residents of Ta'u and Fitiuta agreed that the sun was once 
worshipped and received human sacrifices (Mead 1969:162). Further 
evidence for the practice of human sacrifice in Manu'a is offered by 
a tale about a chief of a village which preceded Fitiuta, who used to 
launch his canoe over human bodies, and by a legend about youths and 
maidens who were sent to the Tui Manu'a and were buried alive in the 
malae (Mead 1969:l6U).
The Manu'an material, supported by western Samoan material, 
suggests that the belief in a reincarnating family god who had to be 
treated with respect and that the belief in the activity of the ghosts 
of the dead were the two most constant and active religious concepts 
in Samoa (Mead 1969:165).
In Samoa, as in Tonga, some villages had houses of the usual 
form which were dedicated to the tutelar god of the community. In 
other places, the council house of the village, which belonged to the 
chief, was also its temple (Handy 1927:176). Normally these god 
houses stood on the village assembly ground, the malae.
Therr is some ethnohistoric information on religious facets 
of mound use. Williamson considered ancestral stone seats in the 
marae of the Society Islands to be comparable to ancestral wooden 
posts in Samoa (Williamson 1937:70). Scott noted that the large 
Pulemelei mound had an apparent circle of stone seats (1969:89).
Stair in 1897 referred to the "pulemelei" type platform mound and 
noted that such raised stone terraces, from 50 to 70 feet square and 
several feet high, were built for houses of people of high rank and

In areas of Samoan religion and ceremony, social organization,

Tokelau Islands
The only other major West Polynesian island for which there is 
ethnographic information regarding the use and architecture of malae 
is Tokelau. Macgregor says (1937:59):
The elements in the religion of the Tokelau people were 
characteristic of the religions of western Polynesia. The 
pantheon was comprised of a supreme deity who resided in the 
sky and a group of nature gods who dwelt in the world. Wo 
stone marae or platforms were erected to the gods. Ritual 
was very slight and almost entirely confined to an annual 
ceremony to the supreme deity. Communication was held with 
ancestral spirits. Nature spirits abounded in the woods and 
sea.
The supreme deity recorded by Macgregor was Tui Tokelau who resided in 
the sky. Macgregor felt that several characteristics of the supreme 
deity, the temple and sacred enclosure, the coral slabs wrapped \:c. 
mats, and the national annual ceremony, strongly distinguished 
Tokelau religion from that of Tonga and Samoa, the "type religion" 
for all of western Polynesia (1937:162). Tokelauans distinguished 
between aitu, the souls of men, and the more powerful atua, gods.
Aitu advised their descendants; worship and communication with family 
gods were conducted in the homes (1937:66).
In Tokelau, each village had the usual Polynesian meeting 
ground, malae, where most of the religious ceremonies, all the dances, 
and the ceremonial division of fish, turtles, and whales, and other 
community festivities took place. A god house stood at one end at

the Tokelau Islands, to the upright stones, -which would

Priests were referred to as taulaitu (taula + aitu = 
taulaitu); others who are described as shamans or prophets were 
called vaka atua. Unlike the priests of Samoa who were outside the 
social pattern, the priests (taula) of Tokelau held a very high and 
important position in society. Macgregor saw this social position as 
resembling that of East Polynesian priests (1937:163). The vaka-atua, 
as in Samoa and Tonga, were tools of the gods.
The major religious ceremony held on the malae was the annual 
one of offerings and prayers to the supreme deity, Tui Tokelau, and 
resembled the Tahitian pai atua both in its organization and in its 
activities.
Summary
West Polynesian religious concepts were characterized by an 
evolutionary creation myth out of which Tangaloa, the most important 
god, arose. The important East Polynesian trio of Rongo, Tane, and 
Tu was absent though Hina and Maui did appear. In West Polynesia gods 
were called atua, lineage deities tupua, and spirits of ancestors 
aitu. There were occupational deities. It is specifically stated, 
at least for Samoa, that the members of a lineage were not descended 
from the lineage god, tupua.
Lineage priests dealt with lineage gods. Priests were of two 
types, taula aitu, inspirational, and va'a Tangaloa, disease curing.
No priests conducted or officiated ceremony. Religious ritual 
’evolved around the burial of important individuals, particularly of 
chiefs, at which human sacrifices were common, and perhaps around sun
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 239 ===

r
worship in earlier times. There were ghosts of the dead. Temples for 
religious activities were like an ordinary house in form, they may have 
appeared on tombs or on malae. In Samoa, apparently, the chief's 
council house fulfilled the same function.
East Polynesia
East Polynesian cosmogenic mythology describes evolutionary 
stages of creation. The four major pantheon gods or "superior gods" 
(Handy 1927:16), Tane, Rongo, Tu and Tangaroa, are commonly descend­
ants of two primal gods, either Vatea (Wakea) and Papa (Cook Islands 
and Hawaii), Rangi and Papa (New Zealand), or Atea and Atanua 
(Marquesas). It is in the descendant pantheon and in the succeeding 
stages of creation where the genealogies reflecting the procreative 
aspect of East Polynesian creation mythology are evident. The clear­
est way to support an individual's claim to power seems to have 
depended upon that individual's genealogical connection to the gods, 
and therefore, to mana.
Polynesia emphasizes their special status. A section devoted to 
detailed presentation of significant features of religious complexes 
on each of the islands follows.
Tane
Tane appears to have represented the male principle. Handy, 
quoting Best (1922:13-11+), used the Maori description of Tane which
Major Gods
The following discussion of the major pantheon gods of East

he thinks "preserves much of the genuine, original Polynesian thought"
(1927:99):
He is the fertilizer, the power that fertilized the Earth 
Mother and caused her to produce vegetation of all kinds. He 
represents light, and brought light into the world. He is 
the parent of the Dawn Maid, and the renewer of the life and 
light of Hina, the moon. He has many names to accord with 
his many functions. For instance, as Tane-mahuta he repre­

the Waiora a Tane of Maori myth, in which Hina-uri, the

and beautiful. Tane it is who warms the body of the Earth 
Mother, and is the cause and conserver of all life.
[Tane is the Personified form of the sun]
As the universal fertilizer, Tane is naturally credited with 
having produced trees and all plants . . . .
Another widespread god in East Polynesia was Tiki; according to 
Handy, Tiki was a personification of the procreative instrument of 
Tane (1927:99)• The word "tiki" was used throughout Polynesia for 
carved representations of ancestral deities. Tane played the major 
role in creating the first man out of earth in the mythologies of 
some islands, e.g., Hawaii and New Zealand. Tane was of little impor­
tance in the Marquesas; here the male principle and light belong to 
Atea rather than to Tane (Handy 1927:2^5), and it was Tiki who is said 
to have produced men by mating with a heap of sand which he piled up 
on the seashore (Hina-mata-one).
Tane was a major god as well as a god of woodcraft in the 
Society Islands. Tane was known in the Tuamotus and food was offered 
to him by social groups, 'Ati Tane, that were associated with him.
In Mangareva, Tane was not an important god, but he was a mere fisher­
man.
Tane was known but not worshipped in Mangaia until after immi­
grants brought the worship of Tane from Tahiti. Four of fourteen

names for Tane were represented by carved ironwood images in the

he had no marae and no incarnation; it appears that he was an aca­

Rongo
Although the term rongo in the Polynesian language refers to 
a number of concepts dealing with sound, the god Rongo was primarily 
associated with agriculture. However, in the Marquesas 'ono (Rongo) 
was the patron of chanting and of the ritual chanters, tuhuna 'o 'ono.
The Marquesan ka'ioi functioned like the 1arioi society of Tahiti with 
regard to Rongo. 
'Ono, according to Handy, was no longer worshipped 
as such in the Marquesas, having been replaced by Tohetika (Handy
1923:2^5).
Ro'o (Rongo) in the Society Islands was a rain god as indi­
cated by the following reference (Handy 1927:108):
. . . the gods of the temple "are directed to , = .Ro'o 
. . . the parent of clouds— to the formation and increase 
of clouds— to the blue cloud, the red cloud, and the low 
hungry cloud, and the horned or pointed cloud" (Ellis 
1831, 1:3^2-31+10.
This association is suggested also by the name Ro'o-te-roro'o, Ro’o- 
the-resounder, as Handy indicated (.1927:108), because he was heard in 
thunder. Ro'o was referred to as the "prayer-chanter" also in the 
Society Islands. Handy thought that in the Society Islands another god 
absorbed the features of Ro'o; this god, called 'Oro in historic times, 
was often thought to be a variant of Ro'o (Gill l876:llt). 
'Oro

received the first fruits and was patron of the 'arioi dancers in 
Tahiti. Handy said, "there can he no question but that Oro [ 'Oro]

Rogo had charge of tumeric. He had the letting down of

According to them, the finger was not pointed at a rainbow 
lest a person die (1938:^22).
Mangaian Rongorongo chanters had a very important role in marae cere­
mony. Rongo and twelve tribal gods were represented in a national 
god house in Mangaia. Rongo was a god of war here, although there is 
some evidence in myth that earlier he was the god of fertility and 
agriculture (Buck 193^+:l62). In Hawaii, Lono (Rongo) was the god of 
harvest; in this role he received the first fruit offerings at 
Makahiki ceremonies. He also functioned as the god of peace in Hawaii 
as well as elsewhere in Polynesia. In East Polynesia Rongo generally 
played the central role in the seasonal fertility cult of agricul­
turalists (Handy 1927:112).
Tu
The third major pantheon god in East Polynesia was Tu, com­
monly the god of war. Handy noted that Tu is mentioned in a Samoan 
account concerning a spear in a war canoe (1927:113).
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 243 ===

r
2261
Marquesan war parties were consecrated to Tu; those partici­
pating in war rites, including priests, their assistants, and warriors, 
were known as the 'ati Tu, the "family of Tu." Human sacrifices were 
offered at times of war. In the Society Islands and the Cook Islands 
Tu had been replaced by new war gods and is present only in myth. Tu 
was an important war god in the Tuamotus in historic times. Emory 
mentioned him in a list of gods recited at a marae ceremony as "Tu, 
and te ati Tu" (19*4-7:66). Tu is represented by wooden images and is 
the major functioning god of breadfruit in Mangareva; his role as war 
god was taken over by a deified ancestor, Te Agiagi (Buck 1938:*4-22).
Tu is mentioned in Mangaian myth, but he was not widely worshipped 
there. In Hawaii Ku (Tu) was the dominant god of the entire pantheon; 
he was also the national war god of the ruling king (Handy 1927:113- 
11*4-). Human sacrifices were offered to Ku in war time. Ku in Hawaii 
had a number of additional roles as patron of several crafts, such as 
canoe making, husbandry (Ku-ka-ao), bird catching (Ku-huluhulu-manu), 
fishing (Ku-ula) , and sorcery (Ku-ka-oe) (Handy 1927:11*4-). New 
Zealand's record shows that all war parties were consecrated to Tu., 
and that he was best known by the name Tu-mata-uenga (Tu-with-the- 
dreadful-countenance) (Tregear 1891:5*40).
Tangaroa
Tangaroa was the only god common to all the main Polynesian 
island groups. He was a god of fishing and of the sea in Samoa, 
though in Tonga he was always considered to be a sky god (Gifford 
1929:289).
