J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 244 ===

r
In the Marquesas Tana'oa (Tangaroa) was a god of the ocean and
a great patron of fishing craft. Fish offerings were made to him at 
his shrines. Ta'aroa was also a patron of fishermen in Tahiti; fish­
ermen prayed at the stone in Taiarapu which was "possessed by the 
spirit of Ta'a-roa" (Handy 1927:117)* Ta'aroa was credited here with 
making the first god house, fare atua, or ark, out of his own body 
(Henry 1928:136). Emory described turtle offerings to Tagaroa on 
marae in the Tuamotus (19^7:61-63). In the divine lineage of 
Mangareva, Tagaroa was third, following Atu-motua and Atu-moana, and 
Tagaroa-hurupapa followed Tagaroa in some recorded lineages and is 
regarded as first of the human 1akariki. Tagaroa was "the god who 
made all the things in the world" (Buck 1938:420). Tangaroa in 
Mangaia was a younger twin brother of Rongo with whom he shared the 
divinity of food, trees, fish, and other things (Tregear l891:U61+).
He was not an important god here in terms of actual worship. Kanaloa 
(Tangaroa) was not directly associated with the sea in Hawaii; accord­
ing to Handy (1937:117), he was replaced by Ku-ula. In contrast to 
Malo's statement (1951:112, 11^, 58) that Kanaloa was prayed to as a 
beneficient deity, Tregear (1891:^6^) describes him as an evil being, 
"ruler of the Infernal world (Po) and he is called Akua ino, kupu kino, 
'the Evil Spirit.'" In New Zealand, Tangaroa (or Takaroa) was recog­
nized as the Lord of the Ocean and a descendant of Rangi (Heaven) and 
Papa (Earth) (Tregear 1891:^63).
Other Gods
Another important deity was Hina, a moon goddess, the first 
woman in creation. 
She was well known in West Polynesia and farther

east. Other deities were Maui, a mythological culture hero; Mahuike,

Gods Invoked
The following section presents ethnographic material on those 
deities who were actually invoked or appealed to in East Polynesian 
religious ceremony. The features of the sacred sites, particularly 
marae; the priests or intermediaries; and finally, the ceremonies con­
ducted, especially those concerning the marae or marae related struc­
tures will be discussed in later sections.
Island Groups 
Marquesas
Handy grouped Marquesan gods into several categories: l) gods
of myth or creation; 2) departmental gods, gods of nature and of the 
elements, patrons of occupations, and gods of sickness; and 3) tutelary 
deities, which included personal, family, and tribal ancestral 
spirits. All of these were ancestral (1923:2Ml). Of the previously 
discussed East Polynesian pantheon gods, only Tana'oa and Tu were 
invoked in Marquesan ceremony.
Teuutoka, Teuuhua, and Tehitikaupeka are three sky gods who 
fall in the class of departmental gods. The subjects of the first two 
were referred to as "ati" of the god; the third had no designated sub­
jects .
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 246 ===

r
229!
Every occupation had. its own diety as did most other activi­
ties. Besides Tana'oa, each type of fishing had its own patron, some­
times represented by stone images which were supposed to bring luck. 
Pupuke was the god of sacred chanting as well as of the inspirational 
priest's house. The chief patrons of canoe-building and wood-working, 
Hopekoutoki and Motuhaiki, were legendary characters who were probably 
famous canoe-builders in antiquity.
Tutelary deities were all ancestral tribal spirits that had 
been elevated to the power of gods through ritual of chanting and sac­
rifice. These tribal deities formed an upper class of ancestral 
spirits; less important deities were venerated in private worship.
Bodies were temporarily mummified and skulls and other relics were 
preserved carefully as embodiments of these ancestral spirits.
Gods of different classes appear to have dwelled in different 
regions. Deified ancestral spirits who were tribal gods (etua) lived 
in regions of the sky and came to their temples only when they entered 
their named images or their inspirational priests. Handy recorded 
three to ten levels of this sky world; the number of levels varied 
with the informant (1923:253). Ancestral deities of lesser importance 
resided in the lower world of Havai'i (Handy 1923:21*7).
The original gods of creation were never thought to be 
embodied in any visible object or image (Handy 1923:21*8). All the 
images at tribal temples were representations of ancestral tutelary 
tribal deities. On the other hand, one would suspect that there 
must have been images or representations of some kind, probably 
unshaped stones, of Tana'oa at the platforms of Fatu Uku where offer­
ings were made to him by fishermen. The tutelary tribal deities had
L 
J

r 
230!
human embodiments in the sense that the inspirational priests at 
times of possession were regarded as actual living embodiments of 
gods.
The fanaua was a vicious female demon who attacked women.
Tokai, also called fanaua, were named sacred places where the bodies 
of women who died in childbirth were buried. When such a site was 
consecrated to a powerful fanaua, there was a special attendant and 
offerings were made (Handy 1923:253). Each tokai had its presiding 
demon who supposedly killed the bodies buried there. Tokai served as 
places of disposal for contaminated clothing and for objects of defil­
ing nature associated with women.
Society Islands
The god 'Oro was apparently the one to whom the greatest monu­
ments were constructed. The major marae of Ra'iatea, Taputapu-atea, 
was dedicated to 'Oro, as were other marae with the same name in 
Tahiti, Moorea, and even the Tuamotus (Henry 1928:107, 119-127, 130).
The incarnation of 'Oro at Taputapu-atea marae in Tahiti is described 
as a six foot long log of toa or aito wood covered with sennit and 
with red, yellow, and black feathers to give it all the original 
attributes of the terrible god (Henry 1928:130).
Henry reports that the god of the smaller, family or ances­
tral marae was always a family secret; this family god would be 
invoked at the marae. The gods invoked at community social marae 
were of the first order, that is, Ta'aroa, Tane, ’Oro, Hau and so on.
The "clan" who invoked a particular god at the marae would be called

Tuamotus
Emory documented Tuamotuan chants which describe the impor­
tance of the turtle as a marae offering for Tangaroa (19^7:61-63).
This indicates that first-order gods were invoked on marae in the 
Tuamotus. Montiton described a chief invoking all of the following 
gods on the marae at one occasion (187^:379):
Tauruhua, Kai-nuku, Puniava, Ruanuku, Tu-teao-tea, Tu-

A long list of family gods (immediate ancestors) followed this list
and these invocations were repeated several times during the turtle
sacrifice (Emory 19^7:66). In Montiton's account there is no mention
of turtle offerings to Tangaroa.

Mangareva
Mangarevan deities were relegated into two classes, major gods 
(etua nui) and minor gods (etua iti). Major gods include: Atu-motua,
Atu-moana, Atea, and Tangaroa, their origins are so remote that they 
are called gods without beginning. The major functioning deity was 
Tu, the eldest son of Tangaroa. Lesser gods, according to Buck 
(1938:Ul8), can be classed into a) descendants of Tangaroa, b) gods of 
foreign origin, c) deified ancestors, d) deified abortions (mota'u).
Tu was the god of breadfruit; the department of war, which was 
normally associated with Tu, was delegated to the deified ancestor,
Te Agiagi. Buck (1938:1+22) noted:
The ceremonial of Tu was conducted on the permanent 
maraes constructed of basaltic stone. It is said that he 
was introduced to Mangareva by Tupa, to whom was attributed 
the construction of important maraes on the various islands 
for the worship of Tu. The ritual recited on these maraes 
commences with the invocation of Tu. His priests were 
selected from the noble families.
Wooden images representing Tu were set up in some of the religious
houses. The complicated ritual addressed to Tu for promoting the
fertility of breadfruit trees is recorded in a ceremony known as the
marae uaikai (Laval 1938:321-331).
The name of Ruanuku, the first-born son of Tu, appears fre­
quently in the lists of gods recited in religious ritual; two marae 
were named after him. Marlu was a son of Ruanuku and so was Koro- 
eriki, the god of song experts, pou kapa. The Mangarevans followed 
the general Polynesian pattern of deifying ancestors in order that 
they might have their support not only in this world but also in the 
next. Some families had two such gods, chiefs had more; King Ma-teoa
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 250 ===

r
233I
had over twenty (Buck 1938:2+25). Lesser gods, the deities of the 
second and third class priests, are of uncertain origin; they were 
probably deified ancestors.
Apparently, invocations were frequently repetitious. The 
greater gods were invoked first, the lesser ones second. Buck (1938: 
2+67-2+68) said: 
"This is evident in many of the invocations which com­
mence with Tu and his family and are followed by Viriga and a number 
of minor gods. 
Sometimes Atu-motua, Atu-moana, Atea, and Tangaroa 
precede Tu; and sometimes a series of ancestors follows the minor 
gods."
Human wood representations in Mangareva are more naturalistic 
in bodily proportions than anywhere else in Polynesia. The tiki were 
mostly of Tu (Buck 1938:2+60). The native text, describing the period 
of Te Mateoa (six generations before 1900, Laval 1938:Table XXVIII) 
says:
Tarai te tiki e mea ku poro mai te etua 'ou i te 'a'a o te taura 
(An image was carved because a new god had spoken through the 
mouth of a priest) (Buck 1938:2+60).
Most of the wooden images were three to five feet tall.
Southern Cook Islands (Mangaia)
The Mangaian religious complex included the gods, their images 
and incarnations, religious structures, priests, and offerings, chants, 
and feasts. The deities of Mangaia included the sons of Vatea and 
Papa, importations from Rarotonga, locally deified ancestors, and 
vaguely remembered gods from older myths.
The five sons »f Vatea were Tangaroa, Rongo, Tongaiti, Tane- 
papa-kai, and Tangiia (Buck 1932+:lU); Tangaroa, Rongo, and Tane were

23^1
widely known elsewrhere in Polynesia; Tongaiti was known throughout 
the Cook Islands, Society Islands and the Tuamotus; and Tangiia was 
confined to Mangaia and Rarotonga. Tu, the god of war in other 
Polynesian islands, has some such function also in Mangaian myth.
Rongo became the national god of all Mangaia, though each 
tribe had its own tribal god. The power of the gods varied directly 
with the temporal power of the group which acknowledged them. Rongo 
and twelve tribal gods were placed in a Mangaian national god house. 
Rongo, unlike in other areas, was a war god here. However, myth 
indicates his former association with fertility and agriculture. He 
was represented by a Triton shell in the god house near the inland 
marae and by two stone images on the shore marae. He seemed to have 
had no incarnation. His Inland and Shore High Priests were drawn from 
his own descendants in the Ngariki tribe, Rongo of Mangaia has been 
wrongly identified by Gill (1892:27) with 'Oro of Tahiti and the 
Leeward Society Islands. There 'Oro (Koro) and Ro'o (Rongo) are dif­
ferent gods (Buck 193^:163).
In Mangaia Tane was a tribal god, he was worshipped primarily 
by the Ngati-Tane (Buck 193^:l6U). Tane functioned as three gods: 
Tane-ngaki-au, Tane-i-t-e-utu, and Tane-kio; each was associated with 
a specific kin group. Tane became further distinguished when he was 
given a separate incarnation which led to the carving of his image as 
a separate form. Tane-kio's priest, Ue, had the marae Maungaroa 
erected to honor the god.
Tongaiti is the name of the tribe as well as of the third son 
of Vatea. The Tongaiti tribe had a god, named Turanga or Matarau 
whose incarnation was a white and black spotted lizard; this is also

=== PAGE 252 ===

the visible form, of the god Tongaiti, and as such it reflects an 
association of god and tribe (Buck 193l+:l65). Turanga, or Matarau, 
was not known as an ancestor; he appeared ready-made with the 
Tongaiti tribe. The tribe's official marae was Au-moana. Turanga 
was the tribal god of the Tongaiti; later when two subtribes branched 
off from the main tribe, they set up their own gods. There was an 
early clash between the Ngariki and Tongaiti tribes; similar trouble 
arose between their gods, Rongo and Matarau (Buck 193^:165).
Buck believed that Tangiia was taken over by the Mangaians 
from a deified Rarotongan ancestor and later added to the list of 
sons of Vatea. Tangiia became the tribal god of the Tangiia or 
Kanae tribe. This god was worshipped on the marae called Rangi- 
taua; he was represented by a wooden image in'the national god house, 
but he had no incarnation.
The gods represented by wooden images in the national god 
house were worshipped by a considerable number of people. The other 
gods represented there, Motoro, Te-aio, Teipe, and Te-kuraaki were 
tribal gods; Utakea and Kereteki can not be identified as belonging 
to any group. Motoro, the tribal god of the Kgariki, superseded all 
other gods except Rongo, to whom he stood next at the god house. His 
followers could not be sacrifices for Rongo. His main marae was 
Araata; he was represented by sennit work and the orongo plant, and 
he incarnated as the m o ’o (blackbird). Te-kuraaki is another deity 
of Rarotongan origin; his carved image was preserved in the national 
god house even after the tribe that had adopted him, the Tui-kura, 
became extinct (Buck 193*+:l66). Kereteki and Utakea were also of

2361
Rarotongan origin; Utakea was probably worshipped by the Tui-kura 
tribe.
Three supernatural beings were believed to bring success in 
fishing: Rua-tamaine, a female goddess; Ruaatu, represented by a rock
to which offerings were made; and Mokoiro, one of the first three 
settlers of Mangaia, who appears to have been deified. Ruanuku, Tu, 
and Tumu, widely known Polynesian gods, were mentioned in Mangaian 
myth but were not actively worshipped (Buck 193^:167-168).
Recorded material representations of gods included Rongo's 
Triton shell trumpet, sennit work, finely lashed adzes, and one coco­
nut leaf charm. Rongo was the only god carved in stone. The princi­
pal gods were carved of wood in crude human form; Motoro's image was 
decorated with red feathers. All god images were wrapped with spe­
cial white bark cloth, tikoru mataiapo (cloth of the first-born), 
manufactured by the priests of Turanga (Buck 193^:170). Priests kept 
extra images in their houses. Incarnations of gods were primarily 
established by legend; some of these were observed for omens.
Northern Cook Islands (Manihiki and Rakahanga, Tongareva)
Little recorded information is available concerning the four 
major Polynesian gods, Tane, Rongo, Tu, and Tangaroa in the Northern 
Cook Islands. Tangaroa was said to be the grandfather of the Maui 
brothers and the possessor of fire. The important gods on Manihiki and 
Rakananga were reportedly stolen from the island of Utone (Buck 
1932b:205). These deities were associated with specific marae. An 
image of breadfruit wood represented Te Puarenga; this image was orna­
mented with sennit braid lashed in a pattern, inlaid with white

shell, and wrapped in a mat. It was burned at the time of the con­

Austral Islands (Tubuai, Ra'ivavae)
Aitken was unable to recover any information about pre­
missionary Tubuaian religion other than the presence of a supreme 
god, Taio Aia; the conversion to Christianity had been complete (1930: 
115)• It is not known what happened to the images of the ancient 
gods. Some stone images were buried, wood ones were apparently 
burned or otherwise destroyed.
Hawaii
The Hawaiians, who followed the general pattern of reli­
gion which their ancestors brought from central Polynesia, 
had many gods who rendered help in the needs and activities 
of this life. The gods, though regarded as invisible spirits, 
were symbolized by material objects, some of natural wood or 
stone and others made in the image of man, in part or in 
whole. 
Supernatural help was obtained by reciting an appro­
priate formula and accompanying it with some offering laid 
before the symbol or altar of the god. Chiefs, warriors, 
craftsmen, and commoners had their own particular deities.
The commoners conducted their simple ritual without any 
special setting, but craftsmen made simple shrines upon 
which to deposit their offerings. The chiefs had their 
services conducted at open-air temples by an organized 
priesthood, which elaborated a more complicated ritual with 
a higher grade of offerings. The commoners and craftsmen 
sought alcHfor thexrThd3?vTdual needs, but chiefly leaders 
sought divine assistance on matters of public importance 
which concerned the welfare of their people. Public ser­
vices at the temples sometimes lasted several days, until 
chief and priest were satisfied that the ritual had been 
conducted without error and so had procured the 'aha, or 
perfection which commanded success (Buck 196k:k6^Y.
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 255 ===

r
2381
The gods invoked by chiefs and commoners alike included the 
four major pantheon gods, Ku (Tu), Kane (Tane), Lono (Rongo), and 
Kanaloa (Tangaroa). Craftsmen normally worshipped some special form 
of Ku.
Gods who were supposed to preside over one place or another 
were countless (Malo 1951:83). Many gods were created by deifying 
ancestors. Spirits (’uhane) were frequently called from the other 
world through persistent prayers and offerings and were established 
then as gods, unihipili. The minor individual or family gods'were 
called 'aumakua (Buck 1964:466). Chiefs were often deified immedi­
ately after death.
Some gods were represented by uncarved pieces of wood or 
stone. Most of these were symbols of the fishermen's god, Kuula.
Symbols of gods were commonly worked into human form. These were 
called ki'i and when they represented gods, akua ki'i. 
Smaller 
wooden images represent the 'aumakua worshipped by individuals and 
craftsmen.
New Zealand
The Maori of New Zealand distinguished four grades of atua, 
supernatural beings or gods; the first class consisted only of Io, a 
Supreme Being; the second class, departmental gods, were originating 
beings or tutelar beings; the third class consisted of a number of 
district gods; and finally, the fourth class included inferior beings, 
deified ancestors, and demons (Best 1952:68-69)• Io, the supreme 
god, was reportedly known only by a select group of priests. The 
most important gods of the second class were Tane, Tu, Rongo,
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 256 ===

r
239I
Tangaroa, Whiro, and Tawhiri-matea, who were all offspring of the 
primal parents Sky Father and Earth Mother. Tane was recognized as 
a god of the sun, Rongo of agriculture and the moon, Tangaroa of the 
ocean, Whiro of evil, darkness, and death, and Tawhirimatea of the 
wind (Best 1952:71)• Many gods of the third class, like those of the 
second,were personifications of natural phenomena. Best noted that 
the term atua was used in a wide sense and that it included all super­
natural beings, spirits or manifestations, from the majestic Supreme 
Being down to low-class malevolent demons. The term was also used 
for familiar spirits, lares, and even for inanimate objects viewed as 
tipua, a word denoting something uncanny or strange or a demon (Best
Zealand), is characteristic of East Polynesian supernatural beings.
In New Zealand, as Best describes, the general term for spirit and 
deity was atua. Categories or classes of gods were normally distin­
guished; the highest of these was one of mythical or creation gods; 
usually, most of these, however, were not actually worshipped or 
represented in material objects. A second level or class consists of 
what Handy referred to as departmental gods for the Marquesas and 
included nature gods, gods of the elements, and occupational deities. 
The pantheon gods, described earlier, may be placed in this second 
category, although some of them often play a role in the creation of 
man, and therefore might be called creation gods. A third class of
1952:57).
Summary
A great range in status, from a supreme god (io of New 
Zealand) down to a low-class malignant spirit or demon (tipua of New
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 257 ===

r
gods may be referred to as tutelary deities, these were recently 
deified ancestors. All of the gods were ultimately ancestral.
Deities of these classes were worshipped or invoked at sacred places, 
ranging in complexity from a single upright stone to large marae; the 
size and nature of the worshipping place corresponded to the god's 
importance and the size of his followers. Different gods of the 
East Polynesian major pantheon, Tu, Tane, Rongo, and Tangaroa, were 
invoked on each island; Tu was probably the one most widely invoked. 
All four were appealed to in Hawaii, but in many other islands some 
had been replaced by more recent ancestors. 
These pantheon gods as 
well as many of the other departmental gods had specific incarnations 
which were sometimes observed for omens. As illustrated in the 
Tuamotuan and Mangarevan material, all the gods, from the highest 
down to recently deified family ancestors, were named at marae cere­
monies. Repetition of invocations is characteristic. Representations 
of family gods range from unworked stones to the large carved stone 
and wood images of the Marquesas, Ra'ivavae, or Tahiti. There is 
some indication that stone was reserved for the images of particular 
gods and wood for others. Table I shows the named gods invoked at 
East Polynesian marae.
The following section presents data on the classification of 
religious structures, with emphasis on marae. A discussion of the 
priests who used these structures and the ceremonies they conducted 
will follow.

Religious Structures and Ethnographic Definition 
Island Groups 
Marquesas
On Hivaoa the taha tupapa'u (taha, place; tupapa'u, corpse) 
were family shrines (Handy 1923:115). This was a private family 
burial place and, although Handy thought of it as a private me'ae, it 
was never referred to as a me'ae, because this term was reserved for 
platforms in which the priest or chief was buried. This latter struc­
ture was the site of tribal ceremonials.
Though the taha tupapa'u was not used on Uapou or Nukuhiva, 
there was a special house in which bodies were treated. In other 
words, the only difference in the practice was that on Uapou the 
house was not placed on a specially erected platform unless the body 
was that of a chief or priest. In this case the house was on the 
platform of the me'ae (Handy 1923:116). A four-posted wall-less 
shed, fa'e vaka (fa'e, house; vaka, cance) or fa'e ha'a pa'a (ha'a 
pa'a, to dry), protected the bier on which the body was exposed. The 
dimensions of the taha tupapa'u averaged between six and eight feet 
in height, ten and fifteen feet in length, and six and ten feet in 
width.
According to Handy (1923:115)> the me'ae (marae) was one of 
the major types of religious structures used by Marquesans in his­
toric times; me'ae were originally burial places, though they also 
functioned as public ceremonial sites. The two functions of the 
tomb-temples were combined because ceremonials performed at the me'ae 
had to do with the bodies of priests or chiefs, with sacrifice to
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 260 ===

tribal deities, or with some phase of the ancestral cult. Other 
sacred places, such as family shrines, places for depositing hair,
and the tokai, were of lesser importance.
On the islands of Uahuka and Uapou, according to Handy (1923: 
115), the term ahu was used instead of me'ae, but Linton (1925:31)
says that "ahu" was used for me'ae structures on Nukuhiva and on
Uahuka. Both authors use me'ae "to avoid confusion," but the termi­
nology is still unclear. Other synonyms were: a tapu (a, place;
tapu, sacred); vahi tapu (vahi, place); and taha tapu (taha, place). 
Handy (1923:115-116) refers to a manuscript by a missionary, Pere 
Jean, which distinguished two types of me'ae: l) ahu fenua, tribal
shrines at which food offerings were presented but no human flesh was 
eaten; and 2) ahu ikoa enana (ikoa, name; enana, man), burial places 
of chiefs and priests of particular families. At this place human 
sacrifices were made and human victims were devoured. The latter 
type of ahu (me'ae) were accessible only to priests, chiefs and war­
riors, but the other tribal ahu were more accessible. Handy said:
"the term used by Pere Jean for the type of ahu used for burial, ahu 
ikoa enana, suggests that such me'ae were named after the individuals 
in whose honor they were built" (1923:118). Linton referred to the 
two types above as "public" and "mortuary" me’ae (1925:33).
Me'ae which were used primarily for burial were in secluded 
spots; those for which burial was a secondary function, and which were 
used primarily for the performance of ritual at ceremonies, were 
attached to and part of the feast place, tohua. The latter type of 
me'ae (ahu fenua) were not built primarily for burial as were those 
in secluded spots (Handy 1923:117).
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 261 ===

Me'ae were usually owned "by the chief as head of the trihe.
A chief or trihe might have one or several me'ae. Private families 
had their own sacred places. The me'ae of the chief, like all of his 
other possessions, was inherited by his first-born (Handy 1923:118).
Platforms were placed on the me'ae according to its use and 
topography, and were often built like sleeping houses with red tuff 
curbs. Others were ornamented with blocks carved as human heads, 
small conventionalized representations of human figures in relief, 
and geometric bas-relief on red-stone slabs. 
The most important of 
the platforms was that which held the images. Normally, this plat­
form was built in a dominant position at the rear of the me'ae. On 
other me'ae platforms were houses for various purposes.
Pits or vaults were dug beneath the me'ae for the burials of 
ceremonial priests (tau'a). Pits lined with cut red stone slabs were 
made in the temple platforms for holding skeletons or skulls, round 
ma (fermented breadfruit) pits were to provide food for the priests 
and their assistants. Other pits served for the disposal of sacred 
objects. 
Sacred trees or sacred groves were universally associated 
with me'ae. Me'ae were always tapu, particularly during ritual.
With the completion of a rite, all temporary structures were 
destroyed, including the shrines and houses, but not the images.
The inspirational priest stayed in the precincts only during cere­
monies (Handy 1923:120).
Each tribe possessed at least one of the tohua which were 
essentially secular and open to all classes of the population. Ter­
races or stadia along the sides were for use by particular classes of 
people. Valleys containing several tribes often had one or more
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 262 ===

r
major tohua, each associated with a separate chief; however, at least 
on some occasions all the tribes of the valley could use a particular 
tohua. Tohua were built and named in honor of young living chiefs and 
for the deification rites of dead chiefs and priests; they were named 
for the person honored (Handy 1923:205).
A special area was marked off on the tohua for old men who 
chanted; also, there were stone backrests (keho) for these chanters.
A square place, a tuu, surrounded by red tufa stone was the location 
of sacrificial rites. At one end or side of the tuu was a place of 
sacrifice (me'ae) where the house (fa'e takau, fa'e tau1 a) of the 
high priest (tau'a) stood and where the priests and their assistants 
performed the rites. The chief's house always opened onto or was near 
the feast place; the warriors' tapu house was similarly located.
These dance areas were the property of the tribal chief (Handy 1923: 
205):
The construction of a new feast place was itself, appar­
ently, always a part of some great festival, such as, for 
example, a fete for the purpose of honoring the son of a chief 
or a great memorial celebration for the dead. In other words 
when a new feast place was made as a part of such a celebra­
tion, the building of it was a part of the rite, the feast 
place and the new temple constituting, so to speak, a votive 
offering.
The chief would invite all his relatives and allies to bring stones 
to aid in constructing the feast place and to participate in the 
feasting. The work was tapu. The great stones used in the platforms 
were brought by groups of men or one very large stone might be 
brought by a whole tribe working together. A tribe, or people, liv­
ing by a quarry would contribute the cut stone slabs. Old feast 
places were sometimes made over or demolished and the material used
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 263 ===

for a new one. The first rite performed on the new altar was human 
sacrifice consecrating it.
Society Islands
The importance of the stone constructions called marae in the 
Society Islands is indicated by a special chant (Henry 1928:150):
Chant on Marae
0 te marae nei te mo'a e te hana- 
hana o te fenua; 'o te teoteora'a ia o 
te ta'ata no teie mau fenua. O' to te 
fenua ia unauna te marae; a aora'i ia
1 pupu hia na te atua.
Marae were the sanctity and glory 
of the land; they were the pride of 
the people of these islands. The orna­
ments of the land were the marae, 
they were the palaces presented to the 
gods.
Henry distinguished three classes of public marae and five 
classes of domestic marae (1928:119): the international, the national
and the local; the family or ancestral marae, the social, the doctors, 
the canoe builder's, and the fishermen's marae, respectively.
The major Ra'iatean marae, Taputapu-atea, became an interna­
tional marae when, according to tradition, chiefs and priests from 
Rotuma, Rarotonga, other Society Islands, and Hew Zealand met there 
with great ceremony (Henry 1928:119-127). Disputes soon led to dis­
solution of the widespread unification, but Ra'iatea and Tahiti con­
tinued to participate in joint ceremony into historic times. 
Even­
tually a marae called Taputapu-atea was established on Moorea and 
even on Faka-rava in the Tuamotus (Henry 1928:107).
The national marae were built during a two or three year long 
period of restrictions; for several months the laborers lived in a
L

camp, puhapara'a, and collected and worked on stones. Those not 
directly participating in the labor were kept away. All the work was 
done under the direction of the tahu'a marae. Marae dedicated to the 
god ’Oro were distinguished from all others by stones which were 
shaped like turtle heads and set at regular intervals along the sides 
of the construction (Henry 1928:132).
grand marae for the 'ofa'i-faoa (chief corner stone), a man was slain 
and placed in the hole dug to receive the stone— his spirit suppos­
edly remained to guard the marae— and then the erect stone was
planted firmly on top of the corpse while the priest prayed to the 
tutelar god. A corresponding stone, ti1a-va (stand-towering), was 
placed at the opposite corner of the marae. These two stones were 
referred to as the horns of the marae (tihi-marae). The platform, 
called ahu, which formed the marae altar had either checkered steps 
with various kinds of stones closely set against each other in pyra­
midal form or had great perpendicular slabs of stratified rock with
steps only in the front. The solid mass fill of the ahu was called
puo (heart); the first step of a pyramidal marae was called niu 
(foundation). The end of the marae with the two tihi was called the 
ahu-mua, and was more elevated and sacred than the other end, which 
was called the opani (closure). The paved court was referred to as 
paepae. Towards the front of the marae there was an enclosure about 
six feet long and four feet wide, the ava'a rahi (great enclosure); 
the image of the tutelar god was placed here when it was carried into 
the marae in its little ark. The enclosure was also called the most 
holy part, vahi-mo'a-roa. Drums were placed near the center of the
In building a marae, a long stone was taken from some other

marae for religious ceremonies. Next to the drums was a row of low 
stone pillars, the vaira’a-tapa'au (holders of the tapa'au). The 
tapa'au were small figures of men made of braided coconut leaves, 
representing the priests praying before the vahi-mo'a-roa. These 
pillars and figures combined were the aho (life) of the marae (Henry 
1928:13H).
Carved planks, unu-marae, stood in the pu marae, a place 
behind the aho extending from the right and left of the front steps 
and from the vahi mo'a-roa to the center of the marae. These planks 
were carved and painted; they served as holders for the property of 
the gods. In the midst of the unu stood two stones, the pu-maro-ura, 
each about four feet high, one representing a king and the other a 
queen. Next to the unu stood two stone slabs, also about four feet 
high, 'ofa'i-tuturira1 a (kneeling stones), against which the king 
and the chief next in rank kneeled, crouched, or leaned during 
prayer. Behind the chief's kneeling stones there were other similar 
stones for the officiating priests, tahu1a pure. The "congregation," 
opure, prayed on the paving of the courtyard, patu-ha' amorira1 a 
(paving for worship), at the closure of the marae (Henry 1928:1314-).
"he long side of the marae was left bare to make it more imposing. 
Secret vaults were excavated in various places in the ahu and the 
court for the temporary deposition of bodies.
The slender, two foot tall stones placed near the inner side 
of the marae served as representations of dead leaders. The first of 
these stones, 'ofa1i-manava-ari1i, were placed parallel to the marae 
and the later ones were set in an outward direction, gradually forming 
a small square which indicated the antiquity of the marae.

=== PAGE 266 ===

A number of altars were to be found on the marae, including 
large flat stones for human sacrifice, and large platforms 6-12 feet 
long and 1+-5 feet wide, called fata-rau, upon which whole hogs and 
fishes, vegetables and coconuts were placed for the gods. The fata- 
'ai'ai were altars for "small eating."
A good-sized house, the fare ia-manaha (house of sacred treas­
ures), located in front of the marae was where the-keepers of the 
marae dwelt, and where they kept the images of the gods, the sacred 
apparel, the drums, and other paraphernalia. A human sacrifice was 
buried beneath the middle house posts of this structure during its 
erection (Henry 1928:136). On the inland side of the marae stood a 
long, low shed called fare-va'a-1e-atua, in which the va'a-ra'a 
(sacred canoe) were kept for the tutelar god to travel in and the 
va'a-hoehoe (canoe for common paddling) in which the marae keepers 
went on sacred errands. The fare-atua was a small container or ark 
made of sacred polished wood about four feet long, two and one half 
feet wide, and three feet high; it had carrying poles so that two 
men could carry the god in it. The ark was stored in the fare-ia- 
manaha.
The third kind of public marae, the local marae, the marae 
mataeina1 a, was built in the same style as the national marae, only 
smaller. The titles and genealogies of the chieftain families or the 
nobility of the land were attached to this marae. There the people 
of the ramages, va'a mataeina’a (canoes of the district), assembled 
for annual celebrations and for local religious rites, over which a 
high priest also presided, accompanied by his staff but without human 
sacrifices.

25FI
Domestic marae were always smaller than the public ones. The 
god of the family or ancestral marae, the marae tupuna, was always a 
family secret. Henry says this marae could be erected upon any por­
tion of land a person owned (l928:l4l) . The hereditary names of the 
family, without which they could give no proof of their ownership of 
the land, were attached to the marae. When land was divided, people 
took a suitable stone from the original marae for the chief corner­
stone, 'ofa'i faoa, of the new marae to be erected upon their respec­
tive shares. The new marae was built of non-consecrated stones and 
was dedicated to the same tutelar deity as the old one. It was inau­
gurated with a feast, the faato'moraa (entering) (Henry 1928:142).
The simplest family marae was a pavement walled in with stone slabs 
three or four feet high; each slab, the 'ofa1i-tutu-rira'a (leaning 
stone), to the left and right represented a member of the household 
A rubbish pit, tiri-a-pera, was located at the rear of the marae. 
Sacred family fetishes such as images, ancestral skulls, jawbones, 
and feather amulets were hidden in slab cavities or cists in the 
front part of the marae.
The marae-o-te-va'a-mataeina'a was the social marae used by 
the members of a community as a unit (Henry 1928:144). It was simi­
lar to the family marae but was dedicated to a god of the first order.
Three kinds of occupational marae were built. One of these 
was marae-tahu1 a-ra1 au, built by the tahu'a ra'au, or doctor. It was 
small and had only one leaning stone where only he prayed (Henry 
1928:145). One-night long ceremonies were conducted by canoe makers

r
on some marae. Fishermen groups built marae called marae-o-te-feia- 
tai'a in which they placed stone or wooden images of fish gods and 
other sacred relics with bird's feathers. The main gods to which 
these marae were dedicated were Rua-hatu-tini-rau, Rua-hatu-o-te 
tai-euea, and Tino-rau.
Research by Kenneth Emory (1933, 1939, 19^7) offers one of 
the most detailed descriptions of marae and their ceremonies. Marae 
occupied a very important position in Tuamotuan culture; to know the 
name of a person's marae was as important as knowing the name of the 
island he came from. Each district, a division of tribal lands, was 
recognized as the property of a kindred, gati, within the tribe; both 
their living representatives and their land were termed matakeinaga 
(Emory 19^7:7). Each matakeinaga had one major marae. In the case 
of Tatakoto Island, the marae Vaiehu, of the matakeinaga Marere- 
vahine, served as the feast area for the entire tribe, since the 
kindred which occupied this land was the senior one on the whole 
island and its head was chief of the tribe. The high chief of kin­
dred Marere-vahine would not only be the head of the tribal marae, 
but also of his district marae; the private family marae, One- 
karamea was within this district at a place called Ahumea where the 
founder of the tribe once lived (Emory 19^7:8).
of kin could erect a marae on land it occupied. A family might have 
several maraes located on different parts of its land for convenience,

and it might build a new one on the occasion of the installation of a 
chief.
Montiton said (187^:502):
In their savage state, our Paumotuans lived scattered 
along the shore of their islands, grouped, however, by fami­
lies. The chief or the most influential member established 
or consecrated his authority by the construction of a marae, 
a fact which at the same time instituted him as the sole 
priest.
People attending a ceremony in honor of the installation of the chief, 
Varoa Tikaroa of Takume Island, are reported to have brought with 
them six slabs to be set up at the establishing of his marae (Emory 
19^7:8). The owner of one marae was supposed to have fashioned the 
first upright pillar on the right end of the ahu (as viewed from the 
court) and his father set up the one at the opposite end. These two 
pillars were called Te Pofatu-nui and Tataki, respectively. The center 
one of the three uprights was called Mata-uru (Emory 19^7".9) •
An individual apparently erected his own marae when he became 
disgruntled at the owners of the marae he previously belonged to. If 
the difficulties were resolved, he could return to the old marae and 
abandon the one he had constructed.
The tribal, district, and local marae apparently differed in 
function. Certain marae were specifically for turtle feasts on 
Napuka, marae tifai, and most of the ones Emory was able to gather 
detailed information about were of this type. He also records the 
marae humi, or marae for the seal.
Emory concluded (l9*+7:10):
So we see specialization in the purpose of maraes both 
as regards the size of the kin group they served and as regards 
the type of activities upon them: 
some serving for feasts of
one kind of food, some for another; some serving for the
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 270 ===

r
253!
disposition of the skulls of enemies; some, for a single 
important event.
The maraes, as the property of kindred, were material 
symbols of them, and formed, a visible connection with the 
past. Always standing on the land occupied by the kindred, 
observable by any who might pass, they came to be a seal of 
ownership. They bound the ancestral spirits and gods of 
the kindred to the land, putting it under their eternal 
guardianship.
The courts, tahua, of Tuamotuan marae were enclosed only on 
the island of Reao. Most courts lie at right angles or parallel to 
the lagoon. The front of the marae, mua, was where the ahu stood. On 
Napuka maraes with double courts were used; they had a row of sand 
marking the division and each side, of course, had its main platform, 
or ahu.
The main platform was the most sacred part of the marae; it 
was generally referred to as ahu, but on Napuka it was called raga- 
nuku, and at Vahitahi, niu. The ahu uprights along the rear edge were 
called keho or pofatu, like those of the court; these terms were 
restricted to marae uprights. As elsewhere in Polynesia, these 
uprights served as backrests for the chief and other dignitaries and 
marked their positions on the court. The slabs along the back of the 
ahu marked the positions of the ancestral gods who attended the cere­
mony. Keho vaguely shaped like a human were referred to as tiki.
Emory believed that the upright slabs did not represent gods because 
they did not receive the names of gods or ancestors.
On Nukutavake the uprights along the back of an ahu were called 
from right to left, the pou mua, pou roto and pou muri, and were named 
respectively, Te Pofatu-nui, Mata-uru, and Tutuaki. The chant given 
by Te Poa for the pou mua begins: 
"Ka te e pofatu nui no te ariki
(erect a great stone for the chief)." Although a Nukutavake informant

Small platforms on the marae court, some directly in front of

recovery was addressed to a certain keho (upright); there were

The seat of the chief on the court, midway between the sides

court uprights. Except at Reao, this seat was a slab back-rest which 
was placed at the rear of a small platform. Other uprights on the

marae courts are the rough, coral platform, ruahatu, apparently asso­

keiga, located at one side or at the rear of the marae court.
One of the most important adjuncts to the marae and its 
ceremony was the miniature god house, fare tini atua, which held 
locks of hair, teeth, fingernails that would be taken from the dead 
before burial and sacred objects employed in rites held at the marae. 
Some may have contained bones. These small wooden houses were about 
2 to 3 feet long and 8 inches high. Ten or so fare tini atua were 
placed on a frame over the ahu of the marae. 
The wooden poles of 
the frame rested on upright stone slabs; the entire thing was 
referred to as a raga-nuku (Emory 19^7:28).
There is little evidence of figures in the round represent­

30). Tuamotuans may have had sennit images similar to the to'o of 
Tahiti. Symbols of deified ancestors are described as a piece of 
wood or a thigh bone with inserted tufts of human hair. 
Turtle 
effigies were also important.

or wood apparently were kept in the fare tini atua and were taken out 
by a priest when consecrating offerings on the marae.
An important house constructed near the marae was the fare 
heiao, or fare heiau, which in most places, except on Napuka, was 
used for the storage of fare tini atua when they were not on the 
altar. There is some indication that people gathered at the fare 
heiau and that it was something more than a storage house (Emory 
19^7:b2).
Mangareva
Mangarevans distinguished between permanent and temporary 
marae, as well as a third class, the family marae.
The permanent marae made of basaltic and other stone were
termed marae po1atu (po'atu, stone) and were associated with higher
gods and superior priesthood. They were said to have been built
(a'ua'u) originally by the voyager Tupa:
He sailed right down to Mangareva and lay afloat in the 
Great-pass-of-Tupa. He went ashore at the islet of Te Kava.
There he made a marae to the god, Tu, which was named Te 
Kava-maru (Buck 1938:^53)•
Buck recorded ten marae accredited to Tupa. Some of these were asso­
ciated with certain districts.
The most famous of the permanent marae was Te Kehika, where 
the whole population gathered for the service to Tu in order to pro­
mote productivity of breadfruit trees. The worship of Te Agiagi, the 
war god, was also held there, but when at war with Rikitea, the other 
districts conducted ritual on their own marae. Though a permanent 
marae, Te Hau-o-te-vehi, had a lower status than Te Kehika, and was 
used by priests of the lower order in rituals connected with the
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 274 ===

r~  
257I
lesser gods. The god Tukimana was invoked here to give power to 
abortion gods and to cause the eyes of initiates to protrude (Buck 
1938:1+5I+).
Mangarevan marae appear to have had raised platforms. A 
small house was erected on the marae by wood craftsmen for the recep­
tion of Tu during certain ceremonies, but, since the building of the 
house formed part of the ritual and was rebuilt at each ceremony, it 
would appear that there was no permanent god house on the marae.
The table ('ata) for food offerings had specially constructed 
wooden legs (pua) to support a flat slab of stone. There is no evi­
dence that upright stones were used as rests on the Mangarevan marae 
beyond the statement by Tregear (1899:38) that a korire was a stone 
on which the god rested.
Temporary marae were constructed with lumps of coral or 
pieces of branched coral to form a raised platform. The platform was 
used for one service and was named after its specific function (Buck 
1938:^55)• Emory calls these marae viriga (1939:8). The platform 
used in the ceremony of creating an abortion god \iias termed a marae 
mota'u; it required a flat surface sufficiently large for the erection 
of a house for the abortion god. These temporaiy marae had no paved 
courts. The right to make such marae carried a certain amount of 
prestige.
The permanent and temporary maraes were used for an elabo­
rate ritual on special occasions, but the family marae which con­
sisted of a house, pavement, image, and offering stands, were used 
by individual families. Raised stone platforms, a feature of the 
temporary and permanent maraes, were absent on the family marae.

r -  
2 5 F I
Buck (1938:^56) felt that wooden images representing Tu, like those 
described by Moerenhout (l837> 1:97) and Beechey (1831, 1:167), were 
part of family marae ritual. Beechey and Moerenhout report a closed 
house with a pavement in front of it. In family observances food was 
placed on the tables or hung on the special stands before the image 
of Tu. The family marae had a special stand for food offerings.
When the breadfruit crop matured, the first fruits were shared by the 
priests with the gods in the touna1a ritual. A number of other offer­
ings, paddles, mats, coils of line, cloth, carved coconut shells, and 
so forth, were presented to gods. Standardized offerings were placed 
on the altar in marae ritual. These included the uo, a piece of coral 
wrapped in cloth and bound with fern leaves, and the eketea, a 
straight or forked stick ornamented with pieces of cloth and maro- 
kura streamers (Buck 1938:1+58).
Cook Islands
The Mangaian national god house, Te-kaiara, a poorly made 
house of reeds, was located between two marae and contained wooden 
images of the principal gods; according to Buck (l93*+:172), they 
remained there even during marae rituals. The marae houses, 'are 
ei 'au or houses for peace, were in miniature; they were about six 
feet long with a small doorway screened with white cloth. These 
were built on each marae after peace was declared. No images were 
placed in these houses, but the god took up spiritual residence in 
it as an alternative home to the national god house (Buck 193^:173).
The house on each marae was for the tribal god who was worshipped 
at that marae.
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 276 ===

Each of the tribal priests had a portion of his house divided 
off with a cloth curtain to form a tapu sanctuary (]Da tikoru) where a 
carved image of a god was kept. The priest used this for ordinary 
purposes; the marae was reserved for public services.
The marae was not only necessary to worship a god, but it 
associated the ancestor or tribe with a particular district, and 
. ablished their right to the land (Buck 193^:17^-) • If a tribe was 
conquered, its marae became a historical landmark and could not alone 
reserve the land for the tribe. Though priests carried out the cere­
monial, the chief who built the marae owned it and his position as 
the source (pu) was recognized.
There is a list of gods who were associated with particular 
marae for Mangaia. Of forty recorded marae, 25 were owned by specific 
tribes or individuals. Buck was able to locate 27 of the forty 
structures (193^:17^-175)* Nothing definite is recorded of the 
arrangements within these marae courts. There was no stone altar, 
but a wooden platform of hala wood ('atarau) was erected on the 
Akaoro marae for the purpose of human sacrifice. At the Orongo 
marae the human sacrifice was laid upon a smooth block of sandstone 
in front of the image of Rongo (Gill 1876:299)*
Northern Cook Islands (Manikiki and Rakahanga, Tongareva)
Buck found ahu platforms on four of 2k Tongarevan marae; these 
had upright slab architecture. Boundaries were defined by upright 
stone slabs. 
Storage houses for material representations of gods 
were located on the marae court at the opposite end from the ahu 
(Buck 1932:150). No marae classifications are available.

=== PAGE 277 ===

Austral Islands (Tubuai, Ra'ivavae)
Tubuaian marae were rectangular courts, sometimes paved, and 
bounded on three sides by a fence of upright stones. There was only 
one example of court uprights. Rone had ahu (Aitken 1930:115).
There are no ethnographic details regarding their use or classifica­
tion.
Hawaii
The relationship between the simpler religious needs of indi­
viduals and those of large social groups of Hawaiians is well indi­
cated in Buck's statement (196^:513):
For every-day communication with his gods, the Hawaiian 
could conduct his simple ritual with his ’aumakua at some 
shrine or wherever it suited. The temple was an expansion 
of the shrine to meet the requirements of larger groups of 
people with more elaborate ceremony and ritual. Large tem­
ples were built for public ceremonies connected with some 
major event, such as war or the increase of food supplies.
This is a common relationship throughout Polynesia.
The shrines consisted of a simple altar without a prepared 
court. They were constructed by individuals or small family groups 
for short rituals which required no priests. 
Offerings were placed 
at an altar. An important type was the fishing shrine, or ko'a, at 
which the fisherman placed the first of his catch as an offering to 
the gods who assisted in his fishing. There is considerable confu­
sion regarding household shrines. Buck (l961+:528) says that they 
should be called kuahu, but Malo (1951:105? HO) referred to house­
hold shrines or heiau as located within the mua, or men's eating 
house. Apparently, sometimes household shrines were separate small 
houses (McAllister 1933:17). One other type of family shrine was
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 278 ===

r~ 
26H
the pohaku o kane, stone of Kane, simply a sacred rock. Road shrines 
were upright stones or a small stone platform where travelers might 
place offerings to placate the spirit of the district. Other road 
shrines marked boundaries between land units called ahupua'a.
In Hawaii, the heiau temple structure was said to have been 
introduced into the islands in historic times by a priest named Paao. 
Allegedly this took place sometime after the islands had been occupied, 
and it replaced an earlier, simpler type of temple. The very simple 
Necker and Nihoa type temples (marae) described by Emory (1928) are 
representative of this early type of temple.
An important feature of Hawaiian heiau, also characteristic of 
Marquesan me'ae, is the tremendous diversity in floor plan. To a 
large extent, this variation is due to frequent re-building and re­
designing by temple architects. In addition to having individual 
proper names, temples were also recognized by the Hawaiians as belong­
ing to two major classes, luakini or mapele (Malo 1951:159-162). If 
the king decided to make war, he ordered a heiau waikaua (war temple) 
constructed or re-built. This was termed a luakini, where priests of 
the god of war, Ku (Tu), conducted ceremonies. The mapele heiau was 
designed to invoke Lono's good will for crops and other peaceful needs. 
The priests who officiated at the mapele were of the order of Lono 
(mo'o Lono). Any chief could build a mapele, but only a king could 
order a luakini. Different types of wood and thatching were used for 
the two types.
The court or plaza area of the heiau was the kahua, normally 
rectangular in shape and the basic feature of the structure. The 
plaza ranged in size from less than 50 feet to over 500 feet on the
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 279 ===

r
2621
long side. A raised stone platform, kuahu, at one end of the heiau 
court shows the clearest relations with marae farther south in 
Polynesia. As seen in Chapter II, McAllister classified heiau into 
terraced temples, walled temples, and composite temples.
Main temple components were the oracle tower, the temple 
houses, the temple images, the offering stands, and the refuse pit. 
The oracle tower, lana nu'u mamaoa, was a tall structure, described 
by Cook to be 50 feet tall (178^,2:200). The framework of the tower 
as made with strong timbers, covered with poles ('aho), and not 
thatched. It was erected after the heiau walls were completed and 
before the luakini ritual began (Malo 1951:162). After offerings, 
the tower was covered with white tapa.
Four houses were constructed within the court: the mana, hale
pahu, waiea, and hale umu. The mana was a large prayer house built 
at the rear end of the court. The hale pahu (drum house) was built 
with its door facing the offering stand near the raised platform.
Malo states that the drums were beaten inside this house. Cook says, 
however, that the hale pahu was a small structure built outside of the 
heiau. The waiea, where the king and high priest consulted regarding 
the 'aha, or favorable progress of the various stages of the whole 
ritual, was built at one end of the mana (Buck 196*+:521). The hale 
umu (earth-oven house) was built at the other end of the mana and was 
the house where the fires for the temple were made.

was assigned to special chiefs who provided the skilled craftsmen.

erected in front. In the midst or middle of a line of images, a

the reconstruction of an old temple, the king consulted with the high 
priest as to whether the old images should be used or new ones should 
be made. Illustrations by early European visitors to Hawaii indicate 
little uniformity in the placement of images on the heiau. Wicker­
work objects supported on poles were displayed near religious houses 
and in temples.(Buck 196^:522).
Stone pavements, kipapa, upon which the temple offerings, 
mohai, were placed until the priest was ready to offer them to the 
gods were built in front of the images on the court floor (Malo 1951: 
l62). Wooden stands, lele, upon which offerings were presented to the 
gods were built on the court near the kipapa. Lele appeared in a 
number of different forms and rested on one to four legs.
An important feature of all heiau was a tapu refuse pit, the 
lua-kini or lua-pa'u. Some pits were inside and others outside the 
temple. Carved slabs and posts, normally representing a human head 
with a very high headdress, were sometimes placed in the stone wall 
surrounding the court, sometimes on the court itself, or on the plat­
form.
New Zealand
Maori religion, in contrast to that of most of Polynesia, is 
marked by the absence of large stone or wood images and of sacred 
stone constructions like marae. There were no images at all repre­
senting the supreme god, Io, but small wooden ones were made for Tane, 
Tu, Rongo, Tangaroa, Tawhiri-matea, Haumia, and Maru (Best 1952:76).

261+1
Reportedly these were sometimes used for the atua to enter the image 
or take possession of it. The stone images were very crude ones used 
in connection with crops. 
Some of these appear to have represented 
Rongo. The tuahu or wahi tapu (sacred place) illustrated by Best 
(1952:Fig.22) consists of unshaped upright stones. The term, tuahu, 
indicates to Emory that the New Zealanders knew of marae (1970:85).
Summary
A number of basic marae characteristics were shared by the 
East Polynesian cultures. In all islands, except New Zealand, the 
presence of various classes of sacred architecture, including differ­
ent types of structures referred to as marae is indicated. A feature 
basic to all religious ceremonial centers,beyond the level of simple, 
single upright stones for family or craft worship, is the court or 
plaza. A second common feature is a constructed stone altar where 
offerings were normally placed. A third feature is stone uprights or 
statues, some of which served equivalent functions. Houses are uni­
versally described in conjunction with marae; these were either on 
the court or near it, and consisted of two basic types, priests' 
houses and gods' houses, with a possible third type, the drum house 
found on Hawaiian heiau. When images were carved in a Polynesian 
society, they were placed or used on the marae. The activity of 
marae construction or the erection of similar structures is described 
ethnographically by the term "ahu" or "ahuahu." Marae or ahu were 
constructed under the direction of the head of an extended family, a 
ramage or a tribe, which would be using the structure. The kin group 
heads were assisted by priests to varying degrees. Marae structures
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 282 ===

r
were recognized ethnographically as signs of land ownership and claim 
and served to tie the people to the land as well as to their gods.
All marae were constructed of stone, including the altars for offer­
ings. The main features of marae are summarized in Table 2.
Priests and Ritual
Island Groups 
Marquesas
In the Marquesas all persons who were subject to possession 
by spirits or gods were referred to as tau'a. The one who acted as 
tribal inspirational priest was named tau'a nui, or sometimes as 
atua, and was distinguished from tau'a of lesser importance, the pri­
vate practitioners. The next position in importance for tribal wor­
ship was the ceremonial priest, tuhuna o'ono (tuhuka o'oko, northern 
dialect), who can be considered as the director of ritual and the 
tribal bard. He sang in rites or led the sacred chants and he was 
also the teacher of chants and legends. Tuhuna as a class, including 
those who were masters in different professions, made up the groups 
who chanted during ceremonies.
Each important tribal temple had one inspirational priest, 
several ceremonial priests, and a considerable number of assistants. 
Tribal inspirational priests could be either male or female; their 
position was usually inherited. Most often, though not always, they 
were related to the chief. The fitness of an individual for the posi­
tion was indicated by a miraculous sign of some kind (Handy 1923:223).
Tau'a had three main functions: 
caring for the remains of
the dead, presiding at the tribal ceremonial, and learning and

267I
voicing the will of the tribal god (Handy 1923:22^). The inspiration 
of such a priest by his god took the form of violent possession during 
which the spirit animated him and spoke through his mouth. The sanc­
tity of the tau'a was greater than that of any other tribal member. 
When he was possessed or when he died, the whole tribe was under 
ceremonial tapu which required that no work be done, that 110 one cir­
culate in the valley, and that no fire be lighted. Although there is 
no clear evidence, it seems that at least some tau'a married. The 
costume of these practitioners consisted of some sort of distinctive 
headdress. An ironwood staff called hoto and three fau sticks 
wrapped in cloth were insignia of the position (Handy 1923:227).
benefit of the tribe or, in some cases, private ritual such as was 
held at death. His primary function, however, was as a leader in 
chanting. He was the professional who taught sacred first-borns the 
chants called vavana, pu'e, and tona pou, among others; he directed 
and led the chanting in family feasts and taught legends. The posi­
tion tended to become hereditary because a man would be most likely 
to pass on this information to his son or to a close relative. A 
wealthy person could pay a tuhuna to teach his son the chants.
Women were excluded from the tuhuna because most of the chants were 
tapu to them. Before a person became ceremonial priest for a tribe, 
he had to be consecrated by two ceremonies at a me’ae. Tests by other 
members of the same profession and contests between famous tuhuna 
o'ono of different tribes were important events.
The family life of the tuhuna was like that of everyone else, 
except for when he was consecrating a tribal ceremony. At these
The ceremonial priest presided over ritual conducted for the
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 285 ===

r
2681
times he stayed in the sacred precincts and was restricted from con­
tact with outsiders while teaching or reciting. Although the tuhuna 
was not as sacred as the inspirational priest, the same rites were 
performed at his death. The chanting and sacrifice following the 
death of either of these priests was designed to elevate his spirit 
to such power that it would become a powerful god to aid the tribe 
(Handy 1923:230). The ceremonial symbol of the tuhuna was the same 
as that of the tau'a. Sometimes, the body of the tuhuna, like that 
of the tau'a, was placed in a vault dug beneath the tribal religious 
center.
Moa, u'u, or taputoho were male guardians and assistants to 
the inspirational priest and were consecrated to aid him during cere­
monials. Tauoha, veveahu, and tauahi were servants of the inspira­
tional priest (Handy 123:223). The duties of the moa were to care 
for, kill, handle, and dispose of the remains of sacrifices; to pre­
pare the priest's food and attend to him generally; to take care of 
the sacred area and the sacred objects, such as the chief's skull 
used in various rites; and to prepare the sacred structures for 
rites. Their dress was a long robe of white cloth, covering the 
whole body. No special rites were conducted at their death; there 
was no unusual treatment of their bodies.
Among the temporary buildings constructed on the temple for 
ceremonies were houses of the inspirational priest and the houses for 
his assistants. A type of small sacred shrine or house, the feia'u, 
appears to have been erected in or near the house of the tau'a.
There were other shrines both inside and outside the sacred houses. 
Restricted areas were indicated by peeled hibiscus stakes wrapped in
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 286 ===

tapa and plaited coconut leaves. The other objects in the sacred 
precincts were images, usually of wood and conch shells, and the 
large pearl shell hooks on which sacrifices were suspended in the 
trees.
The most sacred of the temporary structures was the inspira­
tional priest's house, fa'e tukau or tu'a, built on a platform at the 
secluded tribal temple, or on the sacrifice platform of the taha 
ko'ina. This house was like an ordinary house in shape and ground 
plan. 
One person was sacrificed and eaten at the time of its erec­
tion. No one lived in the fa'e tukau, but it was regarded as the 
house of the inspirational priest. It is not known how he used it in 
his oracular activities at the me'ae (Handy 1923:232). One fa'e 
tukau that Handy describes for Hivaoa was about 60 feet tall and 18 
feet square at the base. No images were placed inside it.
On Hivaoa the ananu'u, a type of shrine built within the fa'e 
tukau, consisted of an upright stake or stakes with a crosspiece, 
four plaited coconut leaves were bound against these stakes. These 
were made as part of both public and private rites. In addition, the 
plaited coconut leaves, fau stakes and conch shells, and coral were 
commonly used in ceremonies and are found on the platforms. Skulls 
were among the most important ceremonial items associated with places 
of worship. The skulls of priests or chiefs were tokens of their 
spirit.
Images, which represent deified tribal ancestors, usually 
stood at the rear of the sacred area on the highest platform. Minia­
ture figures were reportedly used as votive offerings in ceremonies, 
but Handy says (1923:238): 
"By whom images were made, when, and with
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 287 ===

r
270I
what rites is unknown." Some images stood inside of houses and 
others stood outside.
Human sacrifices were offered at some war ceremonials, as a 
means of ending droughts and for securing abundant food supplies.
They were also required at the time of building a new tomb or feast 
place; at important events in the life of a chief or priest; and 
sometimes at the consecration of their new canoe, house, or coffin.
A turtle was sometimes substituted.
Bodies for human sacrifice captured by warriors were first 
taken to the tohua, and then to the me'ae for the rite of fa'i fa'i 
heana. The victims were laid face down in a row on the ground of the 
me'ae. The tau'a lay down upon them and the ceremonial priest per­
formed the rite of fa'i fa'i heana. Drumming and chanting continued 
for seven days. At Pua Ma'u, Hivaoa, Handy was told that at these 
great feasts all the tuhuna assisted in the me'ae, constituting a 
large chorus (Handy 1923:239)* Bodies were suspended in trees after 
the chanting was concluded and the rite ended with signs (tava heana) 
made of plaited coconut leaves replacing the suspended bodies. The 
bodies were then thrown into a pit. Some bodies were eaten at these 
ceremonies. The whole tribe was under ceremonial tapu. All those 
participating took the name Ati Tu, or "family of Tu" (Handy 1923:
2^2). In two other rites, one to assure growth of breadfruit using 
the vavana chant and another, vaititi, to bring rain, victims were 
apparently burned.
Although there is little information regarding the compli­
cated rites which followed death, nine successive feasts in honor of 
the dead were reported: memava hakanoho, tia hahati, vaikaukau,
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 288 ===

vaimata, haoka, menavatu, iki ha'e, tahioa, and kahekaika (Handy 1923: 
lOH). Kava and pigs were consumed in abundance during these feasts, 
accompanied by drumming and chanting of the ceremonial priest. 
Dordillon gives similar feast names (190^:283). For commoners these 
rites were conducted in their own house. The rites for chiefs and 
pri 
ts at me'ae were more elaborate and included human sacrifices.
The actual death of an inspirational or ceremonial priest had to take 
place on the me'ae (Handy 1923:107). The first rite, poou, for such 
high ranking people was performed on the day following the death.
The body was washed and wrapped in a new cloth. On the second day 
the rite kahahakatu followed when a special house was built on the 
me'ae and was decorated with coconut leaves. The rite pouhau involved 
carrying the body of the dead chief into the fa'e vaka and decorating 
this house on the third day. A ceremonial tapu prevailed over the 
entire tribe during these three days; this was followed by 20 to 30 
days of continuous chanting on the me'ae. Usually the body prepara­
tion process took several days, during which the juices from the 
decaying corpse were allowed to drain out so that the body became dry 
and firm.
Offerings placed with the body on Nukuhiva included a pig's 
head and some kava roots. In the islands to the south, a dog was 
hung up in the house and a dead pig was placed under the knees of the 
deceased (Handy 1923:110). Unless the kuhane, spirit, had these 
offerings it would not have been able to reach an underworld. The 
dead individual's possessions and the house built for the preparation 
of his body were all burned at the completion of the work.
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 289 ===

r
2721
Bodies were left on the biers until they fell apart, the
bones were then scraped and bundled up (kokonatua), the skull was 
removed, and a tight bundle was made of the bones by wrapping them 
in cloth. The bundle was either hung from the roof of the house or 
placed in a coffin. Bones were sometimes buried in the sepulchral 
platform or in the platform of a temple. 
Some me'ae had special pits 
lined with cut red stone slabs for depositing bones. The skull was 
sometimes left with the bones in the coffin; more often it was 
detached and hidden. 
Skulls were commonly put on or in the ground of 
the tribal temple or hidden in the sacred banyan tree.
The feasts in honor of the departed spirit of a man were cele­
brated at irregular intervals after his death. Such rites were seldom 
performed in honor of a woman. The oldest male relative determined 
the proper time and made the arrangements. The greatest of these 
feasts were the memorial celebrations conducted long after the actual 
death of chiefs or priests for the purpose of deifying their spirits. 
The elevation of the spirit was done by sacrificing from one to ten 
victims (Handy 1923:107-108).
Three types of important feasts were held in honor of priests:
the ko1ika oke, the ko'ika vaihopu, and the ko'ika u'upua. All three 
contained dancing, mostly naked with sexual -icense, and feasting.
The third also had stilt walking competitions. The term ko'ina 
(ko'ika) referred to feasts which ranged from simple family ones to 
large tribal affairs.

2731
Society Islands
On special occasions Tahitian priests sought signs and omens, 
knowledge from the oracle of the marae in answer to their prayers; 
sometimes they slept on the marae in hope of revelations through 
dreams or directly from the gods. When possessed hy divine powers 
they delivered messages and often became extremely strong and capable 
of unusual things (Henry 1928:153)•
In family marae household heads offered prayers at sunrise 
and sunset; private petitions to the gods could be offered by any 
family member; marriage ceremonies were performed; prayers were made 
for safe delivery of a child; and gods were invoked for the sick and 
dying.
Priests of larger marae chose attendants called 'opu nui.
These assistants could cook and eat their food in the marae pre­
cincts, eat food offered to the gods, and wear the sacred brown tapa 
cloth. Parties of 'opu nui alternated residence in the fare-ia- 
manaha and lived exclusively apart from their families. They guarded 
the sacred images and paraphernalia, weeded the marae, planted 'aute 
(paper mulberry), fed the animals of the gods, and placed food brought 
to the marae on the altars (Henry 1928:152).
Persons skilled in various professions, tahu'a, participated 
in religious ritual. The tahu'a-pure was the "author of prayer," a 
priest. The tahu'a nui or tahu'a rahi, "great author," was a high 
priest. Priests and teachers had schools in which they taught their 
vocations. Priests' schools, for men exclusively, were called
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 291 ===

r 
2751
fare-'ai-ra'a-upu (houses in which to absorb invocations) and were 
always erected on sacred ground (Henry 1928:155).
The order of the priesthood generally descended from father 
to son in their respective districts. The initiate, muhu, was required 
to memorize and chant upu marae (marae prayers), taha tumu (religious 
and political speeches), upu ti'a rau ti (war songs and enchantments 
with the ti leaf), hopu (pleas for peace), upu fa1auru i_ te mehoi o 
te atua (invocation to inspire images or other objects with the gods), 
and many others (Henry 1928:155)• When the initiate had acquired the 
necessary skill, he had to demonstrate his proficiency before the 
assembled priests, the autuhua; after this he was called an utu-pa'a.
The greatest of all marae ceremonies was the pa'i atua, 
assembling and uncovering of the gods, which was performed on the 
national marae on certain occasions, such as the inauguration of a 
chief, his prolonged illness, laying the chief corner stone of a new 
national marae, praying for rain in time of drought, or after great 
natural disasters (Henry 1928:157)• Only tahu'a were permitted to 
observe the pa'i atua. The priests purified themselves through 
extreme isolation as did the high chief and a chosen few people 
called opure (prayers), but to a lesser extent.
The priests stayed apart and chanted during the marae clean­
ing operation. Chanting an opening invocation and uncovering the 
messenger gods, who were sent off to call the gods by chanting 
tu'utu'ura'a (sending off of the messengers), formed the next step of 
the ceremony (Henry 1928:162). The coming of the gods' arrival was 
announced through a long chant; at daylight the priests entered the 
fare-ia-manaha to get the images. The high priest led the procession

gathered at the rear of the marae to form a congregation of worshippers.

invocation, upu fa1atonu (Henry 1928:171). The priests then dis­

If a male strayed upon the scene of a pa'i-atua, he was 
immediately seized and sacrificed to the marae to increase the sacred­
ness of the marae (Henry 1928:169); females were not sacrificed but 
were considered to be crazy if they approached the marae.
An annual national feast, called pararaa matahiti (ripening 
of the year), was held in reaping the first fruits. Contributions of 
food were placed in an immense heap, a poropa, at the districts' 
assembly ground and these were systematically divided into shares 
(Henry 1928:177)•
Another type of chanting ceremony involved casting off a god 
when that god proved to be ineffective. Another god would be chosen 
to replace the former one; the new god would be inaugurated at the 
first pa:i-atua.
Other ceremonies for which there are recorded chants and 
details are those for launching a canoe, particularly regarding the 
rollers; rites for children, particularly those of the royal heir, 
but ancestral marae ceremonies were conducted for all first-born; 
circumcision of the royal heir; and ceremony for the inauguration of 
a king or queen.
Tuamotus
Individuals had a right to sit on any marae owned by a ngati 
to which they could trace blood relationship. One might trace this 
relationship through either his mother or father; however, the
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 294 ===

r
positions he might occupy on maraes would he inferior to the one a 
male could claim through his father, particularly if he were the first­
born son. Normally he would receive the name of one of his father's 
kindred and would belong to his father' s kindred, and one of the marae 
would become his marae when he came of age (Emory 19^7:56). Blood 
relationship gave an individual the privilege of sharing the land of 
the kindred as long as he remained on kindred land. This was an 
important reason why genealogies were preserved.
A man would have the highest position in his own marae, espe­
cially if he had built it himself. The head of the family would have 
the first position in the family marae, entitling him to sit with his 
back against the stone slab on the middle of the court and to receive 
the most desirable bits of distributed food. In the district marae 
he would have to take a seat inferior to that of the district chief.
Seniority in one's branch or seniority within the family 
determined one's position. To the eldest went the title of head.
He was the tuakana (elder on Napuka), he was the upoko (head), and 
if an ariki or of the senior kindred, he was the ariki nui (the great 
chief), or the fakaariki (maker of chiefs) (Emory 19^7:57).
Women and children who had not undergone supercision were 
excluded from marae ceremony and some ceremonies were restricted to 
the elders alone. The head of a family or kindred was entitled to be 
the priest of the marae, but in the case of tribal maraes, some other 
member of the chief's family might hold the office. This high priest 
in Fagatau, for example, was called tu-kau. He had assistants: one
called huhuki, who stood on his right, and two, fakatau and hakari, 
who stood on his left. Emory believes that instead of two assistants

278I
on the chief’s left there was only one, because fa-katu (sacred-
