The hakari described by Montiton brought the sacred stone to the 
chief.
The most frequent occasions for ceremony on marae were when 
turtles, porpoises, or large fish of the sacred class were captured. 
During the off-season for turtle, most of the Napukan marae were 
idle. The first turtles captured at the beginning of the season were 
offered to the gods with the most elaborate ritual.
Other occasions for ritual included cannibal feasts on special 
marae built for that purpose. Ceremonies were also held before going 
on a long voyage to protect against the god Rua. Upon the safe 
arrival at land, the chief went immediately to the marae to acknowl­
edge the protection given by his gods. Famines called for special 
marae ceremonies. Prayers were said at the marae at the first preg­
nancy of a woman of the ariki family. The navel cord of the chief's 
son might also be buried at the marae.
A chief's succession to the position of ariki called for very 
important marae ritual which sometimes included founding a new marae 
for new ariki (Emory 19^7:59)* The dead chief was carried to the 
marae before final deposition of the remains.
The most important of the remembered rituals was the turtle 
feast. Emory recorded the importance of turtles as offerings to 
Tangaroa in the Tuamotus (19^7:61-63). -he man who caught the turtle 
detached the brightest part of the shell and offered it to the god 
whose image was on the bow of his canoe, designating and consecrating 
the victim with a chant (19^-7:59-60). A messenger on shore spread
L

the news of capture with a chant called toinoino. The turtle slung 
on two poles was carried by two to four men to the marae while the 
other fishermen and the whole population danced and chanted around 
them. The turtle was reportedly whipped with a sapling during its 
trip to the marae in order to insure that a large number of turtles 
would be caught during that season. At the rear of the court the 
turtle was placed on his back while the priest tied his flippers and 
neck with consecrated takaikai, entwined strips of pandanus and coco­
nut leaf (Emory 19^-7:6lf).
The chief took up his position on the marae with his back to 
the ahu and faced the tuturi, or assembled crowd. Warriors lined 
both sides of the marae court. The huhuki or spearer gave the chief 
his headdress (fau) and put a staff with a small image on the top 
into his hands. The chief then turned toward the ahu and, with 
"grand contortions and deafening yells" (Montiton I87U:379) invoked 
all the gods (Emory 19^+7:66). A long list of family gods (immediate 
ancestors) followed the important pantheon gods. These invocations 
were repeated several times during the sacrifice.
A long chant collected by Emory in 1931 was for the "awakening 
of the gods" (no te fakarara atua) at the marae (19^7:66). Following 
this awakening, the chief chanted in a low tone and poured a libation 
of coconut water before the "idol" (Montiton 187^:379)* Emory felt 
that Montiton may have been referring to the small image on the 
chief's staff, or more probably, to a representation of the diety in 
stone (19^7:70). After the libation, the hakari reportedly took the 
sacred stone and placed it on the stomach of the turtle, while the 
tuturi chanted the prayer offering the turtle. After the prayer was

delivered, the hakari cut the turtle's throat (Montiton 187^:379) and

small piece of flesh, the haka-ra, from the turtle's side and tied

the gods. Next they prepared the turtle for the first oven. The

the entrails and divided it among his officers. The rest was depos­

marae itself, except by the chief and his officers. The turtle was 
removed from the first oven, divided into small pieces, and placed 
into a second oven for further cooking. The group sang fagu (solemn 
songs) while the turtle cooked. Emory also recorded a number of 
chants for offering food to other gods, such as Tane, Toga, and 
Fakahotu (19*+7:88-93) .

A dead chief's body was carried on a stretcher (tiraga) before

Sometimes a chief or leading warrior, etc., would be 
asked by his descendants to permit his being made into a 
maitu (ancestor-god), or he might himself decide as to the 
deposition of his body, particularly if he were renowned 
as successful in all his undertakings, for in that case he 
would be considered to have great mana, and, as a family- 
or ancestor-god, he would supposedly be particularly able 
to bring success in their undertakings to his descendants 
who should call upon him for help.
If the chief or leading warrior was to be converted 
into a maitu, the body was left on the scaffold support 
(pahata) to dry and desiccate (tufata) in the sun. A fare 
heiau or fare maitu was then built to receive the corpse.

or taura rank.

Mangareva
Priests, taura, formed the medium "between the gods and people. 
Selected candidates were initiated into the service of various gods. 
They were divided into three classes which correspond with the classes 
of gods they represented (Buck 1938:^29).
of priests, those who represented Tu and Te Agiagi. Priests of the 
first class were selected from noble families; the high priest was 
usually a member of the royal family. 
Each tribe had a high priest 
who presided over the priests of the first class. The three classes 
are as follows: l) taura kouae were the first class and were of
noble birth. They conducted the touma ceremony and the marae cere­
monies designed to promote the fertility of breadfruit. These priests 
who officiated on estates called pou'are, received first fruit payments. 
Marae uaikai was the ceremony for inducing fertility. The gods of 
this class were Tu, Rogo, Tahiri, Tu-kite-aga, Te Agiagi, Viriga, 
Tuikura, Tukimana, and Toamiru. 2) The priests of Viriga belonged to 
a lower class, though Viriga is included in the list of gods of the 
priests of the first class. They were concerned with curing disease 
on a special temporary marae made of a heap of coral. The elaborate 
ritual was referred to as marae viriga. A section of this class 
included the taura tao'i tupapaku (priests who handle the dead).
3) Priests called taura umako formed the third or lowest class; they 
dealt with three minor sicknesses as specialties: 
goiter, fishbites,
or fish poisoning. Other experts were catchers of souls (taura 'opu- 
ku'ane) and sorcerers (taura nanati-ka'a); these did not have marae.
High priests, taura tupua, were selected from the first class

28?
Initiations took place on the marae. Candidates built houses 
near the marae as residences during the initiation since they were 
excluded from outside contact.
Some details of Mangarevan religious activity are well 
recorded: the description of the ordination of priests (igogo).
installation of abortion gods and their mediums (marae mota'u), the 
breadfruit ceremony (marae uaikai), treatment of sickness (marae 
viriga), birth, and death.
Though the priests, as direct mediums of the gods, took the 
leading part in the ceremonies, the rogorogo chanters with their drum 
accompaniments were given an important part in ritual. It is evident 
tl .t in the course of time the priests had delegated some of their 
own functions to the chanters; in the marae mota'u ceremony, it was 
the chief chanter who presented the initiate to the gods. The impor­
tance of the rogorogo was further emphasized by their receiving a 
special distribution of food (Buck 1938:^-67). Another important 
group in the religious ceremonials was that of the wood craftsmen, 
who were also given a special distribution of food.
A complete ceremony, including religious and social elements, 
received the general term of gogoro, but its parts were also termed 
gogoro. Buck says:
A number of observances (gogoro) are common to different 
rituals: purification of salt water, the touma invocation
with food, the tura'u ceremony of passing objects through 
fire, the erection of the table for offerings, the erection 
and closing of the door of the house of Tu, and the ta cere­
mony of striking with a coconut leaf to remove tapu and end 
the whole ceremony (1938:468).
The most important religious function of the marae was that it 
provided for maintaining and increasing the favor of the gods,
L

28in
essential for the production of sufficient food. A second function 
related to oracles regarding wars. Certain crises of life were inten­
sified through ritual on marae. Birth and death, particularly for 
individuals of high rank represented important crises of this sort.
The ceremony for human sacrifice at a marae was called 'akajoa 
(striking) because the corpse was reportedly struck 
(Buck 1938:^59). 
The body was placed in an earth oven. Only the bodies of chiefs and 
distinguished persons were given the'akapa ceremony on the marae.
At the death of a king, a victim, tukau, was killed by warri­
ors who wanted to show their affection for the deceased. The 'eaga 
victim was similar except that he was sacrificed after the king's 
death when the new king was consolidating his position. Commoners as 
well as women might be killed as 'eaga victims. The victim was eaten, 
unless the family was influential enough to save the body (Buck 1938:
central Polynesia, there is no certain evidence for this in Mangareva. 
Priestly undertakers, taura tao'i tupapaku, treated and dried corpses 
on the marae. The body of a king or high chief was carried to the 
chiefly marae Te Kehika; the body of a priest was taken to the marae 
of Te Hau-o-te-vehi; a commoner was treated in his own home. Mourners 
gathered in the assembly house, 'are tepere, for singing, dancing, and 
weeping at night. A four post bier was erected on the marae for a 
royal corpse while the rongorongo chanted. Celebrations at death con­
tinued as long as the relatives and supporters could supply food to 
the gathering (Buck 1938:U90). The prestige given the dead by placing 
them on a marae was reflected on living relatives.

Mangarevan marae served as festival places, gogoro, and for 
social events, 'aiga pure (hai, 'ai, means to recite something of a 
religious nature; pure means a prayer), which elsewhere in Polynesia 
meant prayer recital. Here the term means a festival of purely social 
nature as well; thus the terms gogoro and 'aiga pure have come to he 
synonymous (Buck 1938:^69).
Cook Islands
No intermediary was necessary in Mangaian placations of gods 
such as the fishermen's god Eua'atu or in some cases to Rua-tama'ine, 
but in a matter affecting several individuals an intermediary was 
necessary to ensure the proper results. Tribal gods could be consulted 
only by tribal priests. Buck reports (193^:177):
All the priests of the Mangaian tribal gods were inspired 
priests who became inspired by the gods when their advice 
was needed. They were all pi'a atua [pi'a, receptacle]. . . .
As the priest worked himself up into a state of possession 
. . . [his ]eyes became suffused, his face and body twitched, 
his mouth opened ('amama), and finally he spoke the words of 
the god in a trancelike manner, perhaps with an unnatural 
voice. . . .
By the constant practice of their profession, the priests 
could readily throw themselves into the state of possession.
To make doubly sure, however, they drank a preliminary bowl 
of kava.
The identity between the priests and the gods in matters of 
tribal policy is self-evident. As the priests had to answer questions 
on behalf of the gods, they often replied ambiguously or incoherently 
and left it up to the chiefs to interpret their sayings.
Another type of religious specialist in Mangaia was the sor­
cerer, tangata purepure, who used pure, incantations, to lesser gods 
in order to bring about death (Buck 193^:187). Sorcerers were paid
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 303 ===

in food or goods for services such as removing an enemy or expediting 
a love affair.
Food offerings constituted perhaps the largest portion of the 
ritual connected with the marae and religious worship, because all the 
tribal gods were satisfied with food. The quantity or quality of the 
food was not important as long as the act was made in recognition of 
the god (Buck 193^:178). The keeper of the national god house had to 
feed taro to the gods as one of his daily duties. Offerings were 
accompanied by simple, appropriate words; however, for public cere­
monial the incantations (karakia) composed and recited by ohe priests 
were much more elaborate (Buck 193^-:l8U) .
Human sacrifice, called e ika no Rongo (a fish of Kongo), 
represented a more serious type of offering because Rongo would be 
satisfied with nothing else. Traditionally the Tongaiti tribe fur­
nished the first victim for Rongo; Tongaiti were designated as a 
sacrifice-providing tribe, kopu ika, by the Ngariki tribe, and all 
defeated tribes were successively added to the list of kopu ika 
(Buck 193^:180). According to traditional accounts, until recent 
times only one victim was necessary for the installation of a new 
warrior leader on Mangaia. This sacrifice would be chosen by the 
victorious leader in consultation with the priests of Rongo from 
among the sacrifice-providing tribes (Buck 193*+:l80). The priest of 
Rongo gave a sacred belt to the warrior selected to slay the victim. 
The victim was called the ika *akatangi pa'u, the sacrifice enabling 
the peace drum to be sounded, for peace would come with the installa­
tion of the new warrior leader of Mangaia. The ika motu was another 
type of human victim sacrificed to bring victory in battle.
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 304 ===

r
2871
The slain victim was carried on the spears thrust through the
body, but a wooden litter was usually also provided to carry the body 
to the marae of Akaoro where it was deposited on a scaffold of hala 
wood for some time (Buck 1934:182). Later the body was carried in a 
sennit net scoop to the coastal marae called Orongo where it was 
placed on a block of limestone in front of the image of Rongo. The 
ears and nose were divided among chiefs and priests. A third form of 
human sacrifice described by Buck, the ika tea, was an unusual volun­
tary sacrifice by someone of high rank in order to break the existing 
rule (1934:183).
Release from a state of tapu, acquired through contact with 
the marae or gods, to gain the state of noa (free from religious 
tapu) could be sometimes accomplished by changing clothes but it 
required bathing in fresh water to remove the tapu of the marae and 
the close association with the gods.
Death ritual involved the preparation of the body by relatives
who laid the deceased out in his house. Relatives offered gifts of 
bark cloth which were distributed among guests. Sham battles also 
took place. Some bodies were buried in shallow pits, but most were 
cast down deep clefts or were hidden in secret caves that rendered 
them safe from interference by enemies (Buck 1934:191)• Apparently 
corpses stored in caves were occasionally removed, rubbed with oil 
and wrapped in fresh cloth so that they would be preserved indefi­
nitely. Some personal belongings were deposited with the corpse. 
Gill reports (1892:23):
If the body were buried in the earth, the face was invari­
ably laid downwards, chin and knees meeting, and the limbs 
well secured with strongest sennit cord. A thin covering
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 305 ===

r
2881
of earth was laid over the corpse, and large heavy stones 
were piled over the grave. The intention was to render it 
impossible for the dead to rise up and injure the living.
People of importance were buried within the tribal marae.
Noted chiefs and priests were sometimes exposed on the tribal marae
for several hours and then were moved to the tribal burial cave.
Death celebrations were held later to give the family of the
deceased time to prepare food for the feast. The death celebrations
took three definite forms, but all were collectively called eva. The
term eva was specifically applied to dirges accompanied by dances,
death talks, and special dirges and acting which followed a dart-
throwing competition (Buck 193^:192-196).
Marae priests, taura, of Tongareva in the northern Cook
Islands made material representations of gods from coconut leaf,
feathers, or wood which were used in ceremonies to gain the assistance
of these gods. In public ceremonies the high priest ascended the ahu
holding images of the god they wanted to appeal to, seated himself and
was possessed by the spirit. Important functions included appointment
of priests, invoking gods, eating turtles, and drinking tapu coconuts
(Buck 1932b:177).
Austral Islands (Tubuai, Ra'ivavae)
The only ceremonies reported are those of human sacrifice to 
a "supreme god," Taio Aia, conducted by priests at marae (Aitken 1930: 
115).
Hawaii
There were several grades or kinds of priests, kuhuna, in 
Hawaii. Kuhuna held a more important position than the inspirational
L

Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 306 ===

r
2 8 9 !
priests, kaula. Malo referred to kaula as prophets or seers hut said 
very little about their activities (1951:251). The alii (chiefs) wor­
shipped in a different manner than that of the common people who 
uttered their prayers themselves; the alii only moved his lips while 
a priest, kuhuna pule or kuhuna nui, said the prayers on the king's 
heiau (Malo 1951:81). The kuhuna pule conducted ritual; the kuhuna 
nui represented all the gods directly, including Kane, the creator 
of man.
Malo described the kuhuna anaana as a sorcerer who performed 
the kuni ceremony on a dead body if it was felt that his death was a 
result of anaana, that is, from being prayed to death. The kuni cere­
mony was designed to kill the person who had prayed the deceased to 
death. Malo used kuhuna anaana for both practitioners, but Emerson 
in a footnote to Malo (1951:103) changed his terminology to kuhuna 
kuni and kuhuna anaana, stressing that these two sorcerers were dis­
tinct and worked in opposition to one another. Malo also used the 
term kahuna kilokilo for "sorcerer" (1951:112).
Offerings to gods at heiau were presented by kuhuna pule or 
kuhuna nui. Only a temple priest, kuhuna pule heiau, could purify a 
person (in a huikala ceremony) who had come in contact with a dead 
person or was otherwise defiled'. Malo reported that priests conducted 
the kuni ritual for the alii and that after this ceremony the body was 
taken to the mua house and was placed in the heiau so that it could 
be deified and transformed into an aumakua (1951:10^-105). The 
process of apotheosis continued as follows:
The dead body was first wrapped in leaves of banana, 
wauke and taro, a rite which was called kapa lau, garment
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 307 ===

r
290I
of leaves. The hody "being thus completely enveloped, a 
shallow pit was dug and the hody was buried therein about 
one foot below the surface, after which a fire was made on 
the ground the whole length of the grave. This was kept 
constantly burning for about ten days, during which time 
the prayer called pule hui was continually recited. By 
that time the body had gone into decay and that night the 
bones were separated from the flesh and worship was per­
formed to secure their deification . . . (Malo 1951:105)-
After the king was deified, he was worshipped as a real god (akua
maoli). His successor built a new heiau for the reception of the
bones; the deceased king continued to be a god demanding worship.
Such a deity was called an aumakua (Malo 1951:106).
Other life crisis religious ritual came at the time a child 
entered the mua, or men's house, in which case pigs were offered at 
the altar, kuahu, in front of the images of the gods; at the time of 
circumcision, particularly for the sons of high chiefs; and at times 
when alii attempted to secure offspring, especially the first bom.
Kahuna played a major role in the religious worship surround­
ing agricultural fertility and the Makahiki first-fruits ceremonies.
The Makahiki god vrtiich had been carried around the island was finally 
returned to a luakini heiau, dismantled and stored in the heiau (Malo 
1951:150-151)• A temple or heiau called kukoae or kukoae-ahuwai was 
constructed by the king on the 26th day after the termination of the 
Makahiki ceremonies for purifying himself. Malo described the types 
of heiau a king might build at the end of the Makahiki (1951:152).
In the tabu period; Ku of the month Kalua or it might be of 
the month Nana, the king would make a heiau of the kind called 
a heiau-loulu; or it might be, he would put up one of the 
kind called a mao. He might prefer an ordinary luakini; or 
he might see fit to order the building of a temple to pro­
pitiate the gods for abundant harvest, -which would be a 
luakini houululu ai; or he might order the building of a 
war temple, a luakini kaua. It was a matter which lay with 
the king.

r
The king used two rituals in his worship, one was of Ku, the
other of Lono. The priests of Ku (the war god) were of higher rank
than those of Lono, and the ritual itself was more strict and arduous
(Malo 1951:159)* The tern moo-Lono was used to describe the priests
of Lono. The heiau was called a luakini if the king planned to use
the rites of Ku there and mapele if he wanted to use the Lono rites.
Malo reports that the temple was noa, that is,the tapu was removed,
only when the aho, or perfection, was found through incantations
and ritual. Malo described the method of deciding how to build a
luakini (1951:l6l):
The king, in the first place, inquired of his high priest 
in regard to building a luakini; whether he thought the old 
luakini would answer, provided the house and the fence were 
renewed; whether the old stone wall should be allowed to 
remain; and whether the old idols should still continue to 
be used.
If the king's proposition was agreed to, the first thing 
was to perform the ceremony of purification (huikala) on the 
heiau, and make it noa (i.e., free), to enable the workmen to 
enter it, that they might put a new fence about it and newly 
thatch the house with loulu palm or with uki.
If the king, the priests and others agreed that it was 
best to build an entirely new luakini, the kahuna kuhi-kuhi- 
puu-one was sent for. It was his function to exhibit a plan 
of the heiau to the king, because this class of persons were 
thoroughly educated in what concerned a heiau. They were 
acquainted with the heiau which had been built from the most 
ancient times, from Hawaii to Kauai, some of which had gone 
into ruins. These kuhi-kuhi-puu-one knew all about these 
old temples, because they had studied them on the ground 
had seen their sites and knew the plans of them all.
When the luakini was almost completed, the priests, alii, and certain 
other religious persons had to be purified before they could enter the 
heiau. The entry was made under conditions of strict tapu. A series 
of pule chanted by kuhuna initiated the ceremony. The house founda­
tions were marked off, the kaai gods (small carved figures on short 
staffs) and their kahu (bearers) were presented, the service of the

pule-kai was conducted, and the haku-ohia image was brought in and

negate the ritual. Success was followed by a large feast on the tem­

girdle of braided coconut on it and of cutting this "navel string" of 
the idol; on the following day a malo, girdle or breech cloth, was 
placed on the image and it was given a new name, Moi, lord of all the 
idols (Malo 1951:173)• The final consecration of the temple was fol­
lowed by a lifting of the tapu and it was then noa loa.
New Zealand
In New Zealand the term tohunga was generally applied to 
priestly experts (Best 1952:68), of which several types were distin­
guished. The tohunga matauhi were prophets or seers. According to 
Best (1952:80) they could be called also tohunga puri or tohunga 
kiate. The terms pouwhiro for a high class priest and horomata for 
a third grade priest were recorded. Those priests who learned the 
higher type of oral traditions and acquired sacerdotal lore were 
known as pia, taura, and tauira at different stages of their progress. 
Religious knowledge was taught in a house of learning, whare wananga.
tions," used by Maori priests and prayers or invocations; he stressed 
that the chants were more like magical formulae than prayers (1952: 
78). Offerings, mostly of food, were required by all deities below 
the first class (io).
Best made a distinction between the "charms" and "incanta-

29H
Humans were offered in the following instances: l) direct-

carried out to obtain a victim; 3) in war the first enemy victim was

k) on the occasion of building an important house; 5) launching a new 
war canoe; 6) completion of a new £a, a fortified village; and 
7) occasionally in connection with agricultural needs (Best 1952:75).
Summary
It is difficult to generalize about East Polynesian religious 
specialists and the ceremonies they conducted because of uneven 
ethnographic coverage and because of considerable variation from 
island to island. However, two major types of priests are generally 
recognizable, as in the Marquesan case: an inspirational priest
(tau'a nui) and a ceremonial priest (tahuna o'ono). A simpler situa­
tion exists in the Tuamotus where the head of a family or chief nor­
mally served also as priest. On at least one Tuamotuan island,
Fangatau, there were also tu-kau or "high priests," but there was no 
complex religious hierarchy as in the Marquesas or Hawaii. Priests 
in all marae ceremonies had assistants who handled sacrifices and 
performed menial tasks within the highly tapu areas. All inspira­
tional priests were supposed to learn and vociferate the will of the 
tribal god. In this context, they presided over tribal ceremonial. 
Caring for the remains of the dead fell normally to the inspirational 
priest and his assistants. Where priests such as the Marquesan cere­
monial priest were differentiated, they served as their title
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 311 ===

r
2951
(tahuna) indicates, as experts who recited the chants and conducted 
the rituals. In addition to these two specialists, sorcerers were
also commonly recognized although they did not participate in marae
ritual. We have terms to apply to the sorcerers in Mangareva,
Mangaia, Hawaii, and elsewhere.
The ceremonies conducted on marae under the direction of such
priests fall into a few major categories with numerous variations.
The most basic aspect of marae ritual is that of making offerings to 
gods; the offerings ranged from simple food to human beings. Humans 
were usually sacrificed at the death of a chief or priest who was to 
be deified. The series of feasts and ceremonies described for the 
Marquesas illustrates this important function of marae. Only occa­
sionally did marae serve as burial places because the bones were 
removed from the marae after a period of exposure to be secreted 
elsewhere; marae served more as a place at which to honor the dead 
of high status by long periods of feasting and chanting. Other life 
crises for members of the royal line, particularly the first born, 
were part of this same emphasis. In the Tuamotus ceremonies were 
conducted at the birth of the first born and at the first sign of 
pregnancy, at the time of cireum- or supercision, and at the inaugu­
ration of a new king or queen when often a new marae was constructed, 
and at times of tribal crises when the king became ill. The pa'i 
atua ceremony described for the Society Islands well illustrates the 
ritual associated with tribal and national leaders. The second major 
type of marae ceremony concerns the relationships between the people 
and their food supply, particularly horticultural products and first 
fruit rituals, the matahiti of Tahiti and the makahiki of Hawaii.
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 312 ===

The distribution of sacred classes of food, such as turtles, has been 
described for the Tuamotus. The third major set of ceremonies were 
initiations of priests into an order that would continue to appeal to 
the gods of that marae. These ceremonies are best known from 
Mangareva and from Tahiti. The fourth major class of ceremonies 
involved rituals conducted to obtain oracles of military importance, 
but little specific information is available on these. In general, 
marae served not as burial places but as altars and places for making 
offerings to the gods to whom the chief or king (or his representative 
priests) could appeal.
Easter Island
The importance of the ahu and the large stone statues for the
Easter Islanders diminished by the middle of the nineteenth century;
this is indicated by the statement of Eyraud, the first European to
stay on the island for any length of time (nine months in 186^):
. . . although I have always associated with them in the 
greatest familiarity I have not been able to discover any 
truly positive act of religious cult. In all the houses 
many statuetes are seen, about thirty cm. high, represent­
ing male figures, fishes, birds, etc. They are undoubtedly 
idols, but I have not noticed that they have been attrib­
uted any kinds of honors. I have occasionally seen the 
natives taking these statues, lifting them into the air, 
making sone gestures, and accompanying all of it with a 
sort of dance and an insignificant song. What do they 
mean with that? I believe they do not know much about 
it. They do quite simply what they have seen their fathers 
do, without offering it any further thought. If you ask 
them what it means, they answer you as about their games, 
that such is the custom of the country (l866b:69).
Metraux believed that Easter Island religion was more affected by the
series of slave raids and contact with Europeans than other aspects of
the culture (19^0:308), and Roussel, one of the early missionaries,

Gods
Easter Island gods, like all Polynesian gods, must have 
belonged to two classes. The first class included great figures of 
creation and major gods like Tangaroa, Tu, and Rongo; and the second 
class included numerous deities which ranged from the sons of major 
gods to deified ancestors or simple spirits. A god's place in one or 
the other of these two categories did not correspond to his actual 
importance in religion or his importance in the sentiments of the 
worshipper (Metraux 19^0:309). This pattern was evident in our dis­
cussion of all of East Polynesia.
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 314 ===

r
297I
The principal god at the time of European contact was 
Makemake. The great gods, Tangaroa, Tane, Tu, and Rongo, so impor­
tant and characteristic of East Polynesia were unimportant in the 
documented materials on the island. On Easter Island these gods 
were recorded only as legendary figures or as divine ancestors.
Tangaroa
"The kings of Easter Island, like those of Mangareva and 
other Polynesian islands, traced their origin back to Tangaroa"
(Metraux 19^-0:310). Metraux believed that Tangaroa was originally a 
god, although he noted that modern natives regard Tangaroa only as a 
person with great mana. The name Tangaroa-uri signifies the month 
November on Easter Island; the name Tangaroa-mea occurs in a legend 
dealing with this god's trip to Easter Island in the form of a seal 
whose mana was over the sea, while the mana of his brother, Teko-of- 
the-long-feet, was over the land. According to this legend Easter 
Islanders captured Tangaroa-mea at Tongariki where he landed and put 
him into an earth-oven, but when he would not cook, they realized 
that he was a god (Metraux 19^-0:310-311).
Rongo
Metraux said: 
"Rongo is mentioned in the king's genealogy
after Tangaroa and is given as the son of Tangaroa. There is no evi­
dence that the site of Orongo was named after Rongo" (19hO;3H). 
Although there is no record of it, Rongo may have been the patron god 
of the tangata rongo-rongo, the chanters who recited from the wooden 
tablets. This association is found both in Mangareva and the

Marquesas; however, the word "rongo" also refers in a general sense 
to sound.
Hiro
According to Routledge (1919:21+2), Hiro was a sky god to whom 
they directed prayers for rain. Metraux, however, thought that Hiro 
was the patron of thieves on the island. The Pu-o-Hiro (Trumpet-of- 
Hiro) is a large stone with a natural hole which when blown sounds 
like a trumpet. An ahu on the south coast, Tangaroa-Hiro (Metraux 
191+0 :310) also contains the name of this god.
Tiki
Metraux felt that Makemake had taken over or absorbed the 
characteristics of Tiki by the time of European contact. Tiki is 
mentioned several times in the creation chant, in which he mated with 
Hina-popoina (Hina-the-heaped-up), the woman he made by piling up sand 
into a figure. Tiki appears in the creation myth as he copulates 
with his daughter, Hina-kauhara (Metraux 19^0:315)•
Ruanuku
Metraux (191+0:315) maintained that the name "Kaunuku" or 
"Kuanuku" in Thomson's list of kings of the island was a spelling of 
Ruanuku, a widely known god of central Polynesia. More recently,
Barthel (1958) referred to "Atua Ruanuku" which he derived from one 
of the symbols on the rongo-rongo tablets.
Makemake
Makemake was the greatest remembered god in historic times. 
Metraux sees this god name as a replacement for either Tane or Tiki

or both. The earliest record of this god is from the details left

I observed that on the day which we erected the crosses, 
when our chaplains went accompanying the holy images, clothed

supreme god, "Etua," who demanded sacrifices of chickens, sweet pota­
toes, fish, and sometimes children, through priests (1880:117-131). 
Makemake is also credited with causing the first man and woman to 
spring from the earth more or less like a plant. This sounds very 
similar to descriptions of "supreme gods" recorded by other mission­
aries in Polynesia; I suspect that this is merely an interpretation 
given by Zumbohm and which resulted from missionary teachings, rather 
than being a part of the earlier religious belief. I think it is of 
significance here that Zumbohm states (l880, 6:117-131) *• 
"In order 
to designate the devil, we were obliged to add to their vocabulary 
the w-‘ d tiaporo (devil)" (emphasis mine). Conversion to at least 
nominal Christianity took place rapidly. The first missionary activ­
ity came in l861f for nine months, and was followed by the first per­
manent mission in 1866; by 1868 the last natives agreed to being 
converted. Zumbohm reports that Makemake, in contrast to ten other 
gods, was supposed to have had no wife.

In this fragment of myth, Makemake, as creator of mankind, 
plays the part assumed by Tane or Tiki in the mythology of 
central and marginal Polynesia. The theme of the multiple 
copulation of Makemake is paralleled by the various matings 
of Tane in New Zealand and Tiki in Mangareva and the Tuamotus.
In the Maori mythology Tane begat by Hina-ahu-one, his wife, 
the eggs of a bird from which have spring all of the birds of 
the air. As Makemake also produces birds, I shall consider 
Makemake the local Easter Island name for the old god Tane 
(19^0:31*0 .
In the creation chant Tiki had this role; it seems clear that 
Makemake recently assumed the characteristics of this earlier god.

A number of other deities have no parallels elsewhere in

Metraux found that all the lesser gods were referred to as 
akuaku; deities of higher rank maintained connections with a particu­
lar locality and with the people living there. He was able to record 
the localities for only 28 akuaku in 193^ (Metraux 19^0:318), and 
today even fewer are known. A few of the akuaku appear to be real

302I
gods, others were demons or nature spirits, and others were spirits 
of the deified dead. Desmedt (1931:135) considers the Easter Island 
term akuaku to he cognate with the Mangarevan term akuaku, meaning 
"thin, speaking only of men and women." The parallel is supported 
in the Easter Island conception of ghosts resembling emaciated men.
Gods Invoked at Ahu
Nothing is known about the gods appealed to at ahu because 
the ceremonies were not conducted or even remembered in historic 
times. 
Statues recorded by Cook that had ariki names suggest that 
deceased ariki were invoked in ahu ceremonies after their deification. 
Priests could have invoked other major gods also. Akuaku did not 
play a role in ahu ceremonies.
Ahu Structures and Ethnographic Definition
In Chapter I a number of ahu types were recognized. As pre­
viously mentioned, only one class, the image ahu, shows relationship 
to East Polynesian marae. Because they were not in use in historic 
times, little was recorded about the statues and ceremonies conducted 
there. Since European contact ahu were mainly used as burial cairns; 
their use diminished as centers for complex religious ritual. Eluci­
dation of the earlier complex must depend almost entirely on archae­
ological evidence.
Priests and Ceremonies 
Priests
It was impossible to separate clearly the priesthood of high 
priests from ordinary magicians or sorcerers at the time of Metraux's

J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 320 ===

r
3031
study: 
"It is not possible to ascertain whether the ancient priest­
hood was always loosely organized or whether the original institutions 
were forgotten after the Peruvian raid of 1862 which crushed, the 
native civilization of the island" (l9*tO:32U). Geiseler claimed that 
there were no priests, hut that chiefs performed priestly functions in 
earlier times (1883:33)• Routledge believed that there were no priests, 
(1919:239)- Thomson recognized no priests, though there were wizards 
and sorcerers (1891:^+07) . Metraux has referred to Roussel (1926:360), 
whom he considered to be the best authority, to make a case for priests 
and a priesthood: "There were priests and priestesses. Their func­
tions were reduced to very few things: to preside at the rejoicings
on the occasion of the birth of a child and to drive away the bad 
spirits of a sick man." How these individuals differed from the 
ivi-atua and koromaki described below is not known. Priests composed 
and recited chants in honor of the dead, but such limited activity 
does not tally with the exalted social position of the priests who 
were next in rank to the king according to Roussel (Metraux 19^0:
32U). Further support for the existence of a priesthood comes from 
Behrens at the time of initial European contact:
One amongst these was an entirely white man, who was wearing 
white chocks of wood in his ears as large as one's fist, and 
bore a very devout appearance . . . .  I took some of the 
people to be priests, because they paid more reverence to the 
gods than did the rest; and showed themselves much more devout 
in their ministrations. One could also distinguish these 
from the other people quite well, not only by their wearing 
great white plugs in their ear lobes, but in having the head 
wholly shaven and hairless. One of them was with us on board 
the ship as above related. They wore a head-dress of white 
and black feathers which was just like storks' feathers . . . 
(Gonzalez y Haedo 1908:13^, 136, Appendix l).
Gonzalez's report in 1770 provides further information regarding the
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 321 ===

r
presence of an organized priesthood (Gonzalez y Haedo 1908:102):
Others (whom I believe to he their ministers) occupy dwell­
ings close to the statues; these are built of earth below, 
but with an entrance way or porch of very roughly hewn and 
clumsily set up stones, after the fashion of a wall, with 
a certain number of steps for passing from one platform or 
surface of ground to another on different levels. It is 
known that they work the stone, on which may be seen several 
different figures, squares, oblongs, arcs [ rumbos], tri­
angles, and trapezia, by means of another stone of harder 
substance than the mass, and the same method is followed,
I believe, in fashioning the statues.
Metraux said that the power and the duties of the priests did 
not differ very much from the general Polynesian pattern. Makemake 
manifested his will through male or female priests; he entered their 
bodies and spoke through their mouths. Priests received food offer­
ings and, on some occasions, human sacrifices. Both Routledge and 
Metraux recorded the role of the ariki-paka, the noblemen of the Miru 
tribe, in the performance of religious ritual, and it is probable that 
the high priests were taken from royal or aristocratic families.
The sacredness of kingship and nobility linked the ariki 
closely to ceremonial life. Geiseler (l883:33)> on the authority of 
the informant Salmon, suggests the priestly nature of certain ariki 
functions: "In earlier times, when the kings still had significant
power and commanded respect, they functioned as priests who initiated 
and led the religious celebrations" (my translation).
That the attendants of the king were called tahura may indi­
cate that they were priests, because the general name for priests in 
Mangareva, according to Laval (quoted in Metraux 19^0:325), is tahura 
(others gave it as taura). Metraux cautions against over-reliance on 
this association because tahura on Easter Island also means "expert" 
and therefore not necessarily a priest.

r
3051
Two other terms have been used to refer to priests; ivi-atua 
(lineage-of-the-gods), which Metraux translates as "family of-the- 
gods" when he uses it more inclusively, and koromaki as "the one who 
violently desires something" (Metraux 19^+0:136, 325). Metraux says 
that the present population considers the first type, ivi-atua, as a 
magician or sorcerer and uses the same word for charm or spell. Ivi- 
atua are referred to also as inspirational priests (Me'traux 19^0:325). 
The koromaki were recognized experts in easting spells and had the 
power to kill enemies or to benefit people. Their functions were 
similar to the ivi-atua.
Priests spoke in shrill, high-pitched voices when possessed 
by gods. In one legend of young men who pretended to be Makemake, 
it is said that "they made their voices like that of a god (hakaatua 
i te reo)" (Metraux 19^0:325).
A third type of important specialist was tangata taku (taku, 
charm). They had spirit familiars through whom they cured or killed 
people or accomplished difficult tasks.
The power and prestige of priests must have been destroyed 
before the missionaries came, since they made no opposition to the 
introduction of Christianity. Metraux believed that Easter Island 
religious ceremony may have been less complex than that in other parts 
of Polynesia (19^-0:329):
There will always be doubt as to whether the old reli­
gious ceremonies on Easter Island were of the same complex­
ity and importance as those of other Polynesian islands.
Eyraud, who stayed on the island for a long time, never 
saw any ceremony connected with the ahu or any other sacred 
place and never saw the natives in an act of religious 
worship.

One of the few recorded instances of Easter Islanders making 
offerings to statues comes from Roggeveen in his 1772 report:
What form of worship these people comprises we were 
not able to gather any full knowledge of, owing to the 
shortness of our stay among them; we noticed only that 
they kindle fire in front of certain remarkably tall stone 
figures they set up; and, there after squatting on their 
heels with heads bowed down, they bring the palms of their 
hands together and alternately raise and lower them 
(Gonzalez y Haedo 1908:15).
Behrens also noted the use of fire in ritual before the statues
(Gonzalez y Haedo 1908:133):
They kindled many fires by their idols, wither by way of 
offerings or for the purposes of prayer. In the early 
morning we looked out and could see from some distance 
that they had prostrated themselves towards the rising 
sun and had kindled some hundreds of fires, which probably 
betokened a morning oblation to their gods.
Roggeveen may be simply referring to the report of Behrens, and if this 
is the case, it would greatly decrease the reliability of the obser­
vation.
Through their priests people presented food and handicraft 
offerings tc gods. Before every meal, food was presented to Makemake 
and Haua, and occasionally, to some lesser god. Under some unusual 
circumstances Makemake requested human sacrifices. The victims were 
kidnapped children, prisoners, or persons who had displeased the king 
(Geiseler 1883:31, 215, ^73) > (Me'traux 19^0:329).

A priest had to eat special food and conduct suitable rites in 
a house before it could be occupied by private individuals. The food 
was prepared in a special earth oven and was eaten by a priest alone 
in a corner of the new house. There is some indication that such 
duties were incumbent upon the king (Metraux 19^40:330). Agricultural 
fertility rites required that a priest (ivi-atua) bury or spread 
pieces of coral or seaweed on the plantations. These charms were to 
increase fertility. Other charms were made by the ariki-paka (only 
those ariki-paka who were priests) to increase the fertility of 
chickens. Other types of private rites included use of the numerous 
small wooden images kept in the dwelling houses.

Estella (1920:21) said that the King acted as an intermediary

Ceremonies
The lengthy ceremony surrounding the bird cult and the selec­
tion of the annual tangata-manu is best known because it was being con­
ducted up to about 90 years ago and because it was the major aspect of 
organized religious activity on the island in historic times. Metraux 
noted: 
"The rites performed were not only connected with the cult of
the g . eat-c v.t god on the island, Makemake, but were endowed with special 
social significance, since ideas of prestige and economic privileges 
were closely associated with these ceremonies" (l9*tO:33l). Acquisition 
of the first egg of the manu-tara, or sooty tern, by the servants or 
representatives, hopu, of a chief was the primary goal. This chief 
then became the tangata-manu, or bird man for one season.
The ceremonies and feasts associated with the selection of the 
tangata-manu were conducted at the village and ceremonial site of 
Orongo, on the south lip of the volcano Rano Kau opposite the small 
offshore islands of Motu nui, Motu iti, and Motu kaukau where thou­
sands of the sooty terns gathered to lay eggs from August to October. 
There are several caves on Motu-nui which were occupied by the hopu 
while waiting for the birds and while searching for the first egg.
A third site of importance for the ceremonies of the bird cult was 
Mataveri, located on the lower north slope of Rano Kau. Participants
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.

309I
in the ceremonies met here for feasting and dancing. Routledge
(1919:259) said that the Mataveri stay lasted "months." Routledge
described the selection of participants (1919:258):
The great object of life in Easter was to be the first to 
obtain one of the newly laid eggs of this bird manu-tara.
It was too solemn a matter for there to be any general 
scramble. Only those who belonged to the clan in the 
ascendancy for the time being could enter on the quest.
Sometimes one group would keep it in their hands for years, 
or they might pass it on to a friendly clan. This selec­
tion gave rise, as might be expected, to burnings of hearts; 
the matter might be, and probably often was, settled by war.
One year the Marama were inspired with jealousy because the 
Miru had chosen the Ngaure as their successors, and burnt 
down the house of Ngaara.
Routledge also reported that soon as the clan (mata) ascended to the
ao, or power, they took up residence at Mataveri to ensure their
right to search for the first egg the next season (1919:259):
Here [ Mataveri ] there were a number of large huts with 
stone foundations; in these they resided, with their wives 
and families. . . . Naturally the months passed at Mataveri 
were occupied by the residents in feasting as well as danc­
ing, and equally naturally the victims were human. . . .
When July approached the company, or some of them, wound 
their way up the western side of the hill, along the ever- 
narrowing summit to the village of Orongo; the path can 
just be traced in certain lights, and is known as the "Road 
of the Ao." They spent their time while awaiting the birds 
in dancing each day in front of the houses; food was brought 
up by the women . . . .
The village of Orongo was divided into groups of houses for specific
types of individuals:
The group of houses at the end among the carved rocks was 
taboo during the festival, for they were inhabited by the 
rongo-rongo men, the western half being apportioned to the 
experts from Hotu iti, the eastern to those from Kotuu.
"They chanted all day; they stopped an hour to eat, that 
was all." They came at the command of Ngaara, but it is 
noteworthy that he himself never appeared at Orongo, though 
he sometimes paid a friendly call at Mataveri.

The important supernatural associations with the participants in 
search for the egg were described by Routledge as follows (1919:260):
The privilege of obtaining the first egg was a matter 
of competition between members of the Ao, but the right to 
be one of the competitors was secured only by supernatural 
means. An "iva-atua," a divinely gifted individual, of the 
kind who had the gift of prophecy, dreamed that a certain 
man was favoured by the gods, so that if he entered for the 
race he would be a winner, or in technical parlance, become 
a bird-man or "tangata-manu." The victor, on being success­
ful, was ordered to take a new name, which formed part of 
the revelation, and this bird-name was given to the year 
in which victory was achieved, thus forming an easily remem­
bered system of chronology.
Routledge also mentioned four deities who were connected with the
eggs (1919:260):
. . . Hawa-tuu-take-take, who was "chief of the eggs," and 
Make-make, both of whom were males; there were also two 
females, Vie Hoa, the wife of Hawa, and Vie Kenatea. Each 
of these four had a servant, . . . who were also supernatu­
ral beings. Those going to take the egg recited the names 
of the gods before meat [ ?], inviting them to partake.
On Motu nui the hopu lived in a large cave. A line which 
passed through the center of the cave divided the islet into the Ko 
Tuu and Hotu-iti halves. The same dividing line passed through 
another cave which contained a statue about 2 feet high called 
Titahanga-o-te-henua (The Boundary of the Land). The only other 
stone statue which was associated with the Crongo ceremonies is 
called Hoa-haka-nania (Breaking Wave) about eight feet tall and weighs 
four tons. This statue was originally in a centrally located house at 
Orongo called Taura-renga. The statue was made of hard basalt rather 
than the Rano Raraku type tuff. The back and face were white, with 
the "tracings" in red (Routledge (1919:257j Fig. 31> 106).
When the first egg was found by a hopu, the news was immedi­
ately transmitted to Orongo where his employer shaved his head and

During times of peace feasts appear to have been held on a 
nearly continuous basis; many of these had some religious associa­
tions. The general name for feast is koro; this is also the name of 
the large house erected for festive occasions (Metraux 19^0:3^3).
Food and its display were the most important aspects of the feast. 
Accumulation of food for feasts was a shared concern of all people.
The paina ceremony, apparently celebrated to honor the memory 
of a dead parent, generally a father, was "an indirect part of the 
funeral duties of a son," according to Metraux (19^0:3^3). The feast 
could be held right after the death or several years thereafter; how­
ever, it was usually held during the summer when the three central 
stars of Orion were in the correct position. Guests as well as the 
entire family of the deceased contributed food. The feast and the

Although Routledge was told that the paina might be held for a
living relative, Metraux believed that this was erroneous, and he 
maintained instead that the koro feast was for honoring a living 
father, mother, father-in-law, or mother-in-law (19^0:3^6). The 
paina and koro are very similar and may have differed only in that 
one was for a living relative and the other for a deceased one. The 
man conducting a koro presented his father with a string of 200 or 
300 fowls, which his father then distributed among relatives, the 
largest share going to one of the father's brothers, called motuha. 
After the chickens had been received and transferred by five people, 
they were eaten. The man who gave a great koro and was generous, 
earned the title of tangata honui (great man, generous) (Metraux 19^0:

the mother-in-law of his son, Miguel Rapahango. He wanted 
to make his son "big" (haka honui taana poki).

Koro feasts were held in extremely large houses, some were 
several hundred feet long and over 20 feet high; this was a symbol of 
the generosity of the koro giver. Tepano, Metraux’s informant, said 
that the house was intended for young people who resided in them for 
months, but legends indicate that probably adults also enjoyed the 
carefree life in the koro houses (19^0:3^9)* Certain tapus seem to 
have been enforced in the koro but no religious ritual was mentioned 
except that they were consecrated by an ariki. Their relationship to 
the ahu is unclear. Metraux recorded one song performed during a koro 
feast:
I o te korongo mai nei 
I Moana-vera-vera-ra-tahai 
Rima turu, turn
Here is the feast sung 
In Moana-vera-vera-ra-tahai 
Hands raising, raising.
Moana-vera-vera-ra-tahai is a place name I recorded in 1968 near the
cluster of ahu at Tahai. It would appear that the koro, like the
paina, may have taken place on or near the ahu.
A feast similar to the koro, but held only in the fall and 
winter, was the areauti. Special houses were constructed for these 
feasts at the expense of a man who wanted to increase his prestige 
by giving away food. Eyraud described the areauti as follows (l866,
38:66-67):
The paina is succeeded by the areauti, in which there are 
no more big races, no complicated evolutions, and no palat­
able sweet potato banquets. On the grounds of the feast

are built big houses, I should say huts higher than the

Other feasts recorded by Metraux include the puke, the kaunga, 
and feasts of the boat. The puke, celebrated in the month of August, 
involved games using seaweed. The kaunga was a feast reserved for 
young people who danced single-file on a narrow strip paved with 
pebbles, probably beach gravel. The feast of the boat included imi­
tating the activities of a ship's crew, here on an earth ship, miro 
oone. Routledge believed that these dances and the feast commemo­
rated Captain Cook's visit of 177*+ and that Cook and his sailors were 
deified by the natives who erected big houses (hare-a-te-atua) to 
celebrate this cult. Metraux believed that the hare-a-te-atua were 
probably big houses built for the feasts in which ceremonies and 
dancing of several types may have been performed (l9*+0:35l) • None 
of these three feasts have any association with ahu.
Social Organization and Ahu 
Metraux reported: "The natives fully agree that the ahus which
extended along the coast, belonged to different 'families' or line­
ages and that members of the lineage were buried there" (l9*+0 :126). 
The houses of the living members of the lineage and landowning group 
were located behind the ahu. What is the composition and basis for 
membership in such a local group which builds ahus? In Polynesia 
these local groups were structured as ancestor-oriented kin groups 
(Goodenough 196l:13*+6).
If we can demonstrate which landholding kin unit is respon­
sible for construction and maintenance of ahu, we will be able to
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 333 ===

r
3lSl
characterize the cultural context of Easter Island ahu in greater 
detail, and to discuss social organization through time as well as at 
the time of contact based on the distribution of datable ahu struc­
tures.
The first published description of social units among the 
historic Easter Islanders came from Routledge (1919:221, Fig. 9l) 
who referred to ten "clans" (mata) and indicated their geographical 
distribution as well as their dual alignment into the Ko Tuu and the 
Hotu-iti. Williamson (192*0 described the political system in detail 
utilizing some of Routledge's unpublished data. Metraux (l9*t0) felt 
that Routledge's term "clan" was too specific and possibly inappro­
priate for the ten large Easter Island social units, so he referred 
to them as "tribes." These units apparently had a strong patrilineal 
bias but were generally recognized as ambilineal or nonunilinear 
descent groups. Metraux called them "paternal descent groups" (19*1-0: 
120) but also said that if the father was a foreigner the affiliation 
could be made through the mother. Instead of using Metraux's term 
tribe for the ten mata, we should consider the entire island, or 
Segment I as Goldman has recently defined it (1970:118), as the 
"tribe." Earlier this social unit was under the ariki mau and later 
it came under a temporal lord or warrior leader. There was no native 
term for this unit just as there was no name for the island as a whole.
The next smaller social unit would be the ten or so mata.
These should be recognized as the nonunilinear descent groups or 
ramages characteristic of Polynesia. 
These ten mata, as illustrated 
in Figure 51, are the Miru, Hamea, Raa, Marama, Haumoana, Ngatimo,

Kgaurs, Tupahotu, Hitiura (Ureohei), and Koro-orongo. Metraux said 
(19*10:120):
Probably at one time every tribe [mata] had its own ter­
ritory which was known by the name of the group. Later the 
tribes became more scattered and isolated members or entire 
households of one tribe lived within the limits of another 
district; in some regions along the coast representatives of 
more than three groups were mixed. The old territorial 
divisions thus came to be mere districts where the main 
part of the mata, perhaps the senior line, was settled, and 
it had little to do with the actual distribution of the 
tribes.
Goldman referred to these as status lineages, his equivalent of the 
ramage, as Segment II in his designations (1970:119). In other East 
Polynesian islands the term mata was sometimes combined with kainga 
to form matakainga (Society Islands, mata'einanga Manihiki-Rakahanga, 
matakeinanga), meaning "people" or "district." The term kainga was 
much more widely used singly, normally to mean "land" or "territory" 
(Samoa, 1aiga; Onotoa, Ellice Is., kainga; Tokelaus, 1ainga; Tikopia, 
kainanga; Futuna and Uvea, kainga; Hawaii, 'aim).
Island society was a subdivision of a mata which Metraux (19*1-0:120) 
called a "lineage." The Miru, the highest ranking mata, traditionally 
were divided into 13 descent lines (lineages) ranked apparently 
according to seniority of descent. These thirteen are: l) Ongo
(Honga), 2) Mataiva, 3) Rauvai, k) Kao, 5) Toko-te-rangi, 6) Maari,
7) Ko-era, 8) Hahai, 9) Matapu, 10) Moa-tahu, ll) Kai-huharu, 12) Ure- 
te-Niu, 13) Tuu-ko-ihu (Metraux 19*1-0:126). Of these the Honga lineage 
was the senior line and the kin group of the ariki mau, leader of the 
entire island. Each of these kin units had their own territory and 
probably their own ahu. There is some confusion in Metraux's account
A smaller social unit distinguishable in historic Easter
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 336 ===

r
3 1 9 !
lineages; it is not clear whether the Easter Islanders actually 
referred to these units as ivi, the term Metraux translated as "line­
ages," but also as "extended families" (19^0:325, 98). Goldman ques­
tioned whether the Easter Islanders even had a term for this lineage 
level which he designates as Segment III (1970:120). Much of the con­
fusion here results from lack of clarity regarding what "lineage" 
means when applied to Polynesian society. The term lineage was also 
used by Metraux in speaking of a named segment of land, henua poreko 
ranga (land where ancestors were born), as the "ground belonging to a 
lineage" (19^0:1^2). The size of the lineage here is indicated by 
Metraux's mention of the statue rahi on Tongariki (ahu?) which marked 
a boundary between the Vaimaho and Vaikoveka lineages. These two are 
the only lineages other than those of the Miru whose general location 
has been recorded (See Fig. 51)• Metraux also mentioned a strip of 
land extending from the shore towards the interior, but it is not 
clear whether this was called henua poreko ranga. Metraux added that 
the Ahu Anakoiroroa in Anakena belonged half to the Te Kena lineage 
and half to the Honga lineage (19^0:127)• Some ahu may have thus 
served as boundary markers for lineages.
The smallest segment or social unit was the extended family 
which comprised a household, for which Metraux also uses the term 
"ivi." These households were patrilineal and mostly patrilocal and 
probably cultivated land in common. Goldman refers to these as 
Segment IV of the social organization.
The tumu relationship between social units, among the mata 
and lineages, is one of importance for clarifying the spatial rela­
tionships of these groups. Although in the past tumu relationship was
L

192U, 2:ll+5) 3 Metraux believed that members of a group who settled in 
the territory of another group were either related to the inhabitants 
by marriage or were captives and that this would explain why the tumu 
was defined as a group with which marriage connections existed, or as 
a group which was obliged to work for another (19^0:123). The Hamea 
and Raa mata were reportedly the tumu of the Miru, and the Miru was 
the tumu of the Tupa-hotu, Ureohei, and Marama, because they might 
intermarry. Tumu relationships are also known between some of the 
defined lineages: the Honga were the tumu of the Te Kena, and of the
Ure-o-kao; and the Te kena were the tumu of the Ure-o-kao (Metraux 
19^0:123, 126). It seems clear that the tumu relationship is too 
imperfectly understood to be of real assistance.
Summary
Metraux wondered if Easter Island religion had been as com­
plexly organized as that of other Polynesian islands. Polynesian 
ethnography compared in this chapter, supplemented by the limited 
data on Easter Island, indicates that the level of complexity in reli­
gious behavior on Easter Island was similar to that of the Tuamotus 
and was probably never as elaborate as the religious complexes of 
Tahiti or Hawaii. This seems to be particularly true cf the archaeo­
logical phases I defined as Decadent, Protohistoric, and Historic.
The lack of complexity relates directly to the small population in 
contrast to that of the larger island groups.
I stressed that historic and late prehistoric Easter Islanders 
were linguistically, morphologically, socially and, in general,
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 338 ===

r
32H
culturally Polynesian. To what extent, then, can the religious com­
plex he identified as a Polynesian one? The most important Easter 
Island god in historic times, Makemake, had taken over the features 
of the widespread Polynesian pantheon god, Tane (Tiki). Tangaroa was 
known hut not invoked in ahu ceremony, as is the case in some other 
Polynesian islands, notahly the Cook Islands and Mangareva. Kongo's 
presence on Easter Island is indicated, and a possible association 
with the rongo-rongo chanters has also heen suggested. There is no 
clear indication that Tu was a god on Easter Island. Other gods such 
as Hiro (rain), Ruanuku (sea, fishing), were important on Easter 
Island as in other parts of Polynesia. Atua, gods, were distinguished 
from akuaku, lesser gods or spirits, who were often identified with a 
specific locality and offer a parallel to the Samoan tupua and aitu, 
except that akuaku represented the ancestral spirits which are uni­
versally important in East Polynesia.
their use and classification is negligible; it is here that the com­

historic times and were used only as burial cairns, we can not deal 
with the typically Polynesian character!sties of these marae-type 
structures. 
The use of the term ahu, the presence of a court, 
priests' houses, and refuse pits are all reflective of the widespread 
Polynesian pattern of religious ceremonial centers. Emphasis on the 
ahu as well as the presence of the prepared, paved court indicates a 
strong East Polynesian association. The absence of court or platform 
uprights, of structures on the court, and of any altars for offerings

suggests an early, divergent pattern of development from the basic

There is no known reliable terminology for parts of the ahu 
structure, such as for the refuse pit or priests' house. However, I 
did obtain from an informant, Jose Fati, the name tara for the end 
wall or corner of the ahu platform, aro for the front, tu'a for the 
back wall, tini'for the part around the base of the statue pedestal, 
eva for the part directly under the statue, and apapa for the pave­
ment in front of the statue, but their significance for aboriginal 
ahu is uncertain. The term tara (tarahu) was used in the Tuamotus 
and in the Society Islands for platform uprights. Fare tini atua is 
the god house in Tuamotuan marae and eva is the name for Mangaian 
dirges.
Small wooden statues were used widely in historic times to 
represent deceased ancestors but they had little reported association 
with ahu. The large stone statues were no longer carved in historic 
times. Woven or tapa covered images were also used in Hawaii as well 
as Easter Island.
Summary and Conclusions
The comparative material from Polynesian cultural systems for 
which there is sufficient data indicates two or three major kinds of 
religious specialists (Table 3). 
One of these, the inspirational 
priest, was known on Easter Island; however, no detailed information 
is available on his function. There were also ceremonial type priests, 
referred to as tuhunga, or maori. The status of two other religious 
practitioners, ivi-atua and koromaki, is more problematic. The

koromaki may have been a sorcerer along with a third specialist, 
tangata taka. However, ivi-atua seem to have been lineage ceremonial 
priests and were reportedly also inspired by gods. All of these types 
fall clearly within the Polynesian pattern. There were no named ser­
vants of priests or assistants for priests on Easter Island which is 
in accord with the less complicated nature of Easter Island religion 
when compared to the Marquesas or Tahiti. The presence of professional 
chanters, tangata rongo-rongo on Easter Island, rogo-rogo on Mangareva, 
and tuhuna o'ono in the Marquesas is significant, because these names 
do not appear elsewhere.
Almost nothing is known of Easter Island ahu ceremony.
Priests offered human sacrifices, particularly for Makemake, but no 
name was recorded for this rite. Ritual cannibalism was reported. 
Ceremonies designed to awaken ahu gods are not known. Some informa­
tion is available about death feasts; the paina seems to be the clear­
est example of this because it was directed by an ivi-atua. Other 
feasts to which the general term koro was applied, were, like the 
paina, usually in honor of parents.
First fruit ceremonies, very elaborate in Hawaii and Tahiti, 
were present on Easter Island, but only as offerings to the ariki 
henua. Nothing is known of the ordination of priests beyond the one 
ritual for the rongo-rongo chanters. There were houses of learning. 
Ceremony that accompanied burial in ahu is unknown.
The relationship between religious centers and social units is 
clear for all the cultural systems considered, that is, kin based 
groups of various sizes constructed, maintained, and used ahu or marae 
structures as the most important part of religious activity in which

pan-Polynesian gods as veil as more recently deified ancestors were 
of central interest. I conclude that Easter Island ahu or marae use 
represents a continuation of a basic Polynesian pattern into historic 
times, but one that was modified by isolation. It does reflect a 
widespread East Polynesian pattern in religious centers.

CHAPTER IV
THE CULTURAL CONTEXT OF EASTER ISLAND AHU
Easter Island's Relationship With 
Other Polynesian Islands
Easter Island ethnographic data discussed in Chapter III indi­
cates the island’s Polynesian status in historic times. The skeletal 
remains recovered from excavations and daced to the Expansion Phase 
(A.D. 1000-1680) and post-Expansion Phase have also been recognized 
as Polynesian. Linguistically, the historic Easter Islanders fall 
into the East Polynesian subgroup of languages of the larger 
Polynesian group. There has been little questioning of the Polynesian 
status of Easter Island inhabitants since the first contact by Euro­
peans in 1722. How far back in time can a Polynesian culture be 
projected in order to characterize the cultural context of ahu 
throughout the prehistoric record of Easter Island? The major propo­
nent of the view that the early occupants of Easter Island were non- 
Polynesian is Heyerdahl who has maintained in a number of publications 
that the first inhabitants were from South America (19^-1, 1952, 1958, 
1961, 1965? 1968). He regarded the Expansion Phase (Middle Period) 
as the first indication of Polynesian occupation. However, if Easter 
Island were settled by Polynesians at such a recent date one would 
expect that there would be even closer cultural, linguistic, and mor­
phological similarities to other East Polynesian populations than
L
326
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 344 ===

there actually were in historic times. The Easter Island language 
preserves at least one phonological feature of proto-Polynesian, a 
glottal, as in matu'a, tu’a , later lost in Hawaiian, Maori and other 
languages; this indicates that the Easter Island language became iso­
lated from East Polynesian at an early date, before these others 
(Emory 1972:6l). This isolation must have occurred before the 
Expansion Phase. The archaeological record points to cultural conti­
nuity from the time of initial settlement to historic times so that 
on this basis we may project the features of the Polynesian religious 
system back into the Settlement and Developmental phases of the 
island's occupation,
I presented what I consider to be accumulated evidence for 
stylistic changes in stone statuary from the early phases of occupa­
tion leading to the very standardized images of great size character­
istic of my Expansion Phase (A.D. 1000-1680). These standardized 
statues found on image ahu and at the Rano Raraku quarry are too con­
ventionalized to have appeared without stylistic antecedents. There 
are no known statues elsewhere in Polynesia or South America that 
could be the immediate stylistic precursors, thus their development 
must have taken place on the island. Heyerdahl agrees with this 
interpretation (1968:13^-135) hut he associates the early statuary 
with South American settlers rather than with Polynesians. As I 
interpret it, the current archaeological record does not indicate 
more than one migration to the island. While the possibility of more 
than one group of settlers is not precluded, I find no evidence to 
support such a conjecture. It seems apparent that the sweet potato 
was introduced into Polynesia from South America but probably not
L
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.

3281
directly to Easter Island. Yen maintains that Easter Island prob­
ably was not the gateway for the sweet potato into Polynesia, "for it 
obliges a contact toward nuclear Polynesia for which there is no cul­

the further transmissions" (l971a:12). He also suggests a date of 
around the 2nd or 3rd centuries A.D. for the addition of this plant 
to the basic Melanesian crop complex of Polynesia (l971b:3^0). This 
hypothesis of continual Polynesian occupation for Easter Island is 
presented with recognition of the great limitations of the record, 
particularly for the earlier phases of settlement.
early religious centers were within a Polynesian cultural context, 
ethnographic evidence suggests that we can project certain features 
of social organization for these prehistoric Polynesians. Several 
levels of nonunilinear descent groups in Malayo-Polynesian social 
organization are related to land ownership and residence. Because 
they are associated with specific sections of land, their connection 
with ahu marae structures on the same land is also indicated. Named 
levels of nonunilineal descent groups can be demonstrated for Easter 
Island from the descriptions of that culture at the time of contact. 
The existence of these types of kin units in other Malayo-Polynesian 
societies has been described in the ethnographic record and was postu­
lated as characteristic of the original Malayo-Polynesian form of 
social organization (Murdock 19^8, 19^9; Goodenough 1955)• It seems 
clear then, that such a form of social organization, including kin

Goodenough (1955:82) concluded that two types of kin groups associated

organization in the Gilberts included five types of kin groupings: 
l) a true bilateral kindred (utuu); 2) an unrestricted descent group 
including all the persons descended from a common ancestor, regardless 
whether through men or women (ooi); 3) a- household, formerly an

Goodenough (1955) made a distinction between personal (ego- 
based) kin groups or kindreds and ancestor-based descent groups. He 
recognized only the latter as descent groups and used nonunilinear, 
as Davenport did later (1969), "to refer to the residual category of 
descent (ancestor-based gr.ups) whose principles of descent inclusion 
(and exclusion) are other than unilinear" (Goodenough 1961:1343). The 
Gilbertese utuu, then, could not be a descent group associated with 
land. Goodenough objected to Murdock's use of "cognatic" to refer to 
nonunilinear kin groups and also to his use of "ramage" to refer to 
the nonunilinear equi'valent of the unilinear sib (1961:1344).
Goodenough relied on Murdock's arguments for the absence of unilinear 
kin groups in the proto society, stressing that in this situation 
landholding descent groups must have been nonunilinear. 
Since he

this left a descent group like the ooi described above.

of land tenure have developed from a common base of bilat­

a problem only where, as among the Ifugao, permanent-field

studies of Hew Guinea societies.
