'The Changing Cultural Context of Easter Island Ahu 
The following discussion seeks to outline the cultural con­
test of Easter Island religious structures in terms of the cultural 
phases defined archaeologically in Chapter II.
Settlement and Developmental Phase (A.D. 400-1000)
That the cultural context of Settlement Phase ahu religious 
centers would have included recognition of a series of important East 
Polynesian gods, including Tangaroa, Kongo, Tane, Hiro, Ruanuku, and 
perhaps Tu. is indicated by recorded genealogies, legends, and by the 
pan-East Polynesian distribution of these gods in historic times. In 
addition, as Metraux recognized for protohistoric times, Easter 
Islanders worshipped and made offerings to deceased ariki and priests 
who were deified at ahu. I have referred to the latter as tutelary 
deities and to the Pan-East Polynesian gods as mythical and depart­
mental gods; some of these may have been appealed to at ahu. The con­
cept of a supreme god would not have been characteristic of the reli­
gious complex. Although small shrines for family prayer and offerings 
to immediate ancestors would be expected, none have been archaeologi­
cally defined.
In Chapter II5I described in some detail the architecture of 
religious structures in the two earliest phases. It is only possible 
to delineate the use of particular parts of the unit in general
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 351 ===

r
terms, because the structure is uncomplicated. No uprights are known 
which marked the positions of priests or of other participants in 
ritual. There is some evidence of statues on ahu during the Settle­
ment and Developmental Phases, but whether they stood on the altar or 
on the court is not known. Heyerdahl maintained that they stood in a 
"sunken" court, because their round bases would not permit them to 
stand erect on top of a stone platform (1968:135). Observations of 
several statues, which were supposed to be early, like the whole one 
at site 8-13^, indicate that at least some had flat bases and could 
have stood on a stone platform. Regional quarry areas are indicated 
for. the early phases; apparently the red scoria statues of the Tahai 
region came from the quarry Heyerdahl described on Otuu hill and from 
nearby Punapau; early statues in other areas are of Rano Raraku tuff 
and, in some cases, of basalt from unknown quarries.
Tombs, tapu refuse pits, cists, upright stones or other 
markers of positions in ceremonies, and burials are unknown. Only a 
few portable artifacts have been recovered which can be dated to these 
two phases. There are strong indications of rituals associated with a 
sun cult in the solar orientation of early ahu. Goldman even referred 
to a "generalized sun cult" as characteristic of the Early Period 
(1970:107), when the ahu had "a religious function unrelated to the 
use of statues" (1970:101).
Priests in the Settlement Phase were primarily heads of kin 
groups, lineage (ivi), ramage (mata), or tribe, who acted as religious 
specialists for the social unit. The religious and political leader 
for the entire population of the island was the king (ariki), the 
head of the Honga lineage of the Miru mata, a nonunilinear kin group,
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 352 ===

r 
33TI
consisting of several hundred people. This was a hereditary position.
It is unlikely that there were special ahu priests of the taura type 
(inspirational priests), even though specialists (spell casters) like 
the koromaki were part of the religious complex. Englert defined 
"taura" in his 1938 dictionary not as a priest, its common Polynesian 
meaning, hut only as "cordel, maroma, soga, lazo" (1938:123). Metraux 
described attendants of the king, "tahura," who may have been priests 
assisting him in ahu ceremonies. Although Me'traux says that tahura 
means "expert," the term maori, or "sage," contained more of the mean­
ing of tahunga, an expert or a master craftsman of the ceremonial 
priest type, who were probably present and directed ahu ceremonies.
Expansion Phase (A.D. 1000-1680)
Basic Polynesian concepts of the major gods must have con­
tinued during this time, but their importance in ritual, as in most of 
Polynesia, is unknown. It seems probable that one, Tane (Tiki), was 
replaced by a new god named Makemake. Goldman refers to Makemake as 
a "patron god of warriors" (1970:101).
More recently deified ancestors, ariki or priests, had become 
increasingly important by this time and apparently were represented by 
the large stone images on image ahu characteristic of this phase. The 
Tahai excavations indicate the antiquity of stylized statues on ahu. 
Their title of ariki comes from references by Cook's men to several 
named statues. The prerogatives of rank were no longer as closely 
associated with status lineages, like the Honga and Miru, as they were 
during the earlier phases, but increasingly with military power.
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 353 ===

r
3361
The increased size of ahu constructed during this time, such 
as Ahu Hekii, Ahu Tongariki, Ahu Mahatua, reflect both the growing 
population and the power of leaders to make people work on what was 
basically an altar and offering to the deceased father of a new ariki 
and a symbol of his temporal power. Goldman suggested that the 
ability of kin groups to build such large ahu was based on the use 
of slaves, kio, defeated in battle. This being the case, it would 
appear that the priests also became increasingly involved in inter­
preting the outcome of forthcoming battles and preparing leaders for 
battle.
I described certain structural features of the ahu of the 
Expansion Phase that do tell us something about the ceremonies con­
ducted at ahu. The nature of the ahu as a base for images is apparent. 
The continued presence of a large sized court to accommodate an assem­
bled group of participants is evident; this is particularly clear at 
ahu like Ahu Hekii where the elevated court is 80 meters wide by 50 
meters deep. The statues are highly standardized. 
Some statues were 
painted red. A craft guild was responsible for carving them.
Small pits for depositing cremated bones represent a pre­
viously unrecognized feature of ahu in this phase. The five cremation 
pits that have been dated (A.D. 1^4-90, 1380, 1220, 1210 and 1210) indi­
cate a long use of this method of disposing of sacrifices (?). Palmer 
reported this same practice for historic times (l868, l870a). The 
presence of a skull in the cist on the Ko te Riku plaza also indicates 
an ancestor cult which placed emphasis on skull preservation. 
Offer­
ings (?) that were cremated along with corpses appear to have included 
small wooden images with obsidian disk eyes and fishhooks. The
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 354 ===

r
337I
cremations were probably part of offerings to the ahu gods rather 
than a way of deifying or of burial.
nucleated settlements were fairly clearly delineated in this 
phase with the semi-circular spread of boat-shaped houses behind the 
ahu court. The size and number of these villages indicate that they 
were used by a large population, larger than we might expect if they 
were occupied only by the priests who conducted ahu ceremony. The 
significance of the statues erected on the rectangular stone platforms 
of Expansion Phase ahu is that of commemorating deceased ariki for 
whom the entire ahu was a votive offering. The presence of some 
female statues (Barthel, 1958b) suggests that priestesses as well as 
priests may have been deified and represented by stone images. The 
ahu also served as the site for the investiture of a new ariki, and 
probably, as in other Polynesian marae, as a site for ritual marking 
other important events in the life of the ariki. Other ceremonies 
conducted on the ahu during this time, based on the projection from 
the ethnographic record, would probably include; drying corpses on 
the ahu, agricultural rites associated with makahiki (first fruits) 
type ritual, rongo-rongo bards reciting ritual and chants, and the 
paina ceremony as described in its operation in historic times.
The evidence for drying corpses on ahu comes from a time 
when they were no longer characterized by large statues commemorating 
ancestors, but when they served as burial mounds, the semi-pyramidal 
ahu. Routledge obtained the most information about this method of 
corpse preparation, By that time it was common to bury the dessicated 
individual directlv in the ahu. Earlie the remains must have been 
deposited elsewhere. There is evidence for cremation in this time
L  
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 355 ===

period; however, it has been suggested that cremated remains dating 
to this period -were those of sacrifices rather than those of venerated 
ancestors.
The rongo-rongo chanters met at Anakena on a yearly basis to 
perform. It is not clear if they actually met on the court of an ahu, 
and this is unlikely for the time Routledge describes, during the 
reign of Ariki Nga'ara. We may postulate such activity for ahu during 
the Expansion Phase if the rongo-rongo writing system in fact pre­
ceded European contact. The suspected content of tablets, genealogies 
and creation chants, are the topics chanted by specialists in other 
Polynesian marae ceremonies.
The size and nature of social units during this time can be 
ascertained by reviewing the ethnographic evidence for the antiquity 
of nonunilinear kin groups in Polynesia whose appearance we have pos­
tulated for the earlier phases of Easter Island culture. Ahu would 
have maintained their importance for nonunilinear kin groups which 
were apparently increasing in size and in the ability to gather large 
labor forces for the construction of ahu like Tongariki, Hekii, and 
Mahatua in the eastern end of the island. Goldman (1970) maintained 
that the ability to construct such large structures in the Expansion 
Phase (the beginnings of his "Open Society") was possible through the 
use of slave labor, gained through military supremacy rather than the 
religious or sacred superiority of ariki who constructed ahu in the 
earlier phases, his "Traditional Society."
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 356 ===

r
339I
Decadent Phase (A.D. 1680-1722)
No image ahu were constructed during this time, and no statues 
were carved at the Rano Raraku quarry for image ahu. The latest 
actual radiocarbon date for construction of an image ahu is A.D. 1^60 
from Ahu Akivi. Goldman believes that at this time it was impossible 
for the culture to continue to operate as a Traditional Society and 
that the military supremacy, accompanied by reinterpretation of super­
natural sanctions, led to island wide control by military leaders of 
lower status than the Miru Ariki. More and more statues were tipped 
over and defaced, some were buried in the fill of the ahu ramp, as at 
Vinapu and Vai Uri. The process of tipping statues over, however, 
must have begun considerably before this time, possibly as early as 
the middle of the Expansion Phase when the Tahai and Ko te Riku 
statues were tipped over. There were some attempts, presumably during 
this phase and perhaps later, to re-erect statues that had been tipped 
over. The few stones under the prone Tahai image were probably placed 
thexe at the beginning of attempts to re-erect that statue. It seems 
clear that a number of slab-lined tombs were constructed in the demol­
ished ahu during this phase, a practice which probably began in the 
Expansion Phase.
Neither the major East Polynesian gods, who at one time were 
of considerable importance, nor the old ancestral gods, whose place 
of worship was the ahu itself, would have played a major role in the 
religious substantiation or verification of the supremacy of the bird 
men during this phase. Makemake who took over the features associated 
earlier with Tane, became the most important single god in conjunction
L 
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 357 ===

r
3^01
with the temporal lords. The ahu structures themselves were remodeled 
in these times to form the semi-pyramidal ahu which were merely a 
standardized way of covering up the ruins of the image ahu. Some ahu 
were left exposed to a greater extent than others, probably reflecting 
a lesser degree of use for burial. As previously mentioned, corpses 
were dried on biers placed on long pavements in front of some of the 
semi-pyramidal ahu. The use of constructed tombs probably spread to 
include the construction of other non-ahu tombs like those excavated 
by Mulloy (I96la:l47, Fig. 36), the appearance of avanga, and probably 
many of the structures called "hare moa" today. The obvious use of 
the destroyed ahu was for burial, both in constructed tombs and simple 
bundle burials. The tremendous proliferation of spear points during 
this period indicates the severity of inter-mata warfare, often asso­
ciated with attempts to tip over statues of other ramages. The occu­
pation of most caves with constructed entryways falls into the phase 
and can be directly associated with the great increase of raiding and 
feuding. These facets of the culture are attested to in recorded oral 
traditions. The long boat-shaped houses continued to be in nucleated 
settlements but elsewhere the population must have been somewhat more 
dispersed in their search for defensible sleeping places.
(tahura) and the tahunga or maori, but no major ahu ceremonies could 
have been conducted. The paina ceremonies were still performed, but 
there is little indication of the role of a priest in these rituals. 
Although there is no record of rongo-rongo chanters we may assume that 
they continued to perform if they were present in the earlier
Two types of priests continued to function, the taura type
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 358 ===

r 
3i1.1i
Expansion Phase. Priests appear to have played an important role in 
the bird man ceremonies, including both the seers or taura type and 
the conductors of ritual.
Protohistoric Phase (A.D. 1722-1868)
Easter Island gods of this phase were described by Routledge, 
Metraux and the early missionaries and were discussed in the ethno­
graphic coverage in Chapter III; Makemake was the most important. All 
the other spirits were of the akuaku type, who although restricted to 
particular geographical regions, did not seem to have any particular 
association with ahu. All the ahu were in a state of disrepair during 
this time, although a few statues were still standing until at least 
the 181)0's (Admiral du Petit-Thouars 181)1, 2:222-23^-). The primary 
use of ahu continued to be for burial. The last great ariki of the 
traditional Miru line, Nga'ara, died shortly before the Peruvian slave 
raids in the l860's, but by that time the ahu no longer functioned 
in any important way other than as a burial place. Ariki Kga'ara 
directed the chanting of rongo-rongo every year at Anakena. The 
rongo-rongo was apparently in full development at that late date and 
supports Emory's contention that it was a post-contact innovation, 
and one not closely related to the ahu, which were actually defunct 
from the time of the inception of the writing. The paina ceremonies 
continued to be performed on ahu and these are the only known cere­
monies to be conducted there. Most ahu during this time were sur­
mounted with piles of whitened stones, rahui. These tapu markers 
were land boundary markers.
L
J
Reproduced with permission of the copyright owner. Further reproduction prohibited without permission.


=== PAGE 359 ===

r
3»51
The last reported case of cannibalism was in 1864, but this 
again was not associated with ahu ceremonies, where previously sacri­
fices were eaten on the ahu.
The ahu continues to be recognized as an important ancestral 
burial place and was used as such in historic times up through the 
early 1900's. 
Subsurface graves gradually replaced the earlier stone 
slab tombs as a method of burial.
Summary
Because of the great antiquity of marae (ahu) on Easter Island
and continuities in architecture over a period of 1500 years, under­
standing the pattern of development in this small, isolated island 
provides considerable insight into the process of marae elaboration 
throughout East Polynesia. Unanswered questions do arise, because the 
Easter Island structures are the earliest known in all of Polynesia. 
This study has established a protohistoric baseline in regard to ahu 
use and an outline of the changing context of these religious struc­
tures throughout the prehistoric sequence. These hypotheses now need 
to be tested using further archaeological data.
Historic Phase (A.D. 1868-

=== PAGE 371 ===

Drills:
A large number of small, unifacially flaked drill points of obsidian 
were found in contexts ranging from the fill of Tahai I to surface
provenience. There is then an indication of long duration of this
tool type. A total of 38 were found.
Scrapers:
A number of small unifacially flaked obsidian tools were apparently 
used for scraping purposes; all are irregular and no standardization 
is evident, as with the mata'a and the use-retouched flakes, and 
fortuitous flakes were sufficient for the scraping uses they were 
put to.
Hammerstones:
A few hammerstones were recovered, mostly basalt beach stones, but 
including at least one core of obsidian, (cat. numbers: 1^9 (?),
176, 302, 303, 305, 306, 307).
Miscellaneous Stone:
One stone disk of the sort used in Polynesian bowling games was 
recovered (cat. no. 300) (Fig. 58b).
One pecked stone pillow was recovered (cat. no, 579) (Fig. 58a).
A few fragments of ground stone bowls were found in the archaeological 
survey.
Wine small flat obsidian disks were recovered from the two major cre­
mation pits. These are thin unifacially flaked pieces that are the 
same general size and shape as the eye pieces of historic wooden 
images and the same use is attributed to these prehistoric specimens 
(Fig. 58c).
Flakes:
A large number of obsidian flakes showing use retouch were recovered 
in the excavations. They show great variation in form and are for­
tuitous flakes used as cutting tools and then discarded. Only a few 
flakes of basalt were found; most had one ground facet which indicates 
that they are flakes from ground adzes.

Bone:
Fishhooks:
Only one hone fishhook -was recovered in the entire excavation. This 
point leg of a two piece hook illustrated in Fig. 58d (cat. no. 315) 
was found in the cremated bone deposit on the plaza of Ko te Riku. It 
was burned and appears to be associated with the cremations and the 
C lk  date of A.D. 1210 -  90.
Two small pieces of cut bone were recovered; one from Trench 5, Mound 1 
of Ko te Riku (cat. no. 316) which is illustrated in Fig. 59m, and a 
second from Ahu Tahai found under the nose of the prone statue (cat. 
no. 583). The second is probably turtle bone.
Shell:
No shell artifacts were recovered.
Wood:
The only wooden artifacts were the boards covering the grave in the 
Ko te Riku plaza.
Historic:
Historic artifacts included several pieces of wire, nails (particularly 
from the burial in the plaza of Ko te Riku), one large brass nail 
(cat. no. 590)5 and the metal chisel from Tomb IS in Ahu Ko te Riku 
(cat. no. 330). One of the nails was found in the 20-40 centimeter 
level of Trench 3 of Ahu Tahai, -within the fill of Tahai II. It was 
interpreted as an intrusion, because the Cl4 dating indicated a much 
earlier time of construction of the ahu.
Two pieces of glass were recovered; of these, one small fragment of 
green glass from a depth of 23 centimeters in the fill of Mound 1 may 
have chronological significance.
Petroglyphs:
Incised designs on rucks were found in Trench 5 (Mound l) of Ko te 
Riku (one rock in the fill); in Trench 3 of Ahu Tahai (one stone used 
as a paving stone), in addition to several small designs on surface
